The author of this book, when he had completed for you, his sons, this book which is a part of the science of medicine in its entirety and reached the utmost goal therein in its clarity and explanation, saw that he should complete it for you with this treatise which is the part of work by hand.
For manual work is in our country and in our time utterly nonexistent, so much so that the study of its science was almost discontinued. And what remained of it were but slight sketches in the books of the ancients, which hands had corrupted and thrown into error and confusion until its meanings were obscured and its benefit removed.
So I decided to revive it and to compose this treatise on it according to the method of explanation, clarification, and abbreviation, and to give illustrations of iron instruments and all other surgical tools, for this is an increase in elucidation and a fulfillment of what is needed.
The reason why there are no skilled craftsmen in our time is that the practice of medicine is lengthy, and its possessor should first become proficient in anatomy, as Galen described it. So that he may understand the benefits of the organs, their forms, temperaments, connections, and separations, and knowledge of the bones, nerves, muscles, their number, origins, and insertions, as well as the arteries, veins, and their places of origin.
Therefore Hippocrates said that physicians in name are many, but in deed few, especially in manual work, and we have mentioned a part of this in the introduction to this book.
For he who is not knowledgeable about what we have mentioned concerning anatomy is bound to fall into error that may kill people, as I have witnessed many times in those who imagined themselves to be experts in this science and claimed it without knowledge or experience.
Indeed, I once saw a ignorant physician who incised a wen on the neck of a woman, and he severed some of the arteries in her neck so that she bled to death at his feet.
I saw another physician who had advanced in removing a stone from a man who had been stabbed in the tooth, and the stone was large; so it crumbled, and he removed it with a piece of bladder calculus. The man died in about three days, and I had been called to remove it; I saw from the size of the stone and the condition of the patient that this was beyond my capacity.
I saw another physician who was making a living from medicine among some of the leaders of our country.
A black boy happened to have a broken leg near the heel with a wound, and the physician, in his ignorance, hastily bound the fracture onto the wound with bandages and splints tightly secured and did not leave the wound any room to breathe.
Then he released him to his amusements and left him for days, ordering him not to untie the bandages until his leg and foot swelled up and he was on the verge of death.
I was then called to him and quickly untied the bandages, bringing relief and freeing him from his pain; however, the corruption had become deeply established in the limb and I was unable to restore it.
The corruption continued to spread throughout the limb until he died.
I saw another physician cauterize a cancerous tumor, and after a few days it ulcerated, greatly afflicting its possessor; for when cancer is purely from black humors, it should not be treated with iron at all.
Unless it is in a limb that can tolerate the complete removal.
Therefore, my son, you should know that manual work is divided into two parts: work accompanied by safety and work that usually results in harm.
I have warned throughout this book against work that involves risk and danger, and you should beware of it and refuse to undertake it.
So that the ignorant may not find a way to speak against and criticize you, be cautious and careful in your actions and treat yourselves with gentleness and deliberation.
Use the best path leading to safety and a happy outcome, and avoid dangerous and difficult diseases.
Purify yourselves from what you fear may bring doubt to your religion and worldly affairs, for it is the most enduring legacy for your reputation and the highest rank in this world and the Hereafter for your worth.
For Galen said in some of his precepts, "Do not treat a bad disease and become bad physicians."
This treatise has been divided into three chapters.
The first chapter.
On cautery with fire and cautery with caustic drugs, arranged from beginning to end, with illustrations of instruments and iron for cautery, and everything needed for manual work.
The second chapter.
On incision, dissection, cupping and bloodletting, surgery, and the extraction of arrows and the like, all arranged and illustrated with instruments.
The third chapter.
On splinting, dislocations, and the treatment of fractures and the like, arranged from beginning to end and illustrated with instruments.
The first chapter.
On cautery.
Before we mention its use, it is necessary to mention its benefits, harms, and in what temperaments it is used.
I say that discourse on the benefits and harms of cautery is long, a precise science, and a subtle secret, and many sages have spoken about it.
They differed in their opinions, and I have summarized a little of what they said for fear of being verbose.
I say that cautery is generally beneficial for every bad temperament, whether with or without a material cause; there are two temperaments that it benefits: the hot temperament without a material cause and the dry temperament without a material cause.
As for the dry temperament with a substance, they differed in it, some saying that cautery is beneficial and others the opposite, that cautery is not suitable in a disease that is from heat and dryness because the nature of fire is heat and dryness.
It is impossible for someone to be cured of a hot, dry disease by a hot, dry remedy, and those who say the opposite say that cautery with fire may benefit from a hot, dry disease that occurs in people's bodies. Because when you add the body of a human and its moisture to the temperament of fire, you make the human body cold.
And I say the same because experience has revealed this to me many times, but it should not be imagined by anyone who has not trained and practiced extensively in the art of cautery. And he must have considered the differences in people's temperaments, the states of illnesses themselves, their causes, symptoms, and duration.
As for all other temperaments, there is no fear from them, especially cold and wet diseases; all physicians have agreed on them and did not differ about the benefit of cautery in them.
And know, my sons, that the secret of treatment by cautery with fire and its advantage over cautery with burning medicine is because fire is a single element whose action does not extend beyond the member being cauterized and does not harm another connected member except for a slight injury.
Cautery with burning medicine may extend its action beyond the members and may cause a disease in the member that is difficult to treat and may even kill, but fire, due to its nobility and generosity of essence, does not do this unless it is excessive.
This has become clear to us through experience from long service and attention to the craft and standing on the facts of the matter.
That is why I dispensed with lengthy speech, and if it were not inappropriate for this book of mine, I would have presented to you in fire a mysterious secret and how it acts on bodies and negates diseases with philosophical, demonstrative speech that sharpens your understandings.
Know ye, sons, that they have differed concerning the time which is suitable for cauterization and they have made the best time to be spring.
And I say that cauterization may be suitable in every time because the injury resulting from time is consumed in the benefit which is elicited by cauterization. Especially if the cauterization is from necessary, strong pains which cannot bear delay because of what is feared from a destruction that is greater than the slight injury from time.
And do not consider, O sons, what the common people and ignorant physicians imagine, that cauterization which heals from a disease will never have a return, and you make it obligatory.
For the matter is not as they suppose, because cauterization is like medicine which changes the temperament and dries up the humidities which are the cause of the pains occurring.
Except that cauterization is preferred over medicine because of the speed of its success, the strength of its action, and the severity of its power.
And it is possible that the disease may return at some time depending on the temperament of the patient, the strength of his illness, and what prepares itself in his body from gathering excesses and negligence in acquiring them from foods and so forth from those reasons.
Except if the disease for which cauterization is used is a delicate disease and in a member with few excesses and humidities. Such as cauterizing a tooth for pain, and the like, it is possible that the pain may not return in it and this will be at least.
And as for the saying of the common people also that cauterization is the last resort, it is a true statement, but not to what they intend, because they believe that no treatment benefits by medicine or anything else after cauterization has taken place and the matter is otherwise.
Indeed, the meaning of "cautery is the last resort of medicine" is that whenever we have used various treatments for a disease and those drugs have not been successful, and then we use cautery as a last resort and it succeeds, therefore, it is said that cautery is the last resort of medicine. Not according to the meaning which the common people and many ignorant physicians have adopted.
The ancients have mentioned that cautery with gold is better than cautery with iron, saying this because of the moderation of gold and its noble essence, and they said that it does not cause suppuration at the site of the cautery. This is not true at all.
For I have tried it and found that it only does this in some bodies and not others, and cautery with it is better and more excellent than iron as they said, except that if you heat the iron in the fire from gold, you will not be able to discern when it has reached the degree of heat that you desire for the redness of gold.
And because it quickly cools, and if you increase its heat in the fire, it melts and pours out, causing the craftsman to fall into trouble from this, therefore cautery with iron has become faster and closer to being correct for us to practice, God willing.
And I have arranged this chapter on cautery into sections, organized from the head to the foot, to make it easy for the student to find what he wants, God willing.
Chapter One.
Cautery of the Head: A Single Cautery.
This cautery is beneficial for the predominance of moisture and cold on the brain, which are the cause of headaches and frequent colds from the head to the eyes and ears, excessive sleepiness, toothache, and sore throat. In general, for every disease that arises from cold, such as paralysis, epilepsy, stroke, and similar diseases.
The picture of this disease is that you first order the patient to vomit with a purgative medicine purified for the head for three or four nights, as required by the strength of the patient, his age and his habit.
Then you order him to shave his head with a razor, then you seat him between your hands, squatting, having placed his hands on his chest.
Then you place the base of your palm on the base of his nose between his eyes, and where your middle finger ends, mark that spot with ink.
Then heat the olive iron whose picture is this.
Expose from the bone the head of a needle or the size of a caraway seed, then raise your hand, or else return your hand with the same iron or another if it has cooled. Until you see from the bone what I have mentioned to you, then take some salt, dissolve it in water and soak a cotton ball in it, place it on the spot and leave it for three days.
Then apply a cotton ball soaked in butter to it and leave it on until the burning sensation goes away, then treat it with the fourfold ointment until it is healed, God willing.
And they said that the wound, the longer it remains open and discharges pus, the better and more beneficial it is.
And some mentioned that the skin should be burned to the bone and the iron held until some of the thickness of the bone is burned, then the burned part of the bone is removed afterwards and treated.
And others said that one should overdo the burning so that it affects the bone strongly until it falls from the bone like a small chirp or a small flea, and they claimed that steam from the head escapes from that place and leaves the wound open for a long time.
Then it is treated until it heals, and I do not see these two types of cauterization at all except in some people and by way of risk, and leaving it with me is better and safe, if there is any safety. For the head weakens when its natural connection is broken, as we have seen in all other organs.
Especially when the patient's head is weak in nature, the first type of cautery is safest and best for me, and with it I work, so you are safe, God willing.
Chapter Two.
Also in head cautery.
If, in a head wound, there occurs chronic pain and this lasts for the patient and poultices, ointments, cauteries and dressings are used, especially if he has been cauterized with the single cautery that we have described and nothing has benefited him from it then look. If the patient's head is strong in nature and not weak, and he feels intense cold, cauterize another cautery slightly above it. Then cauterize each horn of his head with a cautery until the thickness of the skin is gone and the bone is exposed to the degree we have described. And cauterize a cautery on the back of his head in the place known as the ax, and lighten your hand in this, and do not expose the bone because the patient will feel severe pain from it unlike the pain of all other head cauteries and I will mention this cautery in its place.
The iron with which the horns and back of the head are cauterized should be finer than the iron with which the middle of the head is cauterized, and this is its image.
Chapter Three.
In cautery for non-chronic migraine.
If there occurs in a head wound pain with headache and the pain extends to the eye, let the patient be purged with purgatives for the head and use all the treatment that I mentioned in the divisions of diseases, but if this does not succeed then cauterize it on two sides. Either cauterize with a harsh burning medicine or with iron.
As for cautery with burning medicine, it is to take a single garlic clove, peel it and cut off its ends on both sides, then incise the painful spot from the temple with a wide lancet until there is a subcutaneous place large enough for the clove.
Insert it under the skin until it disappears, then tighten it firmly with a bandage and leave it for fifteen hours. Then untie it and remove the garlic, leaving the wound for two or three days, then dress it.
Cover it with cotton soaked in ghee until the area suppurates, then treat it with ointment until it heals, if God wills. If you wish, you may do this with some caustic drugs which I have mentioned in the eighteenth chapter on caustic drugs.
As for cauterizing it with iron, it is done as follows: heat the iron whose shape is as follows:
It is called a nail iron because its head is shaped like a nail, with some indentations and a small protrusion in the middle.
Then place it on the painful spot and hold your hand while turning the iron slightly. The amount you burn should be about half the thickness of the skin. Lift your hand so as not to burn the artery below, which would cause bleeding. Then soak cotton in salt water and place it on the area, leaving it for three days. Then dress the cotton with ghee, then treat it with ointment until it heals, if God wills.
If you wish, you may cauterize this sciatica with the sharp point of the iron, taking care not to cut an artery in this acute case, especially.
Chapter Four.
On cauterizing chronic sciatica.
If you have treated sciatica with the remedies previously mentioned and those in the classification of diseases, and treatment has not been successful, and you see a cause which cannot be addressed by the first cauterization with medicine or fire. Then heat the pointed iron until it turns white, after marking a line on the painful spot about half a finger long. Leave your hand only once while tightening it so that it severs the artery and reaches near the bone.
But you must be careful of the contact of the jaw that moves when chewing, lest you burn the muscle or the nerve that moves it and cause paralysis.
And be wary of the bleeding from the artery which you have cut, for there is danger in cutting it. Especially for one who does not know what he is doing and is not an experienced practitioner, it is best to leave the work undone.
The treatment of bleeding from the artery will be mentioned in its proper place in this book, God willing. If you see a condition that cannot be treated by these means.
And you saw the body of the patient bearing a wound in the middle of the head, as we have described, and treated the wound until it healed, God willing.
And if you wish, use the cautery that we mentioned in the chapter on ligating the artery with an iron having two knives; it is a better and more effective cautery than this.
Chapter Five.
On Cauterizing Earaches.
If pain occurs in the ear due to cold and is treated with laxatives and all other treatments mentioned in the division, but the pain does not go away, then heat the iron called the point, which has this shape.
Then apply it after heating it all around the ear as it curves, or all around them both if the pain is in them. And keep the cautery away from the base of the ear a little after learning the location with ink, making the cauterization about ten points in each ear or thereabouts, then treat the spots until they heal, God willing.
Chapter Six.
On Cauterizing Sores.
The type of sore that is treated with cautery is the one that occurs from phlegm, as mentioned in the divisions of diseases.
He avoids the kind that causes hollows and nerve tension.
When you have treated this type of weakness with poultices and fomentations and gargles, and your treatment has not been successful, the patient should be cauterized with three cauteries. One at the base of the ear, the second a little below his cheekbone, and the third at the junction of the lips.
Make your cauteries opposite the affected side because relaxation only occurs on the side that appears sound.
And the cautery should be made opposite the upper part of the ear, under the horn of the head a little, and another on the cheekbone, its length being the length of the thumb.
Lower the cautery with your hand until it burns half the thickness of the skin, and this is the image of the cautery iron, which is a type of surgical knife whose picture has been shown, but it is slightly finer than it, as you see. And the knife should have a little extra thickness, then treat the area as previously mentioned until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Seven.
On cauterizing chronic stroke.
If the stroke has persisted and you have treated it as we have mentioned, and your treatment has not been successful, and the patient does not have a fever, cauterize him with four cauteries. One cautery on each horn of his head, and one in the middle of the head as we mentioned, and a cautery on the back of the head as previously stated.
The description of the cauteries is as previously mentioned, and it may also be cauterized on the mouth of the stomach, which will be more effective, then treat as previously mentioned.
Chapter Eight.
On cauterizing forgetfulness.
The one that is from phlegm should first be given to the patient large poultices and herbs that purify the brain.
Then his whole head is shaved and a mustard plaster, as mentioned in the article on plasters, is applied to the back of his head; it is held there repeatedly, for it is a type of cautery.
Do this on the rank itself, which was mentioned there. If he is cured by that, otherwise, make three cauteries in the back of his head, lined up from the top of the head to the nape of the neck.
Make a thickness of a finger between each cautery, then treat the cauteries as mentioned before. If you wish to increase [the treatment] and the patient can tolerate it, make a middle cautery.
If you wish to increase [the treatment], then cauterize the temples and treat them until he is cured. The cautery iron should be olive-wood, as mentioned before.
Chapter Nine.
Cauterization for paralysis and relaxation of the whole body.
You should proceed with the purification of the head with eyepieces and what we have mentioned, then shave the patient's head, and cauterize him with a cautery in the middle of the head, a cautery on each temple, and a cautery on the back of his head.
And three on the vertebrae of the neck; if, in a case of relaxation of the body, you need more than that and the patient can tolerate it and the illness is strong and chronic, cauterize him with four cauteries on the vertebrae of his back. Cauterize deeply until it burns most of the skin, then raise your hand and treat it as previously mentioned until he is cured, God willing. The cautery iron should be olive-wood.
Chapter Ten.
Cauterization for epilepsy.
Only the epileptic whose epilepsy is from phlegm should be cauterized. First, his brain should be purified with large eyepieces and all the treatment mentioned in the division, if the patient is adult and can tolerate taking medicine.
If it is a child who cannot tolerate medicine, let gargles and purified brain washes be used before that by many days, with the improvement of his diet.
Then shave his head, then cauterize one cautery in the middle of the head as previously described in the description and another cautery at the back of it. And on each temple of his head a cautery, if the patient is strong and can tolerate it, cauterize the cauteries mentioned in the case of paralysis and relaxation of the body on the vertebrae of the neck and the vertebrae of the back, and let the iron be olive-like as previously described.
If the ailing one is a child, make the iron delicate in this manner.
Chapter Eleven.
On Cautery for Melancholia.
If the cause of melancholia is foul humors and thick phlegm, cauterize the cauteries we mentioned in the case of paralysis.
If the cause of melancholia is a predominance tending toward blackness and the patient's body is moist and foul, purify his brain as previously described in the division.
Then shave the patient's head, then make a tight cake of linen like a circle, then lower it into the middle of his head while the patient is sitting square and holding on from each side, then take one pound of old sheep tallow.
Then heat it on the fire to a moderate warmth that the finger can tolerate if inserted, then pour it into the middle of his head within the circle and leave it until it cools; do this for the patient once a week with all his other good treatment until he recovers, God willing.
And if you wish, cauterize it with many small dots without holding your hand on the iron, but let it be a branding; for this type of cautery moistens the brain moderately.
Then place upon it cotton soaked in butter or chicken fat.
The Twelfth Chapter.
In cases of watery discharge from the eye.
If the beginning of the watery discharge from the eye becomes apparent with the signs mentioned in the division, hurry and make the patient wash his head and protect it from all moisture and sweat him in the bath on an empty stomach for several days.
Then shave his head and place a cupping glass in the middle of the head, then place two more cupping glasses on the temples if the discharge has just begun in both eyes or on one side if it is in one eye.
Then cut with a hot iron all the veins and arteries under the skin, let the cauteries be long in the direction of the temples, and preserve from bleeding.
If you see anything of it, cut it off immediately with any treatment that is possible for you, and I will come to the wisdom of cauterizing the arteries and cutting them while preserving from bleeding.
And two large cupping glasses may be applied to the nape of the neck under the collarbones.
The Thirteenth Chapter.
In cases of chronic tearing.
If the tearing of the eye is chronic and constant and was previously from the veins and arteries in the outer part of the head outside the skull and you are sure that it is from a cold, thick phlegmatic humor, then apply the cupping mentioned exactly as described for the beginning of watery discharge. One cupping glass in the middle of the head, two on the temples and two in the nape of the neck under the collarbones, and if you need to increase it, apply a cupping glass on each side of the tail of the eye at the end of the eyebrow with a small hot iron.
The Fourteenth Chapter.
In cases of foul-smelling nose.
If the treatment mentioned in the division does not succeed, hasten to cauterize the patient's coccyx for three nights, then shave his head and cauterize the middle of it with an olive iron. Then cauterize it with a nail iron twice above the eyebrows, slightly under the hair, and be careful not to cut the artery. This is an image of the nail iron.
Chapter Fifteen.
On cauterization for relaxation of the eyelid.
If an eyelid relaxes due to illness or moisture, cauterize the eyelid once with this crescent-shaped iron.
And if you wish, cauterize above the eyebrows a little twice on each side, keeping them apart from the temples, and let the length of each cautery be along the eyebrow.
Do not overdo the cautery, but only to the extent that one-third of the skin is burned. The iron will be shaped like this.
Chapter Sixteen.
On cauterization of the eyelid.
If its eyelashes turn inward and irritate the eye, then cauterization is done in two ways. Either cauterization with fire or cauterization with a burning medication. As for cauterizing it with fire, order the patient to leave his eyelashes alone beforehand, if he is one who plucks them, until they grow long and even.
For if it is irritated while sprouting, bind his eyes with a bandage so that they do not move until they grow. And when they have grown and are even, place the patient's head in your lap, then draw a mark on the eyelid with ink in the shape of an ascal leaf.
The beginning of the mark should be near the eyelashes, then place cotton soaked in egg white or in the saliva of a barberry under the eyelid. Then heat an iron shaped like this.
Then it should be cauterized according to the shape you have been taught little by little many times until the surface of the skin, which is like an ivy leaf in appearance, is completely burned. A sign of your work being done well is that you will see the eyelid contracted and the hair raised from the fleshy part of the eye.
Then raise your hand at that time and leave it for three days, then apply cotton wool with butter to it until the scab comes off, then treat it with ointment until it heals if God wills. If anything of the hair returns after a while and the eyelid relaxes.
Then cauterize that spot again as you did first, and if the hair is in the lower eyelid, pluck it until it returns to its natural position so that the hair does not pierce the eye. As for cautery with a burning drug, it is to order the patient to leave the eyelashes alone until they grow long and even.
Then make a shape from paper like an ivy leaf, then take from the common soap and quicklime that has not been slaked one dirham in weight or thereabouts of each, and crush them all together thoroughly, and do this quickly so that it does not dry out. Then spread some of it on the paper you have made in the shape of an ivy leaf and place it on one or both eyelids, and put cotton wool soaked in egg white underneath the eye and the patient's head in your lap.
Place your index finger over the medicine and press it a little, moving it whenever the patient feels the burning sensation of the medicine because he will find it pleasurable like fire. As long as he feels the burning, leave the medicine and move it with your finger; but when the burning stops, remove the medicine and wash the eye with water.
Look and see if you find that the eyelid has risen as it rises when cauterized by fire or cut; otherwise, apply more of the medicine to the spot where the medicine has not affected and has not blackened until your work is complete and the eye becomes contracted, then place cotton wool with butter on it until the scab from the burn comes off.
Then treat it with nabath ointment or another until it heals, and you should be extremely careful that nothing from the medicine falls into the eye. If the eyelid relaxes after a few days and we fear something from the hair is in the eye.
Then apply medicine to that part of the eyelid which has relaxed, just as you did initially, and then treat it until it is healed. This is a picture of an asparagus leaf.
Know that people's eyes vary in size, so your cautery should be adjusted accordingly. This is not a subtle path to correctness for those who have practiced this craft.
Chapter Seventeen.
On the treatment of a nasal polyp that appears in the corner of the eye.
If you treat the polyp as described in the sections on diseases and your treatment is unsuccessful, it should be cauterized in this manner.
Order the patient to place his head in your lap and have a servant hold his head between your hands, holding it still so that he does not move or jerk his head.
Then place a cotton swab moistened in egg white or in the saliva of a sparrow on his eyes, and then heat the iron which is pictured here.
It should be hollow like the quill of an eagle's feather at one end, which is the cauterizing end. If you wish, the other end can be open, or if you wish it can be closed like a pomegranate seed, but this hollow end is best for your work, God willing.
Then squeeze the polyp if it is open and express the pus from it, or if it is not open, open it and remove the pus. Then place the hot iron on it, holding it with your hand until it reaches the bone.
Withdraw your hand slightly when cauterizing, moving it away from the eye towards the nose so that you do not miss with your hand or alarm the patient and cause the iron to fall into the socket of his eye and spoil it. If you reach the bone in the first cautery, then stop; otherwise, apply the iron a second time if needed.
Leave it for three days, then apply cotton wool soaked in butter and treat it with drying ointment until it heals. If forty days pass and it does not heal, apply a sharp, corrosive medicine so that the bone is exposed and prepare it as will be mentioned in its chapter, God willing.
Another method mentioned by some of the ancients is to intentionally cut the nasion and then place a thin tube in the same incision. This is its image.
Pour into it a weight of lead equal to one dirham, melted. Hold the tube firmly with your hand so that it does not move at all, lest the molten lead flow into his eye. You should place cotton wool soaked in egg white or water on the patient's eye.
For the lead will burn the area of the nasion and heal it in a wondrous way. If the nasion is healed by the cautery and treatment mentioned, then there is no need for anything else. Otherwise, one must resort to using nasal piercing and returning the nasion to its nasal passage as will be explained in its proper place.
Chapter Eighteenth.
On Cauterizing a Harelip.
A cleft often occurs in the lip, called a "harelip," especially in the lips of boys; it happens quite frequently. If you treat this cleft with what we have mentioned in the division and the treatment is unsuccessful, then heat a small, pointed iron like this.
Its cavity should be as sharp as a knife, then place it heated quickly into the same cleft until the cautery reaches the depth of the cleft, and then treat it with cerate so that it heals, God willing.
Chapter Nineteenth.
On Cauterizing a Nasion That Occurs in the Mouth.
If a tumor appears in the gum, palate, or roots of the molars, then it ulcerates and bursts, and a fistula forms from the pus discharge, and if you treat it and it is not successful, then you should heat an iron to a size that fits into the fistula.
Then insert it protected in the nostril hole and hold your hand until the protected iron reaches its depth and end, and do this once or twice, then treat it afterwards with what we have mentioned of treatment until it recovers if God wills.
If the material is cut off and healed, otherwise it is necessary to uncover the place and remove the corrupt bone as will be mentioned in its chapter if God wills.
Chapter Twenty.
On Loose Teeth and Relaxed Gums.
If the gums relax due to moisture and the teeth move, and you treat them with medicine and it does not work, then put the head of the patient in your lap and then heat the iron whose shape will come after this after putting the tube on the tooth.
And insert the iron into it protected quickly and hold your hand a little until the patient feels the heat of the fire has reached the root of the tooth, then lift your hand and then return the iron several times as you wish, then the patient fills his mouth with salt water.
And he holds it for an hour and then throws it away, for the loose tooth will be fixed and the relaxed gum will tighten and the corrupt moisture will dry up.
Chapter Twenty-One.
On Relieving Toothache.
If the toothache is due to cold or there are worms in it and treatment with medication does not work, cauterization is done in two ways: either cauterization with butter or cauterization with fire.
As for cauterization with butter, it is to take cow's butter, boil it in an iron ladle or in a shell, then take cotton and wrap it around the end of a rod, dip it in the boiling butter and place it on the aching tooth quickly and hold it until it cools.
Then return it several times until the force of the fire reaches the root of the tooth, and if you wish, dip a wick or cotton in cold butter and place it on the aching tooth, then put the heated iron on top of it until the fire reaches the bottom of the tooth.
As for cauterizing it with fire, take a copper tube or an iron tube and let there be some thickness in its body so that the heat of the fire does not reach the patient's mouth, then heat the iron which comes in its shape and place it on the same tooth, hold your hand until the iron cools down, do this several times.
Then the pain will go away either that same day or the next day, and after that cauterization, the patient should fill his mouth with good butter and hold it for an hour then spit it out, this is a picture of the iron.
Cauterize with whichever end you wish and to the extent possible, and this is a picture of the tube.
Chapter twenty-two.
On cauterizing pigs.
If the pigs are phlegmatic and have cold humors and do not yield to ripening with medication and you desire quick ripening, heat the hollow iron which has this shape, open at both ends.
So that the smoke comes out from the other end when cauterizing, place it heated on the same tumor once or twice if you need to do so, until it reaches the depth of the tumor, if the tumor is small, make the iron according to the size of the tumor.
Then leave it for three days and apply cotton soaked in butter to it until what was burned by the fire goes away, then treat it with ointment and wicks until it heals, God willing.
Chapter twenty-three.
On cauterization for hoarseness of voice and shortness of breath.
If phlegm prevails in the trachea, especially if it is accompanied by a cold temperament, the patient should first be purged with laxatives, and then cauterized with a cautery at the hollow of the throat, in the low position.
Beware of reaching the throat with the cautery, and do not burn more than half of the skin. Then apply another cautery at the nape joint, at the last vertebra, and it should be a nail-shaped cautery as previously described.
Then treat him as previously mentioned until he recovers, God willing.
Chapter Twenty-Four.
On the Cauterization of Lung Diseases and Cough.
If the cough and lung disease are from cold phlegm, and the patient does not have a fever or consumption, and the illness is chronic, cauterize two spots above the clavicles in the low soft empty spaces, and another cautery in the middle of the chest between the breasts.
The cautery should be nail-shaped as previously described, or if you prefer, cauterize with dots using the cautery called "the point," whose shape was previously described in the cauterization of earache. The points should range from thirty to that number.
Then treat him as previously mentioned until he recovers. You may also make a cautery with three branches in this shape to expedite the cauterization, as you cauterize three spots at once with it. This is the image of the cautery.
Chapter Twenty-Five.
On the Cauterization of the Armpit.
If the head of the humerus dislocates due to slippery phlegm, or does not remain in place after being put back so that it repeatedly dislocates with the slightest movement, as we have witnessed, then the joint should first be put back into place. Then the patient is laid on his back or on his right side, and then the skin inside the armpit is lifted upwards with fingers of your left hand if the joint has dislocated inwards.
Then a heated iron with two blades, such as this pictured one, is held.
Then it is cauterized with it until it penetrates to the other side, and the shape of the cautery becomes four cauterizations; it may also be cauterized with a three-bladed iron, in which case the shape of the cautery will then be six cauterizations, and the blades are on the neck of the vein.
And this is a picture of the three-bladed iron.
And one may be added to this number, making the cauterizations eight; then carded wool soaked with salt is applied to the cauterization, and the patient must rest and not move the limb for a time until it strengthens. If the dislocation is upwards, this rarely occurs.
So a single well-done nail cautery is made above the elbow, or many pinprick cauterizations; for the joint will be strengthened and the moisture will depart, and the patient will recover.
Chapter Twenty-Six.
On Cauterization of the Stomach.
If cold and much moisture occurs in the stomach, so that it is displaced from its temperament, and catarrhs increase to it, and it has been treated with various remedies without success, then the patient should lie on his back, extend his legs and arms, and then be cauterized with three cauterizations. One cautery under the spoon of the breast, the size of a finger, with a nail iron; and two cauterizations below on either side of the single one, so that the shape of the cauterizations becomes triangular, and they are spaced apart so as not to meet when they heal. The depth of the cautery should be two-thirds the thickness of the skin, and the shape of the cauterizations will be .
In this manner and to this extent no more.
And if you wish, cauterize one large eschar in the middle of the abdomen in this manner.
And the iron with which you cauterize this single eschar should be circular in shape as shown here.
And the stomach may be cauterized in dots for those who fear this cautery, which is to draw dots on the stomach with ink according to the desired size, then cauterize them with a dotted iron and treat them with the previously mentioned remedy until they heal.
Chapter twenty-seventh.
On cauterizing a cold liver.
If pain arises in the liver from coldness and dampness or from excessive flatulence until it departs excessively from its natural temperament, and the patient is treated with what we have mentioned in the division, but this does not succeed. The patient should lie on their back, and three cauteries should be drawn with ink in this shape and size exactly on the liver below the clavicles where the human elbow ends.
And there should be a space of the thickness of a finger between each cautery, and the cauterization should be straight along the body, do not press hard with the iron.
Let the amount you burn of the skin's thickness be half, no more, and the patient should stand on their feet; if they are unable to stand, they should lie down with extended legs and raised arms. This is an image of the iron.
You may be able to cauterize these eschars with a narrow iron if you have experience and skill in the craft, but be careful not to linger on the cautery so that it burns all the skin and reaches the abdomen, as the skin there is thin, know this well.
Chapter twenty-eighth.
On cauterizing a liver tumor.
If an abscess appears in the liver and you wish to know whether that tumor is in the flesh of the liver or in its peritoneum, then if it is in the flesh of the liver, the patient will find heaviness and pain without sharpness.
But if it is in the peritoneum of the liver, there will be severe sharpness with the pain and you see that physicians have become weary of treating it, so the patient should lie on his back then apply a poultice to the warm spot with medicated paste.
Then heat an iron in the fire, which is the kind that resembles a plane, and this is its image. Cauterize it with a single application until it burns all the skin and end the cautery at the peritoneum so that all the pus comes out, then treat it with the treatment for abscesses until it is healed.
And this type of cautery should not be used except by one whose training in medicine has been long and who has treated these diseases many times over by experience. Only then should he undertake such work, and leave it with me as the better option.
Chapter Twenty-Nine.
On Cauterizing a Fistula.
The ancients mentioned cauterizing a cold fistula with the root of zedoary in this manner: take one long, dried zedoary root as thick as your finger, dip it in oil and heat it in the fire. Then cauterize it with a single application between the connection of the clavicle and the neck, and two small cauterizations slightly below the nipple line, inclined toward the side under the chin, and two large cauterizations above the breasts between the third and fourth ribs.
And two more cauterizations between the fifth and sixth ribs, inclined slightly backward, and another in the middle of the chest, and one above the stomach, and three cauterizations from behind: one between the shoulders and two on either side of the spine below the cauterization that is between the hands.
Your hand should not be inserted deeply, but rather a cautery mark should be made on the surface of the skin. Some of the ancients have said that some people used to use an iron poker resembling a spear point which they would heat and insert between the ribs until it reached the tumor itself.
And the probe is removed as we described in regard to liver tumors, and with this cautery there is great danger; either the patient will die immediately or an incurable fistula will develop at the site.
Chapter Thirty.
Cauterization of the Spleen.
If you have treated splenic disease as described in the division and your treatment has been unsuccessful, then cauterization should be performed in three ways, all of which are correct. One is to apply three or four cauteries in a line along the length of the spleen.
These cauteries should be made in the same shape as those for the liver, with a space of the thickness of a finger or slightly more between each cautery. The iron should have the same properties as described for liver cautery and your hand should not be inserted deeply. The patient should lie on their back.
The other method is to raise the skin opposite the spleen where the patient's left elbow ends. The skin should be raised across the width of the body so that the cauteries fall along the length of the body.
Then insert the heated speculum very carefully until it penetrates through the skin on the other side, then withdraw the iron and you will have made four cauteries. If you wish, you may use the three-pronged iron for cautery instead.
Then treat the cautery site, allowing it to drain pus for many days; this is more effective than any of the previously mentioned treatments.
Chapter Thirty-One.
On the treatment of dropsy.
Cupping is only beneficial for dry dropsy, especially if you have treated the patient with the various treatments we mentioned in the division and your treatment was unsuccessful, then four cuppings should be applied around the navel and one cupping on the stomach.
Another cupping should be applied to the liver, and one to the spleen, and two behind his back between the shoulder blades, one opposite his chest and the other opposite his stomach. The depth of the cupping should be close to the thickness of the skin, then the cupping should be left open so that pus drains for a long time.
The patient should not be deprived of treatment after cupping with what is appropriate so that both meanings are combined and healing is hastened. The shape of the iron with which the back is cauterized is nail-shaped, as its image has previously appeared, and the one with which the stomach is cauterized is olive-shaped.
Chapter Thirty-Two.
On Cupping the Feet and Legs.
If the feet of a patient with dropsy become swollen and filled with yellowish water, cupping should be applied on the back of the foot in the depression between the little toe and the next one.
Hold your hand with the iron and do not bend it, then raise your hand and do not return it at all, for it will drain yellowish water. The iron should be shaped like this.
Then, two cuppings should be applied to each leg. The cupping should be done with the sharp end of the iron and should be along the length of the leg, one below the knee and the other below it towards the middle of the leg, and two cuppings on each thigh.
Leave the cupping open without further treatment for a long time so that water drains from it, then treat it with all that we have mentioned.
Chapter Thirty-Three.
In cases of diarrhea.
If the diarrhea is from cold and humidity until it weakens the holding and digesting powers which are in the stomach and intestines, and it was treated by various treatments and did not recover, and you see the patient capable of enduring a cautery and of strong power, then perform a large cauterization on his abdomen. As for the cauterization of the stomach, it is done with a circular iron and four light cauterizations around the navel, using a fine nail-shaped iron. One large cautery on the cotton above the pubis or two cauteries.
If you see the humidity is abundant and the patient can endure it, then perform a cautery on the groin and one on each buttock and perhaps add two small cauteries on the stomach near the large one. For this is an effective treatment whose benefit does not fail, God willing.
Chapter Thirty-Four.
On Cauterization for Anal Hemorrhoids.
If there are chronic hemorrhoids in the rectum, many or one, and they were from thick cold humors or corrupted moistures, and they were treated with what we mentioned in the division but the treatment was not successful, then cauterize the patient with three triangular cauteries below the sacral prominence under the coccyx a little.
And one cautery under the navel the size of two fingers, and if you believe his stomach has become cold and his food does not digest and you see his face swollen, then cauterize him with a large cautery on the stomach as previously mentioned and one on his liver. And another on his spleen with a nail-shaped iron and leave the cautery open for some time, then treat him until he recovers, God willing.
Chapter Thirty-Five.
On Cauterization for Warts.
After cutting them, heat an iron shaped like a nail, then insert it hot into the same cut wart and press hard until the iron reaches the mouth of the vein from which blood flows. Do this once or twice.
If there are many wens, cauterize each one as we have described, then treat them with the appropriate ointment until they heal. If you cauterize a large wen at once with cotton, it will be more beneficial.
Chapter Thirty-Six.
On the cautery of fistulas that occur in the perineum and its surroundings.
If the patient does not respond to incision and the procedure we have described in its place, and refuses it, then perhaps he will be cured by cautery. If a fistula occurs, and pus and foul moisture flow from it, the first thing you should do is measure it with a fine probe.
Then determine the depth of the fistula with the probe, and prepare a cautery iron that resembles a chisel. Insert it heated into the fistula itself along the straight course of the fistula and to the depth that the probe reached. Repeat the cautery until all those foul substances are burned away once, twice, or three times, as needed.
Be careful not to burn a nerve if there is one, or a large blood vessel. And be cautious if the fistula leads to the bladder or intestine.
Be careful of all these places, and only do this if the fistula is in a fleshy place and you determine that it is impassable. Then treat the area until it heals. If the area closes and the discharge ceases and remains so for some time, know that it has been completely cured.
If the discharge does not cease, know that it is passable or that there is a large foul substance in its depth or something similar to what will be mentioned in its place.
Chapter Thirty-Seven.
On the cautery of the kidneys.
If pain occurs in the kidney due to cold or a strong wind, and as a result the patient's semen is reduced, you should cauterize him on both sides, on each kidney, with the nail-shaped iron previously mentioned. Perhaps you will cauterize it three times on the same table, resulting in three cauterizations in a row. This will be the most beneficial.
Chapter Thirty-Eight
On the Bladder.
If there is weakness and relaxation in the bladder due to cold and dampness, so that the patient cannot hold urine, make incisions in the lower part of the perineum, on the bladder, where the pubic hair begins; one incision on the right side of the perineum and another on the left.
The incision on each side should be the length of a thumb joint. Make an additional incision in the lower back, or two if necessary. The cautery should be pointed as previously described.
Chapter Thirty-Nine.
On Cauterizing the Womb.
If a substance of cold and dampness occurs in the womb, so that menstruation is obstructed and its flow corrupted, or if pain occurs upon its arrival, three cauteries should be made around the perineum as we described for cauterizing the bladder. One cautery should be made on the pubic hair below the back, or two if necessary. The cautery should be pointed.
Chapter Forty.
On Cauterizing to Dislocate the Hip.
Moist humors may sometimes collect in the hip joint, causing it to dislocate. The sign of this is that one leg will be longer than the other when they are measured against each other, and you will find a void at the site of dislocation. A semicircular cautery should then be made on the hip joint itself.
After you have marked the area around the joint with ink, as if drawing a circle to enclose it, make sure that the center of the circle corresponds with the dislocated joint. The cautery iron used should be the one previously described for cauterizing the stomach. If this iron is not available, make three incisions with a large olive-wood cautery.
The depth of the cautery should be equal to the thickness of all the skin, then treat it until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Forty-One
On the cauterization of the sciatic nerve.
If pain occurs in the hip and its cause is cold and moisture, and the patient has been treated as mentioned in the division, but the treatment does not succeed and time passes, then it is necessary to ease the patient from thick humors with purslane or marshmallow, or the like.
Then cauterize the area, and there are two types of cautery: cautery with burning medications or cautery with fire. Cautery with fire is done in several ways, one of them being to apply three triangular cauteries directly on the hip itself in this manner.
They should be of an appropriate depth, and the distance between each cautery should be the width of a finger. The cautery iron should be oiled. Sometimes one cautery is applied to the center on top of the hip itself, resulting in four cauteries. If you wish, you may cauterize it with the circle mentioned previously for hip dislocation with one cautery.
This circle will encircle the entire hip and burn all the skin thickness. This is the description of cautery for sciatica if the pain is localized to the hip itself and does not extend to the thigh and leg.
But if the pain extends to the thigh and leg, apply two cauteries on the thigh in the spot indicated by the patient as painful, and one cautery above the ankle bone with four fingers toward the outer side. Use a thin iron, and the depth of the cautery should be equal to the thickness of the skin only.
If the patient indicates that the pain extends toward the toes, apply three or four cauteries with a pointed iron where indicated, or more if needed. If the pain is indicated as being below the knee toward the leg, apply one thin cautery there.
Be cautious in all your cauteries not to burn a nerve or large blood vessel, as this will cause the patient serious harm or disability.
I have seen one or two people who cauterized above the ankle and then squeezed in the cautery, until the leg swelled to the point of reaching the foot and it all became suppurated and ruined the whole leg, then diarrhea and death followed. If the pain is on both sides, cauterize them in the same manner.
Some wise scholars have mentioned that when cauterizing the hip, it should be done in this way: make a kind of crucible out of iron, with an opening half a handbreadth wide, and at its mouth a thickness equal to or slightly less than the kernel of a date. Inside this crucible is another, and inside that one there is a third.
The space between each crucible should be the width of a thumb joint. Each crucible should be open on both sides and about a handbreadth or two in height, with an iron handle firmly fixed to them. This is its illustration.
Then it is heated in the fire until red-hot and the sparks are removed, then placed on the buttock bone and the ilium while leaning on the correct side. It cauterizes three circular cauteries at the same time. Then it is left for three days and bandaged with butter, leaving the wound open for several days.
Afterwards, it is treated with ointment until it heals. The author of this book said that this type of cautery is rarely used due to its ugliness and frightening appearance and because few people can endure it, but it is a good cautery for those who are patient enough to endure it and are affected in the right place.
As for cauterization with burning medications, it is done by making two crucibles resembling a mortar, into which the reflux of the retort descends, made of copper or iron.
The crucibles have a height of about two handbreadths, and the space between them is about the width of a finger. They are open at the bottom and top, and some are fastened together in this manner.
Then you lower it onto the right hip bone, with the patient lying on his right side and hold your hand firmly; then pour between the two circles the sharp water, which has been heated by a small fire pot, and keep it there for the space of an hour-glass. The patient must endure its stinging and burning, for he will find a pleasure like that of fire.
Until the stinging subsides, then you raise your hand with the instrument and wipe off all the sharp water from the hip with fresh water; then leave it for three days and apply butter to it until the blackness of the burn is gone. Leave it for several days so that pus flows from it, for this is most beneficial.
Then treat it with ointments until it is healed. If the pain extends to the thigh or leg, make for him a mold that will hold the water according to what you made for the hip. This is the description of sharp water and he has proven it in his treatise on the Correction of Drugs.
Take one part each of saltpeter and quicklime, crush them together and put them in a new pot which has been pierced with a single small hole at the bottom, just large enough to admit a reed. Place another glazed pot underneath.
Pour fresh water over the saltpeter and quicklime, enough to cover them by a finger’s breadth after you have well pressed them together with your hand, and leave the pot until the sharp water descends into the glazed pot below. Then collect all of that water and pour it over new lime and saltpeter which have also been renewed.
It will then be extremely sharp and effective for many medical purposes and in cauterizing all the organs, for it acts like fire itself. There are other drugs with which the hip can also be cauterized, such as thafasiya, honey of bladder senna, and lime mixed with soap.
Galen related a story about an ancient man who had a remedy for hip pain and sciatica, exaggerating its importance greatly and claiming that no other treatment was needed and that it would cure with a single application.
Even he may enter the bath carried and come out of it completely healed. It is to take from the fresh green chickpea, if green is not found, then one takes a dry chickpea. Grind it with some fat and put it on the hip where there is pain, or in the leg or thigh.
Tighten it and leave it for three hours, or as long as the patient feels the burning subside, then put him in the bath. If his body sweats, put him in the basin, for the pain will go away and he will be healed by God's will. If he is not healed, repeat the poultice after ten days again.
He will be healed if God wills, and know that no one should use cauterization with these medicines except after the body has been purged, and Dioscorides mentioned that goat's hair if used to cauterize sciatica is beneficial.
Cauterization is done as follows: take wool, soak it in old oil and place it on the deep location between the thumb of the hand and the index finger, closer to the index finger. Then take a dry goat's hair.
Ignite it with fire until it becomes a coal, then place it on the wool and leave it until it goes out, then take another and continue doing this until the sensation reaches the middle of the arm to the hip and the pain subsides by God's will. This type of cauterization is called hair cauterization.
Chapter Forty-Two.
On Cauterization for Back Pain.
Back pain can arise from many causes, such as a fall, blow, excessive purging, and so on. It is often due to the accumulation of a cold, wet substance. Cauterization is applied only in this type.
When back pain is caused by the accumulation of a cold substance, after the patient has been purged with a laxative like natron and so on, they should be cauterized on their back where the pain is, in three rows across the width of the table itself. Mark the location with ink. In each row, apply five or more cauterizations according to the patient's tolerance and strength.
It should be cauterized with a pointed iron, and if you wish, cauterize it three or four times with a medium-sized nail iron in this manner.
Chapter Thirty-Third.
In the cautery of the beginning of the swelling.
This ailment often occurs in young children, and its initial sign in a child is shortness of breath upon standing and moving, and you will find at the end of his back vertebrae a lump that has protruded over all other lumps.
If you see this and want to stop it, cauterize it with an iron that is circular in this manner. Let the cautery be applied to each side of the vertebra evenly, and if you wish, cauterize it around the vertebra with a pointed iron in two or three rows, making sure the dots are close together; then treat the area until it heals as we have described.
And be careful to use cautery on the swelling that is caused by nerve constriction.
Chapter Forty-Fourth.
On the Cauterization of Gout and Joint Pains.
If joint pains are from cold dampness settling into any part of the body, and if these pains occur in the legs, it is customary for physicians to call this gout specifically.
If cold gout is treated with the types of treatment mentioned in the division and the pain does not go away, then cautery will remove it.  This involves cauterizing around the ankle joint after vomiting, applying many cauteries. Use a medium-sized olive iron in this manner.
And if you need to cauterize the face, do so with a pointed iron; if the pain rises to the knees or to all other joints, which often happens, cauterize each knee three or four times on each side using this same olive iron.
If you need more than this, take it and do not delve your hand into the cautery, but let it be about the thickness of the skin only. If the pain ascends to the hips or back, use what we have mentioned about cautery in its chapter.
If the pain is only in the hands, punctuate around the wrists as two rows turn. If the pain remains in the fingers, punctuate each knuckle with a point and on the back of the hand. If the pain ascends after a few days to the elbows or shoulders.
Cauterize them from all sides and let the patient not be without good management and taking medicine, for if he eats well and expels phlegm, he will recover with this cautery, God willing.
Chapter Forty-Fifth.
On Cauterizing The Skull.
If a hernia occurs in the groin and some intestine and omentum descends to the testicle, and this is recent and near the beginning, it is necessary to order the patient to abstain from food for a day and to use laxatives that will expel stool from his bowels.
Then let him lie on his back between your hands and order him to hold himself until the omentum or intestine protrudes, then push it back in with your finger, and then mark with ink under the hernia on the pubic bone a mark resembling a semicircle with its ends pointing upwards towards the body.
Then heat an iron in this shape.
Until it becomes white and throws sparks, then push the intestine or omentum back into its place and have a servant hold his hand over the spot so that the intestines do not protrude again; with the patient's legs parted and a pillow placed beneath him, and another servant holding his legs.
And another on his chest, holding his hands, then lower the iron onto the mark itself and hold it upright with your hand until it reaches the bone, or repeat it again if it did not reach the bone the first time.
Preserve your efforts from the protrusion of the intestines while cauterizing so as not to burn them, for that would cause the patient either death or a great affliction. Know that if you do not reach the bone with the cautery, your work will not succeed. The cautery iron should be gentle according to their ages, both for children and adults.
Then treat the cauterized spot with butter after three days so that the burn's dryness goes away, and then treat it with all other ointments until it heals. Let the patient lie on their back for forty days until the wound closes.
You should make his food during his treatment something that softens his stomach so that the intestines do not protrude when straining and defecating. Then, if he wishes to get up after forty days, use a tight bandage and keep it for another forty days. Reduce exertion, fullness from food and drink, and loud shouting.
If he follows this regimen, he will be completely healed, God willing. I will mention the treatment of hernia by incision in its chapter, God willing. As for the hernia that occurs in the rest of the abdomen and is in its early stages, if you want it not to increase, cauterize it with a round cautery iron according to its size. Let what you burn of the skin be one-third of it, then treat it as we mentioned; it will not increase, God willing. Some of the ancients have mentioned cauterizing the hernia with a triangular iron in this shape.
After you have learned to mark the hernia with a plumb line, make a horizontal line at the top of the hernia and the other line below it. Then put your hand in the middle and cauterize it with one nail-shaped cautery. The first cautery is easier and better, God willing.
Chapter Forty-Six.
On Cauterizing the Hernia.
If pain occurs in some of the members due to a fall or blow, and that pain lasts for a long time and is not relieved by medication, it should be cauterized with a point iron, cauterizing each member according to its size, weakness, and strength.
If the pain and inflammation are relieved by the first cautery, then it is healed. Otherwise, repeat the cautery because it is customary for these pains to transfer from one member to another nearby, so it should be followed up with cauterization until the patient recovers.
Chapter Forty-Seventh.
On Cautery for Leprosy.
As for lepers, they may benefit greatly from cautery, especially the type of leprosy that results from putrefaction of phlegm and black bile.
If you wish to cauterize them, and the leprosy is in its early stages and you have treated it as mentioned in the division, but it has not subsided or stopped, and you fear that corruption will take over all of his humors, then apply five cauteries on the head. One in the middle of the head, the usual spot; the second below it toward the forehead at the end of the hair; two on the temples; and one behind on the nape of the neck. Press your hand firmly during the cautery for a short time until it slightly affects the bone.
Scales will peel off, making it easier for the thick vapors to escape. Cauterize another cautery on the same spleen as mentioned above. As for leprosy that has persisted in the patient and appeared clearly, you should apply these cauteries we mentioned on the head. One cautery on the tip of the nose, two on the cheeks, two on the sides of the neck, six on the sides of the back, a large one on the coccyx at the base of the spine and another above it in the same area, and two on the thighs, one on each. Two on the knees, one on each knee, two on the elbows, two on the elbows, two on the armpits, and a bandage on each joint of the fingers of his hands and feet, as well as on each heel and on the wrists of his hands.
And preserve the nerve that is on the back of the heels so as not to burn them, for it may be cauterized on the pubic bone and another on the mouth of the stomach and another on the liver, and know that the more you increase the cautery, the more beneficial and effective it will be.
And know that the patient does not feel pain from cauterization as a healthy person does, because his body has become numb, and the cautery irons should be of varying sizes according to the limbs and joints as previously described regarding the characteristics of the irons.
Then treat the cautery with frankincense resin mixed with honey and all other treatments until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Forty-Eight.
In Cauterization of Numbness, When a limb has become numb and has been treated with medications, ointments, and bandages but has not recovered, cauterize the same numb limb with cautery irons according to what suits the size of the limb, whether large or small, and let your cautery be slightly deeper in thick skin.
Then treat it with ointment until it heals, and sometimes cauterization can be performed for some numbness that occurs in the hand or foot in cases of back pain at the exit point of the nerve that moves that limb, thus eliminating the numbness.
And this should only be done by someone who is knowledgeable about the anatomy of the body and the exits of the nerves that move it.
Chapter Forty-Nine.
On Cauterization for Leprosy.
If leprosy has become advanced and no medical remedy has been effective, cauterize it slightly deep according to the thickness of the skin so that the whiteness disappears and its color changes.
Then treat it with lentil flour mixed with rose oil, plantain leaf and pigeon blood or the blood of small birds, a part from each. Mix all together and apply it to a cloth and bind it over the area until it is healed.
Chapter Fifty.
On the Treatment of Cancer.
If the cancer is in its early stages and you wish to stop it, cauterize it with a circular iron, going around it as if turning. Some sages have mentioned cauterizing a significant amount in the middle of it, but I do not approve of this because I expect it to ulcerate. I have witnessed this many times.
The correct method is to cauterize around it in a circle as we have said, or with many cauterizations.
Chapter Fifty-One.
On the Cautery of an Aneurysm.
If someone has an aneurysm which has slowed in maturation, either due to the efficacy of the cause or because the patient is old and has little blood, or simply due to time, and you wish to hasten its maturation, cauterize around it with many small, punctuating cauteries.
Then leave it; for it will hasten maturation. If you wish to cauterize it, use an iron with this shape.
Lower it into the middle of the aneurysm until it penetrates the skin. Let the cautery be as described below so that pus can easily flow, then treat it accordingly until it heals.
Chapter Fifty-Two.
On the Cautery of a Fistula.
A fistula is a corruption that progresses within a member, consuming it like fire consumes dry wood. If you see a fistula in a location suitable for cautery with fire, use many small and large cauterizing irons, shaped like nails, according to what suits the location of the fistula.
Then cupping is done from all sides until all the corruption is eradicated and nothing remains of it at all, then it is left for three days and sulfur powder mixed with oil is applied to the scorched areas so that all the scabs and corruption are removed.
Then it is treated with flesh-producing ointments. If you see after three days that the flesh grows healthily without corruption, otherwise, cauterize what remains of the corrupted areas, and the eating sore may be treated with a caustic drug.
For it acts in place of fire more quickly and beneficially, and I have mentioned its treatment with a caustic drug in the division, so take it from there when you need it.
Chapter Thirty-Five.
On Cauterizing Reversed and Unreversed Nails.
This ailment often occurs on the soles of the feet, it is something rough that afflicts a man, and cauterization for it takes two forms: either cauterization with fire or cauterization with caustic water.
As for cauterization with fire, it is to heat a hollowed iron resembling an eagle's feather, made of iron to the extent that it encircles the nail from all sides, and its edge should be thin, then lower it while hot onto the nail, and turn your hand with the iron around the nail until the iron reaches the depth of the nail.
Leave it for three days so that it fills with pus, then dress it with wild barley flour pounded with salt and leave the dressing on it overnight; for it will come off from its roots, then treat the wound site with a flesh-producing ointment until it heals. If the nails are not reversed.
It often occurs on the surface of the body, especially in the hands and feet, so you should take a tube made of copper or iron or an eagle's feather and place it on the nail or wart, then pour a small amount of caustic water into the tube.
Hold your hand and turn it slightly with a squeeze of your hand so that the rim of the tube touches the base of the nail, allowing the sharp water to find its way to the base of the nail. Let the patient endure the stinging of the sharp water for a moment.
Then you leave it, the nail will come off at its base. You do this one by one until all of them are removed from the body, then treat their places after they are removed with ointments that promote flesh growth.
Chapter Forty-Five.
On Cupping.
If someone experiences cupping due to cold in a nerve or from fever quarters or something else, four or five cups should be applied on the back between each vertebra and one cup on their chest. And a cup on their stomach with an olive oil lamp, for cupping soothes and accelerates the ripening of cold illnesses.
Chapter Fifty-Five.
On Cupping for Swelling in the Body.
Ugly blisters may erupt in the body, resulting from thick, cold, and corrupt humors. Upon their initial appearance, a gentle cup should be applied to the head of each blister using a slender aspen twig with one end ignited by fire or the root of long asphodel or a lentil-shaped iron.
Similarly, boils should be cupped in this manner upon their initial eruption so that they do not worsen, and the active principle dissipates, leading to recovery for the patient. However, it is important that this be done after the patient has undergone bloodletting.
Chapter Fifty-Six.
On Cupping for Bleeding After Artery Severance.
Bleeding from a severed artery often occurs during external wounds, incisions into tumors, or cupping of an organ, making its cauterization difficult.
If this should happen to anyone, quickly put your hand to the mouth of the artery and place your middle finger upon it and press it firmly so that the blood is held under your finger and nothing comes out. Then take small and large olive-wood splinters and heat them in the fire until they become very hot.
Then take one of them, either small or large according to the wound and the place where the artery has burst open, and apply the hot splinter to the vein after quickly removing your finger and hold it there until the blood stops flowing.
If the blood flows again when you lift your finger from the mouth of the artery and the splinter goes out, quickly take another splinter from those prepared in the fire and continue to do so one after the other until the bleeding stops.
Be careful not to burn any nerves that may be there, for this would cause the patient further pain. Know that if blood flows from an artery, it cannot be cut, especially if it is a large one, except in one of four ways: either by cauterization as we have said, or by amputation if it has not already been amputated. For if it is amputated, its ends will contract and the bleeding will stop. It can then either be tied tightly with thread or have medications applied to it that staunch the blood flow and be bound firmly with bandages.
But attempting to cut it with a ligature, or by tightening cloth around it, or by applying burning objects, and the like, will not stop the bleeding except in rare cases. If someone suffers this and cannot find a doctor or medicine, let them quickly place their middle finger over the mouth of the wound itself as we have described.
Press it down very firmly so that the blood is confined and flows from above the wound onto the artery and finger. Do not remove your finger with cold water, always keep applying it until the blood freezes, thickens, and stops flowing. During this time, see what cauterization or medicine is needed.
Chapter Two
On incision, cauterization, bloodletting and surgery and the like.
He who came before us has already mentioned in the first chapter every disease that can be treated with burning, cautery and its causes, reasons and instruments, and the figures of the cautery irons; and he arranged this in chapters from beginning to end.
And I will follow the same path in this chapter so that it is easy for the student to attain his goal.
Before I begin with that, you should know, my sons, that this chapter contains more intricacies than the first chapter on cautery.
Therefore, caution should be even greater here, for work in this chapter often involves the evacuation of blood, which sustains life, when an artery is opened or an incision is made on a tumor or abscess is lanced, or surgery is performed to remove an arrow or to incise a stone, and so forth. All of these carry risk and danger, and most of them can result in death.
And I advise you against venturing into what is doubtful, for in this craft you may encounter people with various diseases. Some of them may be weary of their illness and find death preferable.
Some may offer you their wealth, hoping for a cure, while their disease is combative. You should not assist those who come to you with this disposition at all.
Let your caution be greater than your desire and zeal, and do not proceed with anything unless you have certain knowledge that will lead to a favorable outcome. In all treatment of your patients, use advance knowledge and warning as guides to safety.
For in this way, you will gain praise, glory, remembrance and gratitude. May Allah inspire you, my sons, with right guidance and never deprive you of correctness and success, for these are in His hand. There is no god but He.
This chapter has been arranged in sections, from the top of the head to the feet, as was discussed in the previous chapter on cauterization, so that you may more easily find what you seek, God willing.
The First Chapter.
On the treatment of water that collects in the heads of boys.
This ailment often occurs in boys at birth if the midwife presses on the boy's head without gentleness, and it may also arise from a hidden cause that is unknown.
I have not seen this condition in anyone other than boys, and everyone I have seen with it quickly died; therefore, I decided to abandon treating it. I once saw a boy whose head was full of water, and his head grew larger every day. Until the boy could no longer sit up because of the size of his head and the moisture increased until he died.
This moisture either collects between the skin and the bone or gathers under the bone on the peritoneum. The procedure is as follows: if the moisture is between the skin and the bone, and the swelling is small, one should make a single incision across the middle of the head, about two finger-widths long, so that the moisture can drain. This is an illustration of the surgical instrument.
But if the moisture is greater and the swelling larger, make two incisions that intersect, like this:
And if the moisture is under the bone, a sign of which is that you see the sutures of the skull open on all sides and the water descends when you press it with your hand into the interior—this will not be hidden from you—then you should make three incisions in the middle of the head, like this:
After making the incisions, all the moisture will come out; then tighten the incisions with rags and bandages, and then cover them from above with a drink and oil until the fifth day. Then loosen the bandage and treat the wound with poultices and ointments. Do not neglect to bind the head moderately, and feed the patient with all dry foods that are low in moisture.
Until the member becomes strong and heals, another method of incision is to look where the bone appears at the gathering of water, for it may be more in the back of the head or in the front of it, or on the right or on the left.
So you intend by the incision where the swelling appeared to you and filled with water, so make an incision as much as you can while preserving that you do not cut an artery causing hemorrhage from which the patient may die along with vomiting of moisture.
Chapter Two.
Cutting the arteries behind the ears, known as the temporals.
When one is stricken with a sudden cold to the eyes or chest, and it persists and medications do not succeed in treating it, then the treatment for that is cutting these two arteries.
If you wish to cut them, shave the patient's head with a razor then rub the place with a rough cloth so that the artery appears. Then tighten the patient's neck by means of their garment. Then look where the pulse is and the two places are the lower depressions behind the ears, they rarely hide except in some people.
