Then mark them with ink and cut them with a scalpel to the bone, this should be done across the head. If you wish, you may insert the scalpel under the artery and pull it upwards while cutting, the length of the incision being about two fingers' widths.
For if the artery is severed, blood will flow out in a pulsating manner, springing forward and back repeatedly. If the artery does not appear to the touch, then you should measure three finger widths from the ear and mark with ink and cut to the bone. What is necessary...
Send six ounces of blood at once, and perhaps you may send more or less; all this depends on the strength of the patient and the fullness of his arteries. Then look at the wound, if any peritoneum remains upon the bone, cut it off.
So that a hot tumor does not appear, then tighten the wound with a wick of linen cloth, and then treat it with ointments until it heals.
Chapter Three.
Sever the two arteries in the fingers.
If a person has chronic migraine or severe acute illnesses from humid heat and muscle fever in the fingers or chronic severe headache, and has been treated with various medical treatments without success, we have found that severing or cauterizing the arteries of the fingers, as described, is effective for these conditions.
The procedure for severing them is as follows: shave the hair on the fingers and aim for the visible artery in the thumb, which will be apparent to you from its pulse; it rarely hides except in rare individuals.
Or if the cold is severe and it is hidden from you, have the patient tighten their neck with their garment; then rub the spot with a rag or compress the spot with hot water until the artery becomes clearly visible. Then take the scalpel pictured here. Then gently peel back the skin with it until you reach the artery. Insert a ligature into it and pull it upwards until it emerges from the skin, freeing it from the fascia beneath on all sides. If the artery is thin, twist it with the end of the ligature. Then cut a portion of it, depending on how far apart the ends need to be so that they constrict and no bleeding occurs. For if it is not severed and does not break, the blood will not recede at all; then drain the blood from six to three ounces.
If the artery is large, it should be tied in two places with a strong doubled thread; let the thread be either of silk or of lute strings so that gangrene does not set in before the wound heals and cause bleeding.
Then you cut the remainder between the two ligaments, doing this at that moment or later. If you wish to make it adhere to the bone with a cauterizing iron until its ends are severed, this will serve as well as, or even better than, the previous operation.
However, if the patient has a fever or a disturbed temperament, because cauterization helps to eliminate moisture, it will be even more beneficial. After ligating the arteries, the site should be stuffed with soft cotton and tight bandages should be applied. After removal of the ligatures, it should be treated with dry medications that promote flesh growth and bandaged until it heals, God willing.
If, during your work, bleeding occurs from an artery, immediately cut it, either by cauterization or by filling the area with pitch and pressing your hand until the bleeding stops. If none of this is available, place your finger there until the blood clots.
Wash the area with very cold water to soothe the burning and bandage it properly. Something easier and less severe than ligating the artery is to cauterize the vessel with this two-bladed iron after marking the locations with ink.
Lower it while very hot until it reaches the bone and the vessel is severed in two places so that there is distance between its ends, for it will not heal at all with this cauterization. This is a picture of the iron.
Its two blades are sharp, resembling small pincers but they are much less sharp than a knife because if they were as sharp as a knife, they would cool quickly and the flesh would not be severed readily. If there is some thickness to them, they will retain the heat of the fire.
And it cuts the flesh quickly. This operation is better than all others, lighter and easier, and the distance between the blades should be the width of a finger.
Chapter Four
In the treatment of continuous hot tear flow to the eyes.
If the tears are continuous and flow from the veins on the outside of the head, and no benefit is derived from medical treatment with drugs, and you see the face of the patient has reddened. And he feels a tingling in his forehead like the crawling of ants and his eyes are shaken and wet, their lids have become firmly glued from the intensity of the tears, so those who came before us in treating it resorted to this procedure which is called treatment by sword, which is to order the patient to shave the hair on his forehead.
Then make three parallel incisions on the forehead, each incision being about two fingers long. The first incision is parallel to the length of the nose in the middle of the forehead, the second a short distance from the movement of the muscle in the temple, and the third on the other side.
Take care not to cut the arteries on either side and keep your hand away from the connection of the jaws. The space between each incision should be about three clenched fingers. Have prepared pieces of sponge or many absorbent cloths ready to staunch the bleeding.
Then insert the sharp, double-pointed bistoury, which is pictured here. From the incision next to the temple to the middle incision, and scrape away all the skin and fascia on the bone between them. Then do the same from the middle incision to the third incision. Then remove the bistoury. Then insert into the first incision another instrument called a scalpel, sharp on one side and smooth on the other. This is its picture:
Place the sharp side upwards towards the flesh adhering to the skin and the smooth side downwards toward the bone. Push it until it reaches the middle incision, cutting all the vessels that descend from the head to the eyes without the cut reaching the surface of the skin.
Then you do that in the middle incision to the other incision, and after a moderate amount of blood flows, press the places of the solid blood clots, then put a wick of cotton in each incision and place a bandage soaked with syrup and oil or vinegar and oil on it.
So that a hot swelling does not occur, and on the third day the ligature is dissolved and much irrigation is used with lukewarm water, then it is treated with balm of balsam with rose oil and all that you treat wounds with until it heals, if God wills.
Chapter Five.
In the treatment of tears and discharges to the eyes from the interior of the head.
When one has frequent, sharp, persistent discharges and sees the eyes as two small agitated objects and their sight has weakened and the eyelids are ulcerated and eyelashes fall out from them. And there is a sharp, painful ache deep in the head and continuous sneezing, know from these symptoms that those substances and discharges come from many deep vessels and the best treatment for it is this treatment, which is to order the patient to shave their forehead, then make one incision in the middle of the forehead.
And there is a sharp, painful ache deep in the head and continuous sneezing, know from these symptoms that those substances and discharges come from many deep vessels and the best treatment for it is this treatment, which is to order the patient to shave their forehead, then make one incision in the middle of the forehead. Or raise it a little sideways and begin the incision from the left temple to the right temple, the incision being to the bone and keeping your hand away from the temporal muscles that move when chewing, until the bone is exposed and all the blood is dried with a sponge.
Then separate the edges of the incision with lint cotton or a twist of linen, then tighten it from above with bandages and soak the bandages with syrup and oil so that a hot swelling does not occur. When you dissolve them and see that the hot swelling has subsided, you should rub the bone until the growth of flesh begins there.
Then treat it with a drying treatment that promotes the growth of flesh, such as taking two parts of wheat flour and four parts of litharge and preparing an ointment from them to be used in the growth of flesh in such surgeries.
Chapter Six.
In the treatment of what falls into the ear.
Everything that falls into the ear is one of four types: either a mineral stone or something like stone, such as iron and glass; or a vegetable seed, such as chickpeas and kernels and the like; or something liquid, such as water and vinegar and the like.
And if an animal falls into the ear, whenever a pebble or something like a pebble falls into the ear and it does not exceed in the ear, expose the ear to the sun. If you see the pebble, put something of violet oil or cerate on it, then try to remove it by moving your head or sneezing with a nosebleed. And block the nostrils when sneezing after placing a collar of cloth or wool around the ear and extending the ear upwards, for it often comes out with this treatment. If it does not come out, then try to remove it with the delicate tool pictured here.
If it comes out with the tool, or else try to remove it with a fine, flexible blind hook. If it doesn't come out that way, then make a tube of copper and insert the end of the tube into the ear hole carefully.
Seal what is around the tube with softened pitch so that the wind has no path but the tube, then draw it strongly with your breath and it often comes out this way. If it does not come out as we have described, then take a little mastic from the gum arabic or the prepared gum used to catch birds and place it on the tip of the probe.
After wrapping it tightly with cotton, then insert it into the ear hole gently after drying the ear from moisture. If it does not come out with all that we have described, then proceed to incision before a hot swelling or spasm occurs.
The incision prescription is to lance the patient in the temple first and let out blood according to their strength, then sit the patient between your hands and turn their ear upwards. Cut a small incision at the base of the ear near its cartilage in the lower part of it.
The incision should be crescent-shaped until you reach the stone, then remove it as best you can with instruments, and sew up the incision quickly afterwards and treat it until it heals.
If the object that has fallen in the ear is one of the grains that swells and becomes enlarged, try to remove it as we have mentioned; if it does not come out, take a fine, delicate probe shaped like this.
And try to cut that kind of grain that has fallen in the ear; but only do this if you are sure that the grain has become moistened by the vapor of the ear until it becomes many small pieces, then remove it with a blind syringe or a fine forceps or a sucker.
As we have mentioned, it is easy to remove it; but water that has entered the ear should be treated by having the patient sneeze with a reed first, and having filled his ear with some loose cotton while he lies on that side where the water is, if it comes out that way.
Otherwise, take many small pebbles, smooth and flat along the fingers, warm them slightly in the fire, and have the patient insert one of them into the hole of his ear, while he stands on one leg from that side and strikes with another stone the stone that is in his ear.
He should continue to do this pebble by pebble until all the water comes out; and the water may come out if one takes a reed or a feather and inserts one end into the ear, while he applies the other end to the fire until most of it burns away then repeats with another feather.
Do this several times until all the water comes out, or draw it out with a tube as previously mentioned with the pebble; but if you wish to remove an animal that has entered, first look and see: if its body is small like a flea or the like, treat it as I have described in the section on...
If its body is large enough to be felt, try to remove it with forceps and probes; this is easier than removing anything else that grows in the ear. As for extracting worms bred in the ear, if you have treated them as mentioned in the section on catarrhs and your treatment has not been successful...
...you should look into the ear towards the sun; if you see any part of the worm, remove it with forceps or fine probes. If you do not see any part of it, take a tube shaped like this.
It should be narrow at the bottom and wide at the top, and insert the thin end into the ear as far as the patient can tolerate, then suck strongly with it. Do this several times until all the worms come out. If they do not come out for you, seal around the tube with wax as I mentioned to you in the case of a stone.
If they do not come out as mentioned, use the catorrhs that I have mentioned; the ancients used them to kill worms and you will find this in the section on catorrhs. Pouring ointments and medicines into the ear with this instrument, which is shaped like this:
As you see, make it from silver or brass, narrow at the bottom with a small hole and wide at the top. If you wish, the cannon in the inside of the tube can be made of solid brass; if you wish, take a spindle and wrap cotton tightly around its end.
Then pour the ointment or juice, or whatever medicine you want, into the tube while it is in the ear. Then insert the spindle with cotton from above and squeeze it moderately with your hand until the ointment is driven into the auditory canal and he feels it.
Let the patient feel it going in, and let whatever you pour into the ear be warmed slightly by the fire. Beware that what drips from it is not too cold or too hot, for the ear cannot tolerate it.
Chapter Seven.
In the treatment of aural obstruction.
Some children may be born from their mothers with their ears unpierced.
It may also happen that some people experience blockage in the ear canal from a wound or growing flesh. This blockage may be deep within the ear hole, imperceptible to sight.
Sometimes it protrudes slightly from the hole and is visible, while blockage deep within the ear and imperceptible to sight tends to be more difficult to heal. If the blockage is visible, one should expose the patient's ear to sunlight. Observe it carefully; if you see the blockage, open it with a delicate perforator, which should look like this:
Its tip should be slightly broad, with a limited width, and the rest of the perforator should be smooth on both sides so as not to injure the ear. If the blockage is due to flesh that has grown in the ear canal, grasp it with a delicate snare and cut it carefully until all the flesh is removed.
If the blockage is deep within the ear, take a delicate, smooth millet stalk, heat it slightly in fire, and insert it into the ear canal. If you feel the blockage and the patient experiences hearing difficulty, gently push it further.
Be careful not to injure the eardrum. Then place a wick in the ear, sized to the width of the opening and coated with Egyptian ointment. Continue this treatment for several days until you are sure the wound has healed. Alternatively, soak a wick in water and sprinkle powdered glass over it.
Use this method; if you see the ear has become swollen and inflamed, remove the wick and replace it with another wick coated with a rose ointment made of clarified butter to soothe the inflammation. Continue treatment until the ear is healed.
If bleeding occurs, immerse a sponge or cloth in cold water and place it on the ear, and use all the treatment that stops the mentioned bleeding in the article on catarrhs.
Chapter Eight.
In the treatment of warts appearing on the eyelids.
These warts appearing on the eyelids may be moist or dry, so they should be grasped with forceps or a snare and cut at their base with a scalpel; then powdered lapis lazuli should be applied to the area.
If blood flows from it, cauterize it with a mild lentil-sized iron after cutting; and it is best to cauterize after cutting. It often recurs if any part of its root remains. And if cauterized with fire, those roots will be burned and will not return.
Chapter Nine.
In the treatment of ophthalmia appearing on the eyelids.
Often, something resembling a cold in severity and stiffness appears on the eyelids.
It is called ophthalmia because it involves a gathering of thick moisture in the upper and lower eyelids. If the inflammation is raised on the surface of the eyelid and moves to all sides, its treatment is easy: make a horizontal incision across it.
Then carefully peel it off from all sides until it is removed, then hang it with a snare and cut it off. If you cannot cut it except after penetrating the eyelid, this will not harm the patient. If the incision is large, bring it together with stitches and treat it until it heals.
If it is small, there is no need to worry because the ointment will bind and cauterize it. If the inflammation is inclined inward toward the inner surface of the eyelid, turn the eyelid over and hang the inflammation with a snare without needing an incision, and cut it off from all sides.
If you cut the eyelid, it will not harm anything; then wash the eye after cutting the bandage with salt water and treat the area with something that promotes healing until the patient recovers.
Chapter Ten.
In the treatment of a stye that appears on the eyelid.
A stye is a boil that forms in the layers of the upper eyelid and it most often affects boys. It weighs down their eyes and makes them prone to colds at night, and they are unable to look at sunlight. So that tears flow quickly to them.
That is why you see them leaning on their faces all the time and sleeping on them, with their eyelids wet under their eyebrows, swollen, and the stye clearly visible. When you press the area with your fingers, you feel the stye between them. The procedure is to have the patient rest his head in your lap.
Then take a linen cloth and prepare a wick from it, making a circle out of that wick large enough to surround the stye on all sides. Place it over the stye and press with your fingers on all sides to gather the stye in the center of the circle.
Then cut in the center of that dampness with a sharp scalpel, making a horizontal incision no larger than the one made during bloodletting. As for depth, you should cut through all the skin until you reach the stye.
In most cases, the stye will protrude from the incision in the form of a white lump of fat. Grasp it with a cloth that you have wrapped between your fingers and pull it outwards while turning your hand right and left until it is removed. Be careful not to widen the incision further, lest the scalpel injure the eye.
If the stye does not appear in the first incision, you should gently widen the incision slightly until the stye emerges, then pull it out as we described. Then soak a cloth in vinegar and water and apply it to the area, securing it with a bandage. Some people crush salt instead.
He places it in the depth of the incision to dissolve any remaining moisture, then treats it until it heals; if a hot tumor occurs at the site, he treats it with cooling poultices until it is cured.
Chapter Eleven.
On Varieties of Ophthalmic Cautery.
If a superfluous eyelash grows on the eyelid, outside of the natural course beneath the normal eyelashes, and it becomes inflamed, it will harm the eye and cause various diseases such as constant tearing, relaxation of the eyelids, leukoma, and opacity, to the point that it leads to blindness.
Ophthalmic cautery is performed in four ways: either by burning with fire, or by a caustic drug as previously mentioned in the chapter on cautery; or it may be cauterization by cutting and suturing, or by means of a reed, as I have already stated.
You should place the patient's head in your lap, then turn back the eyelid with your left hand; if it turns back, otherwise insert a needle with thread from below the eyelid and pass the needle with the thread from above, this being near the eyelash itself. Draw the thread upward with the eyelid.
Turn it back with a speculum, then incise within the eyelid, away from the superfluous eyelash, with a dissecting knife, from the larger meatus to the smaller; then draw up the thread and place under the eyelid a small dressing of cotton or linen cloth; then apply cerate to the eyelid.
Like the shape of an ace leaf, except that the shape should be according to the amount you wish to raise the eyelid because it may vary among people; some may need a quantity cut from the eyelid, according to how much the eyelid has relaxed.
And some may need less cut, all according to the relaxation of the eyelid; then incise with a dissecting knife along the lines that you marked, beginning from the larger meatus to the smaller; let each incision be near the natural eyelash, about the width of a speculum.
Then the snare is inserted into one of the angles of the skin, then it is pulled out altogether, and the two lips are brought together with a needle and fine wool thread, and the blood is wiped away. The remaining threads are stuck onto the temples with something adhesive.
If you wish to do so, or not, it does not matter. Then the stitching and threads are left for about three or four days, then it is treated. If you wish, you can leave the wound unstitched and treat it with something that dries and constricts. For when the wound closes and comes together, the eyelid will rise. Stitching is better.
This method of treament, described by those who came before, has some inconvenience for the patient but it is good and without danger. There is another way to treat it which also involves applying a shape on the eyelid like the shape of an as leaf, as we have described. Then the eyelid is lifted with three forceps which are either separate or grouped in this manner:
Then what remains of the eyelid is cut with a small scissors in this manner, making an even cut:
If you cannot hold the forceps steady, take a needle with thread in it and insert it into the center of the shape. Insert another thread near the larger angle and a third thread near the smaller angle. Gather the threads evenly between your fingers and then raise them moderately with your hand.
Raise the entire marked skin as we have described, then bring the edges of the wound together with stitches and treat it until it heals. If there is a hot swelling after cutting or incision, soothe the swelling with cerate and similar soothing ointments.
The lower eyelid may also sometimes turn inwards, in which case the methods we have described for cutting, stitching, cauterization with fire and caustic medicines should be used. Cauterization with fire or a caustic medicine is more effective and less painful for the patient than cutting and stitching. Cutting and stitching are better in both cases.
Cauterization with sugarcane is done as follows: turn the eyelid and make an incision on the inside, as previously described, then make two reeds or thin pieces of wood the length of the eyelid and narrower than a lancet.
Cut the ends of both sides so that the threads can be attached, then gently bring them together on the remaining eyelid and tighten the reeds from both sides. Leave them for several days; the stretched skin will die, turn black, and rot.
It will fall off on its own, but if it is delayed, cut it with pincers and then treat it until it heals. When the wound closes, the eyelid will rise and the eyelashes of the eye will not sag. This is the shape of the reeds, they are made this size in length and width: .
Chapter Twelve.
On removing ingrown eyelashes with a needle.
If it is one hair or two, the procedure is as follows: take a fine needle and thread a very thin smooth silk thread through it. Then gather both ends and tie them in a delicate knot, making the length of the thread about a span long.
Then attach another thin thread to the needle, shorter than the first, and tie its ends if you wish. Have the patient place their head on your lap, preferably near sunlight so that you can clearly see the work, as it is delicate. Insert the needle with the threads into the base of the ingrown hair.
Quickly push it through the eyelid until it comes out above the natural hair, then pull the thread upward and insert into the needle that ingrown hair - if there is only one or two or at most three.
Then pull your hand so that the hair comes out with the needle in the eyelid along with the natural hair. If you pull the needle and the hair doesn't come out, pull the needle down with the thread you attached to it until the needle comes out of the upper hole.
Then return the hair to it and pull them, do this until it rises in the upper hole and you see it with your eye. Then loosen the tweezers and the thread and take them out, tighten the eyelid and leave it tightened for two or three days so that the hair settles in its place and flesh grows on it.
If the hair is short, then either add a long hair from natural hair to it so that it rises with it, or leave it until it grows longer after a while, then lift it.
Chapter Thirteenth.
In the Treatment of a Stye That Occurs in the Upper Eyelid.
The stye that it is subject to is called an arnebia, and this stye can be either natural or accidental; the accidental one is from a bruise of a wound, a scratch, or something similar.
The way to treat it is to cut that bruise and separate its edges, and put a wick of linen between them and tie it so that it heals. And it should not be treated with things that dry and constrict.
For if you do that, the stye will return worse than it was. Rather, use things that soften it, such as fenugreek and a poultice with water in which henna has been boiled, flaxseeds, and diachylon ointment melted with some oil, and apply it to the wick and treat it with that.
The goal of its treatment is to try by all means to prevent it from healing in the shape it was originally in, and this work that we mentioned is only some improvement of the stye, not returning the place to its original state completely.
Chapter Fourteenth.
In the Treatment of a Stye That Occurs in the Lower Eyelid.
This stye, which is from below, is truly called a hordeolum and can be either natural or accidental; the accidental one comes from a wound, a scratch, a burn, or something similar.
The way to work with it is to take a needle with a double thread and insert it into the flesh, passing it from the left angle to the right angle until the thread is at both ends of the flesh, then stretch the flesh upwards with the needle and cut it with a wide spatula.
If the shape of the eyelid returns to what is proper, otherwise take a ligature and place it on the site of the incision, turn the eyelid with it and make two incisions on the inside of the eyelid, the ends of which are from the angles of the cuts that were made until they meet, so that an angle is formed from them. Until when they come together it becomes shaped like this:
And it is the Greek letter lambda, then remove that flesh to the extent that the sharp side of it is below what follows the eyelid, then gather the scattered parts with two stitches sew it with wool thread, then treat it with the softening and relaxing medicines we mentioned until it heals.
And if the scar appeared from a cut, stitch or burn, it should be cut in a simple incision without the hair of the eyebrows as mentioned before, then separate between the lips with a twist as we mentioned.
In conclusion, in the treatment of a scar whether it is from above or below, you should carry out the work according to what seems suitable to you from the shape of the scar, for it may be very different in appearance, and the skilled craftsman manages by any means possible.
Until the shape returns to its natural form or approaches it, and the same disease indicates to you what is suitable for it from work and tools in most cases.
Chapter Fifteenth.
On the adhesion of the eyelid to the conjunctiva or cornea.
This adhesion may occur to many people, the upper eyelid adhering to the white of the eye preventing it from moving easily and hindering it from its natural functions, so you should insert the end of a ligature under the eyelid.
Raise it upward or extend it with a string, then cut the adhesion with a fine scalpel, which should not be as sharp as other scalpels but rather blunt so as not to alarm the patient during the operation. The cutting should be done gently as if peeling away that adhesion.
Wait until the eyelid returns to its natural shape and the adhesion is completely healed. Then pour saline water into the eye to wash it, or drip a solution of Indian frankincense in water. Afterward, separate the eyelid from the eye with a linen wick.
Place a bandage soaked in egg white over the eye. After the third day, use powdered frankincense until it is healed.
Chapter Sixteen.
On Cutting the Veil and Removing Flesh from the Eyelids.
The veil comes in two types: either nervous, resembling a thin, firm membrane; or non-nervous, resembling a solid white moisture that breaks apart when touched by iron or grasped with a string.
Neither type can be grasped with a string. Both types begin at the larger eyelid and gradually extend until they cover the pupil, blocking light and eye movement. To remove it, have the patient rest their head in your lap.
Then have them open their eyes and lift the eyelid with your hand. Grasp the veil with a slightly curved string and pull it upward. Take a needle, thread a horsehair or cow hair through it along with a strong thread, and bend the tip of the needle slightly. Insert the needle into the middle of the veil.
Push the needle through and tie the thread to the veil, pulling it upward. Peel the side of the veil next to the pupil with the hair as if spreading it to the end, then cut off the rest at the base of the eyelid with a fine scalpel or small scissors. Leave the natural flesh of the eyelid.
So that there should not be a continuous flow of tears from the part cut, the ligature may be applied with thread alone, or with thread and needle as we have said, then the end is removed with a smooth scalpel.
Be careful that the scalpel does not touch the corneal membrane, lest a perforation occur and the pupil be injured; then after the incision, place in the eye some powdered salt or zanzibar gum, and leave it for a day, then treat it as is appropriate until it heals.
If the chalazion is not nervous and you are unable to insert a needle into it or secure a ligature, this type of chalazion is often treated with medication and heals as described in the section. If you wish to excise it, open the patient's eye.
Take a delicate, smooth scalpel with a fine edge, like this: Carefully pare the chalazion from above with this scalpel. If you see that it is loosening and breaking down, and the paring has only affected it superficially, apply a poultice to the eye of zanzibar gum or red gum, or powdered salt, and bandage the eye for a day.
Then repeat the procedure until it is all gone, unless you encounter a hot swelling in the eye, in which case leave it and treat the hot swelling until it heals, then resume paring until it is healed. As for the fleshy growth behind…
If the growth is causing severe pain to the eye, suspend it with a ligature and cut off some of it. Do not overcut lest there be a continuous flow of tears. Then apply red gum or zanzibar gum to the socket until it heals.
Chapter Seventeenth.
On Excision of Pterygium and Removal of Excess Flesh in the Eye.
Sometimes, red flesh grows in the eyes of some people until it covers the pupil or comes close to it or spills over onto the eyelids. Sometimes the eyelids turn outward and resemble a pomegranate flower.
The procedure is to place the patient's head in your lap, then open their eyes and grasp all of that red flesh with suitable forceps.
Or you may hold it with a forceps or tweezers, then cut the flesh one by one until it is all gone, preserving the eye so as not to injure it while working, and your cuts should be either with the scalpel we described for cutting the membrane, or with small scissors. Like those used to pick up splinters, as its image will appear in the following chapter.
Once you have finished cutting and all the pterygium is gone, fill the eye with pounded salt or instill a drop of red ochre, or a similar caustic medicine into it, and apply cotton soaked in egg white externally to the eye to prevent hot swelling.
But if some pterygium remains, and blood overwhelms you, and you fear hot swelling, leave the eye and treat it with what calms the swelling, then return to work on it until it is healed. Do the same for excess flesh of this type which appears in the eye.
However, you should avoid treating any weak eye, either by nature or by appearance with iron instruments, and do not put your hand into anything of these operations until you see that the organ can tolerate it.
Chapter Eighteen.
On Removing Splinters from the Eye.
Splinters are red vessels that spread across the eye, preventing vision and weakening it over time. Therefore, you should first look and see if the eye containing the splinters is strong and has no other disease besides the splinters.
Then, the extraction is as follows: Have the patient place their head in your lap, then grasp those vessels with one or two forceps according to your skill. The forceps should be fine and bendable in this manner:
Or there may be two double sockets in one body like this:
Then take fine forceps and gently grasp those vessels, wiping away the blood from time to time until you see that the eye has gone dark from those vessels and the blood has clotted, and preserve the eye so that it is not harmed by the tips of the forceps, and let your work be done in broad daylight facing the sun.
Be very steady in your work so as not to cut anything other than those vessels, and when you are finished cauterize the eye with green or red vitriol, so that its sharpness consumes what remains of the vessels; if you cannot grasp it all at that time, then bandage the eye with something that relieves eye pain.
Leave it for days until its pain subsides and the hot swelling is gone, then repeat the work on it in the same way until this image of the forceps' pain is removed from it.
Leave it [with the head of the patient resting in the student's lap, the last one drying the tears and ensuring vision in the front and back of the eye and you] the main principle in the work is to open the eye with fixed or triangular openers held by a student.
Concentrate, with the head of the patient resting in the student's lap, the last one drying the tears and ensuring vision at the front and back of the eye; and you have the option to begin by grasping from above the eye or below it, the first being to dry two sockets on the upper side, one at the end of the iris and the other at its beginning.
Grasp them between the middle finger and index finger, then attach forceps from underneath opposite them, then grasp them between the ring finger and little finger and lightly lift your hand until you see that all the vessels have risen; cut them with the smallest scalpel if you wish, or with a fine probe.
Thread the needle and hurry to lift all the vessels until they rise above the conjunctiva, do not cut the conjunctiva because tears and blood will obscure your vision.
Then cut off with scissors what exceeds the crown above and below so that nothing remains projecting except in the cornea. If you can lift it from the cornea, remove it with a forceps, for this is best. It may become necessary in some people.
And if you cut it, it will be shaved for a few days until the pain subsides and the hot swelling disappears, then resume work on it.
Chapter Nineteenth.
On Returning the Feather to the Nose.
Physicians call the feather a "nasora" if they treat it by cautery or with a sharp, burning medicine as previously described, and it does not heal. There is no recourse but to incise the swelling when ripe and extract all the moisture or pus within it.
Until the bone is exposed. And if, when the bone is exposed, you see decay or blackness in it, scrape it with an instrument of this form:
It is called a "khashna ra's" (rough head) and is made of Indian iron. Its head should be round like a button, engraved with a fine pattern by a rasp or an eskelfajj. Place it on the decayed part of the bone and turn it between your fingers while pressing your hand slightly until you know that the decay has been removed.
Repeat this process several times, then bind the area with drying astringent medications. If the area closes and flesh grows there, the discharge ceases for forty days and does not recur or cause any incident.
Then know that it has healed. Otherwise, there is no recourse but to return the nasora to the nostril in this manner: expose the bone again with iron or a caustic medicine. Once the bone is exposed, take a speculum of this form:
The tip of the iron should be triangular, and its handle conical, tapering towards the end as you see. Place it on the bone itself, near the cartilage and keep your hand further away from the eye. Then turn your hand with the speculum until the bone is pierced and the patient feels it.
If he finds the wind coming out, when he presses his hand on his nose, then let him compress the spot with astringent drying medicines as I have taught you. And if flesh grows and the place hardens, then the substance which was protruding outwards will return to the nose, and this will be easier for the patient God willing.
Chapter Twenty.
Returning a Protruding Eye.
If the eye protrudes in its entirety and there is no injury or impairment to vision, then you should ease the patient, then dissect them in the cephalic region, then apply a ligature to the nape without fail and suck gently.
Then place on the eye a poultice made from acacia, frankincense, myrrh and asafetida, then tighten the eye over the dressing with many bandages. The recipe for the bandages is to take soft linen cloths, fold each cloth four times, making many of them.
Then place them one on top of the other according to what the eye needs, and tighten them with a strong, firm cloth.  Repeat the ligature with suction without fail, then remove the ligature and leave the eye tightened for a day and a night.
If the bandage relaxes during this time, it should be tightened again. Then remove the bandage after a day and a night as we have said. If you see that the eye has returned, well and good, otherwise repeat the poultice, bandages, tightening and ligature until it returns.
Chapter Twenty-One.
On Excision of a Chalazion.
If a protrusion occurs in the conjunctival layer, then swells and protrudes outside the eyelids like an unripe grape, disfiguring the appearance of the person, and you wish to excise it, proceed as I describe.
This is done by inserting a needle into the base of the chalazion from below upwards, then inserting another needle with a doubled thread from the side and passing it through, leaving the first needle in place.
Then cut the place where the double thread bends and tie some linen around the needle and tighten it well, then remove the needle and put wet wool on the eye with egg white and leave it until the threads fall off with the linen, then treat the eye with something that strengthens it.
Until the wound is healed, and you may tighten the linen from all sides with a circle of cloth so that the eye increases in size, well, then apply a thin bistoury which reaches the depth of the eye so that the white moisture flows and the eye descends and softens in its place, then tighten it until it is healed.
Chapter Twenty-Two.
In the Treatment of Cataracts.
This condition, called cataract, is a turbidity that gathers in the eye resembling descending water, but it is not so. The procedure for this involves having the patient sit upright on a chair, then taking their head with your hands on both sides.
Move it until you see the turbidity move downward with your eye, then fix it so that it does not return, and light will be released and the patient will see things as they used to. If the turbidity does not descend, we know it is water. If the descent of the turbidity is not prepared as mentioned,
otherwise, seat the patient before you, then take a thin bistoury and make an incision above the corneal membrane slightly at the junction of the suspensory ligament with the corneal membrane in the limbus, until the material is expelled.
Then, if a cataract forms in the eye, pour hot water mixed with honey or water boiled with fenugreek and honey into it, then treat it with all usual treatments until it is healed.
Chapter Twenty-Three.
We have mentioned the types of water in the classification and which one is suitable for removal, explained in detail. Take it from there according to what is correct. When this is done, the patient should sit squarely before you facing the light near the sun.
You connect his sound eye and bind it tight, then lift the eyelid of the affected eye with your left hand if the watery eye is the left one, or with your right hand if the watery eye is the right one.
Then you place the tip of the trephine near the limbus with the thickness of a millet seed in the same white part of the eye from the side of the smaller pupil, then you push the trephine forcefully while turning it with your hand until it penetrates the white part of the eye and you feel the trephine has reached something empty.
The trephine should go to a depth equal to the distance from the pupil to the last part of the blackness, which is the limbus of the eye, for copper is seen by the observer in the same way as an eye due to the clarity of the corneal membrane; then you turn the trephine upward to the place where the water is.
Then you press it down repeatedly, and if the water descends from its passage, the patient will see what has been opened before him with the trephine still in his eye, then he rests for a while; if the water ascends, lower it again without removing the trephine.
If it becomes stable and does not ascend, carefully remove the trephine while twisting it slightly with your hand; then dissolve a little bit of pure rock salt in water and wash the eye from the inside; then place a compress or damp cotton wool over the entire outside of the eye.
Anoint it with rose oil and egg white, and bind the sound eye with it; as for us and people of our time, we put powdered cumin mixed with egg white on it. If the trephine does not enter the eye because of its hardness (since some people have very hard eyes), you should take the scalpel called a "briquet", which is pictured as follows: .
Pierce the same area with it, but do not dig deep, just lightly touch it so that the trephine can enter as we have described; and when you complete your work, prepare a firm bed for the patient to lie on his back in a dark room.
He must abstain from all movements and coughing, and his food should be what softens his nature. He should not move his head to the right or left at all. The bandage remains as it is until the third day, then it is loosened in that dark room, and his sight is tested.
Show him things, then return the bandage to the seventh day. This should not be done during treatment or after the hour has passed, but it should be avoided before water ascends quickly with intense looking.
If a hot swelling occurs, the eye should be loosened before the seventh day and this is remedied with what soothes the swelling until it subsides, at which point his sight is released and a veil is placed over his face to train his sight from beneath it for days while he is in that dark room.
He gradually leaves the house and acts according to his reasons. Know that a learner cannot do without observation in this procedure, so when he has done so many times he proceeds with the work. I have heard from some Iraqis that one of them said that he makes in Iraq a hollow aspirator which absorbs water.
I have not seen anyone in our country who has made this, nor have I read it in any book of the ancients. It may be something new. This is a picture of different types of aspirators so that he can recognize them.
It is made of copper, especially with a tip shaped like this, triangular at the end and sharp.
Chapter Twenty-Fourth.
On the Treatment of Growing Flesh in the Nose.
Various types of excess flesh may grow in the nose, some resembling many-legged scorpions and others being cancerous, hard flesh the color of rust, and still others soft flesh not the color of rust.
For any of these soft fleshes that are not malignant or cancerous, the patient should sit before you facing the sun. Open their nostril and insert a snare into the flesh, then draw it out. Cut off what was grasped with a delicate sharp scalpel from one side only.
Until you know that all the flesh has gone, if there remains something which you cannot cut, scrape it off gently with one of the fine instruments so that nothing remains. If blood overcomes you or a hot tumor appears.
Meet it with what is appropriate, or if it is one of the malignant tumors, hasten to scoop it out so that the blood stops and all the flesh goes, then after cutting, put in the nose vinegar and water or wine; if the nose opens and moisture flows from it to the throat.
Know that it has been cured, if the moisture does not flow as it should, know that inside there is fresh flesh on top of the loose bones to which the instrument did not reach in cutting, then you should take a thread of linen somewhat thick.
Tie many knots in it, making the distance between each knot the width of a finger or less. Let the patient insert one end of the thread into his nose with a needle or whatever is possible after fashioning it like a spur, and pull on its thread until it reaches the cartilage.
And it comes out on his throat; often boys in schools do such a thing, and it is easy for whoever wants to do it. Then gather the ends of the thread, the one that came out of the mouth and the one that remained in the nose.
Then use the knots in the thread to spread the flesh, do this until you know that the flesh has been cut by the knots of the thread, then remove the thread and after cleaning the blood put a wick which has been soaked in Egyptian ointment into the nose, do this for three days or more.
Until the ointment eats up all that remains of the flesh, and the last thing in the nose is a lead tube for some days until it heals. If it needs drying treatment, use that.
This is an image of a syringe with which ointments and medicines are dropped into the nose:
He places one made of silver or copper, resembling a small lamp, open-mouthed and with its passageway similarly shaped.
If you prefer, make the tube closed like a reed, and the syringe plunger flat.
It has a handle at its end as you see, which is held when fat or whatever juices or liquid substances are heated in it.
Chapter Twenty-Fifth.
On Plant Warts at the Tip of the Nose.
A wart often grows at the tip of the nose, it enlarges and increases with the days until its appearance is unsightly.
Therefore, it should be cut at its first appearance and its entirety extirpated, then cauterization or a burning medication that acts in place of cauterization should be applied to the area. If cutting it is delayed until it grows large, examine it; if it is hardened, stone-like in color and insensitive.
Do not apply a hot iron to it, for it is a cancerous tumor; I have often seen this tumor cut out and the patient suffer greatly as a result. If the tumor is soft to the touch and not stone-colored, and you see that it can be cut out entirely. Empty the wart and cut it without hesitation or fear, then treat the area with something that dries and constricts until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Sixth.
On Sewing the Nose, Lip, and Ear. If the connection is severed due to a wound or something similar, know that when a separation of connection occurs in one of these cartilages, little can be done.
Therefore, when one of these occurrences presents itself, examine if the wound is fresh and bloody; then gather the lips of the wound with stitches and treat it until it heals. If the separation has completely divided the lips and each side has become whole, you should flay each side with the thickness of its outer skin until it bleeds.
Then the lips are brought together with sutures and tightened, and myrrh and frankincense, powdered, are sprinkled over them. A plaster of palm balm or another kind of emollient ointment is placed on top and it is left tight for two or three days, then it is loosened.
The dressing is changed and left until the stitches fall out on their own, then it is treated with ointment until it heals. The method of suturing is to bring together what was separated, either with needles as we described in abdominal suturing, or with thread as we explained there.
Chapter Twenty-Seven.
On the Removal of Nodes Appearing on the Lips.
Many people have small, hard tumors inside their lips, some resembling caraway seeds, others smaller or larger. The lip should be turned over and each nodule incised and ligated with a snare.
It should be cut from all sides, then the area after cutting packed with powdered borax to stop the bleeding, and gargled with vinegar and salt. The areas are treated with astringents until the wounds heal.
Chapter Twenty-Eight.
On the Excision of Excess Flesh from the Gums.
It often happens that excess flesh grows on the gums, called by the ancients "epulis." It should be ligated with a snare or grasped with forceps and cut at its base. The wound is allowed to bleed for a time, then powdered borax is applied to the area.
Or one of the dried astringent herbs. If the flesh returns after treatment, which it often does, it should be cut again and cauterized, for it will rarely return after that.
Chapter Twenty-Nine.
On the Extraction of Teeth with Iron Instruments.
Rough, unsightly scales may collect on the surfaces of the teeth both internally and externally and between the gums. They may blacken, yellow, and even green until decay reaches the gum and the teeth become unsightly.
The patient should sit between your hands with his head in your lap. Expose the molar and tooth on which you see the scales and something resembling sand, removing it entirely. Do the same for blackness, greenness, yellowness, and anything else until it is purified.
If all the scaling is removed in the first scraping, well and good; otherwise repeat the scraping on another day, a second and a third time until you reach your goal. Know that molars require various types of scrapers, different in shape and form according to what suits your work.
For the scraper used to remove scaling from inside a molar is different from the one used for the outside, and the one used between molars has yet another shape. These are several types of scrapers you should have prepared:
Chapter Thirty.
On the Extraction of Teeth.
You should treat the molar for its pain by every means and delay extracting it, for there is no replacement for it if removed because it is a noble entity, even if extraction becomes necessary.
If the patient insists on extraction, you should make sure that the molar's pain subsides before proceeding. Often, the patient is deceived by pain and believes it originates from a healthy molar, so he extracts it; but the pain does not subside until the diseased molar is also removed. We have seen barbers do this repeatedly.
When the molar's pain subsides completely, you should then make an incision around the tooth with a strong forceps until the gum is loosened on all sides; then move it with your fingers or delicate pliers, first a little, then a little more, until it becomes loose.
Then you will be able to grasp the two large canines firmly, and the patient's head should be between your knees so that he cannot move. Then pull the tooth straight up so as not to break it; if it does not come out, take one of those instruments and insert it under it from every side gently, rocking it back and forth as you did at first.
And if the tooth is decayed or loose, you should fill that cavity with a rag and pack it down well with the end of a thin probe so that it does not fall apart when you pull on it with the forceps; you should also thoroughly examine the gums all around.
Take care not to break it, lest some of it remain and cause the patient more trouble than his initial pain. And beware of doing what ignorant barbers do in their trade, rushing to extract it without using what we have described.
They often inflict great evils on people, the least of which is breaking a tooth and leaving all or part of its roots behind. Or they may even extract some of the jawbone, as we have seen many times. After extraction, rinse with a drink or vinegar and salt. If bleeding occurs from the site, which often happens, then crush some alum and apply it to the area.
If the alum doesn't help, cauterize it. The figure of the delicate forceps with which you move the tooth first has long arms and a short handle, thick so that it does not bend when you grasp the tooth with it. And this is the image of the large forceps:
As you see, it has thick handles so that when you grasp them they do not yield or bend; the arms are short. Let it be made of Indian iron or tempered steel with well-fitted tips, and let there be teeth at the end which interlock for a firm and tight grip.
And limbs may be made like a rasp, and they will also be strong in control.
Chapter the Thirty-First.
On the Extraction of Roots of Teeth and Removal of Broken Jawbones.
If, after the extraction of a tooth, a root has broken off, it is necessary to place cotton wool soaked in butter on the spot for a day or two until the area relaxes; then insert a probe or forceps into it. Which resembles a bird's beak and is called pliers, which are pictured:
It should be made like a rasp from the inside or like a scalpel. If it does not come out with these pliers, you should dig around the root and expose all the flesh with a spatula; then insert an instrument that resembles a small chisel, which is pictured:
Short and slightly thick at the end, and not sharpened lest it break. If the root comes out with this, well and good; otherwise, use these other instruments which are pictured: the first is triangular at the end and somewhat thick.
A picture of another delicate triangle:
We may also use this two-pronged instrument, which is pictured:
And other instruments and tools that have been mentioned previously in the removal of teeth. We may also use this instrument which resembles a large hook, as pictured:
Triangular at the end, curved and somewhat thick so as not to break, and not sharpened. Know that dental instruments are many, as are all other instruments; a skilled craftsman can invent for himself instruments according to what work dictates and the diseases themselves suggest.
For there are diseases for which the ancients did not describe instruments due to the variety of their types. If a bone of the jaw or one of the bones of the mouth has broken or decayed, search for it in its place with what is suitable from these instruments and pliers that have been mentioned for extracting roots.
And we seek aid in its likeness:
Let there be some slight thickness to fix the bone so that it does not slip until the bone comes out, and we immobilize the place with appropriate medicines. If there is a slight putrefaction in the bone, remove its pus and discoloration until it is purified, then treat it until it heals.
Chapter Thirty-Two.
On Spreading Supernumerary Teeth.
If supernumerary teeth erupt in an unnatural direction, they disfigure the appearance, especially if this occurs in women and delicate individuals. Therefore it should be examined: if the tooth has erupted behind another molar.
And if it cannot be spread or pushed back, extract it. If it is attached to another molar, cut it with this instrument, which has this shape:
It resembles a small beak and should be made of very sharp Indian steel.  The cutting should be done over many days due to the hardness of the tooth, so as not to disturb other teeth. But if it is a protruding tooth capable of being pushed back, push it back with a dental brace which has this shape:
It should be entirely made of Indian steel, and its working part should be very finely engraved like the file used to make needles. Push the tooth back a little at a time over many days gently so as not to loosen it and cause it to fall out. Then smooth it out last and remove some of it with scrapers.
If a molar has broken off and is hurting the tongue when speaking, it should also be filed down so that the roughness of the break goes away and it becomes level and smooth, so as not to injure the tongue or spoil speech.
Chapter Thirty-Three.
On Fixing Loose Teeth with Silver or Gold Wires.
If the old molars become loose and move after a blow or fall, and the patient is unable to chew anything for fear they will fall out, and treatment with astringent medicines has not been successful.
The trick is to tighten it with a thread of gold or silver, and gold is better because silver tarnishes and rots after a few days, while gold remains as it is forever and is not affected by that. The thread should be medium in fineness and thickness, according to what fits between the molars.
The image of the weaving is to take the thread and insert its bend between the two sound molars, then weave with the ends of the thread between the moving molars, one or more, until the weaving reaches the sound molar on the other side.
Then you redo the weaving to the side where you started and tighten your hand gently and wisely so that it does not move at all, and your tightening of the thread is at the roots of the molars so that it does not come loose, then cut the excess ends of the thread with scissors, gather them and twist them with a ligature and hide them between the sound molar and the moving molar.
So that it does not injure the tongue, then you leave it tightened as long as it remains. If it comes undone or breaks, tighten it with another thread so that he enjoys it this way forever, and this is a picture of the molars and the weaving in two sound molars and two moving molars, as you see: .
And it may happen that one or two molars return to their place after they have fallen out and interlock as we described and remain so. This is only done by a skilled craftsman, who may carve bone from the bones of oxen and make it in the shape of a molar and put it in the place from which the molar has gone. And he tightens it as we said, so it stays and he enjoys it.
Chapter Thirty-Four.
On Cutting the Frenulum Under the Tongue Which Prevents Speech.
This frenulum under the tongue may be either natural, with which a person is born, or accidental, from a wound that has healed.
The operation consists of opening the patient's mouth and placing his head in your lap, then raising his tongue and cutting the nerve band across so that the tongue is released from its hold. If there is some stiffness and complexity due to a wound contraction, insert a trocar into it and cut it across until the band is severed and the knot dissolves.
Be careful that the incision is not deep in the flesh, lest you cut an artery there and cause bleeding. Then rinse after the cut with rose water or vinegar and cold water. Then place a linen wick under the tongue for the patient to hold in his mouth every night.
So that it does not adhere again. If bleeding occurs, apply powdered alum to the area. If the bleeding is severe, cauterize the area with a lentil-shaped iron suitable for this purpose. Then treat it with all other remedies until it heals.
Chapter Thirty-Five.
On Removing a Frog Born Under the Tongue.
It may happen that a tumor resembling a small frog forms under the tongue, preventing the tongue from performing its natural function and possibly growing until it fills the mouth. The operation consists of opening the patient's mouth facing the sun and examining the tumor.
If you see that it is dark red or black, hard, and the patient does not feel it, do not interfere with it because it is cancerous. If it tends toward whiteness and has moisture, insert a trocar into it and cut it with a fine scalpel, freeing it from all sides.
If bleeding becomes excessive while you are working, apply powdered alum to it until the bleeding stops, then return to your work until you remove it completely. Then rinse with vinegar and salt.
Then treat it with all appropriate remedies until it heals.
Chapter Thirty-Six.
In the Treatment of Tonsil Abscesses
And whatever may grow in the throat of other tumors, glands may appear inside the throat that resemble those appearing externally, called tonsils; if you treat them with what I have mentioned in the division and they do not heal.
Therefore, observe if the tumor is hard, dark in color and insensitive; do not expose it to the iron. And if it is red in color and its origin is thick, do not expose it to the iron either for fear of bleeding; rather leave it until it matures. Then either you may poultice it or it may burst on its own.
If it is white, round, and its origin is delicate; this is what should be cut. The procedure is that you look before working to see if its hot inflammation has completely subsided or decreased somewhat.
Then seat the patient facing the sun, his head in your lap; open his mouth and take a servant in your hands to press his tongue down with an instrument like this:
Make it of silver or brass, thin like a knife. When you press the tongue with it and the tumor becomes visible to you, take a snare and insert it into the tonsil, drawing it outward as much as possible without pulling any of the membranes along with it.
Then cut it with an instrument like this, resembling scissors except that its two ends are curved, and the point of each is sharp; made of Indian iron or Damascus steel:
If you do not have this instrument, then cut it with a scalpel like this:
Sharp on one side and blunt on the other. After cutting one tonsil, cut the other in the same manner. Then, after cutting, let the patient gargle with cold water or vinegar and water.
If bleeding occurs, gargle with water in which pomegranate peels or bay leaves or the like astringents have been steeped until the bleeding stops. Then treat him until he is healed. Other tumors besides the tonsils may grow in the throat; cut them as described for cutting the tonsils, similarly.
I treated a woman for a tumor that had grown inside her throat, extending towards the clavicle. It was slightly sensitive and almost blocked the throat. The woman breathed through a narrow passage and it prevented her from eating or drinking water.
She was close to death if she had remained for a day or two, and the tumor had two branches that extended out of her nostrils. I quickly inserted a probe into one of them and pulled it, extracting a suitable piece.
I cut it where I grasped it from the nostril, then I did the same with what protruded from the other nostril. Then I opened her mouth and pressed down on her tongue, inserted the probe into the same tumor and cut away some of it; only a small amount of blood flowed.
The woman's throat opened up and she immediately began drinking water, then she ate. We continued to cut from that tumor repeatedly for a long time, and the tumor would leave behind replacements for what was cut, which lasted a long time for both of us.
I finally managed to cauterize the tumor inside her throat, so it stopped growing. Then I traveled away and didn't know what God had done with her afterwards.
Chapter Thirty-Seven.
On the Excision of a Goiter.
If a cold descends into the thyroid gland and it swells, and it is white and oblong, it is called a column. If it is thick at the bottom and round, it is called a goiter. If treated with what we mentioned in the division (on treating swellings) but treatment is ineffective.
And I saw that the hot swelling had subsided and it was thin, so it should be cut out. But if it were a solid round mass and did not have length, and was bloody or lead-colored, black, or without sensation, then cutting should be avoided.
For it is a marvel for the patient, so if you see it on the form that I mentioned from its whiteness and length, let the patient sit opposite the sun and press her tongue with the instrument previously described, then insert the fishing line into the mole.
Pull it down and cut it with one of the two instruments mentioned for cutting the tonsils. You should not cut more than what exceeds the natural state, for if you cut more, you will harm the voice and speech.
Then, after the cut, use what we described for cutting the tonsils and treat it until it heals. If the patient is afraid of cutting it, you should use a trick to cauterize it without fear or caution. The way to cauterize it is with a sharp medicine, which is to put the patient's head in your lap.
Then press their tongue with the instrument we mentioned, then take some of the sharp water that we mentioned in the chapter on cauterization and knead it into a non-extinguished ember, making it neither too thick nor too thin. Fill the hollow of this instrument with it. This is its form:
The end where you put the medicine should have a hollow like that of a quince spoon, and place the instrument with the medicine on the mole itself. The patient should lie on their side so that the saliva flows out from inside their throat, lest anything descends into their throat and harm them.
Then hold your hand with the medicine as you press it on the mole for half an hour until it turns black and the sting of the medicine subsides. If you wish, you can take some cotton, wrap it around the end of a quince spoon, and soak the cotton in the medicine.
Insert the quince spoon with the cotton into a tube from above until the cotton sticks to the mole. Repeat this several times until you reach the desired cauterization of the mole, then leave it. It will wither and fall off after three or four days. If you need to reapply the medicine, prepare it again.
After cauterization, wipe around the uvula with cotton dipped in butter and dry it. Then gargle with cold water and treat externally with astringents and internally with gargles until it heals. This type of treatment is safer than cutting and free from fear.
Inflammation can also be treated with something gentler than cautery and cutting: treat it with incense in this way. Take frankincense, spikenard, thyme, saffron, savory, chamomile, wormwood, and similar herbs. Gather them all or some of them in a pot and immerse them in vinegar.
Boil the mixture in a pot coated with philosopher's clay, and make a hole in the center of the lid. Place a hollow instrument on top as follows: it is made of silver or copper.
Insert the end with the rose into the patient's mouth so that the steam rises to the inflammation through the tube until the inflammation softens, then repeat this several times until it subsides.  Be careful not to perform this treatment at the very beginning of the swelling.
For it often increases the swelling, but you should only do this when the hot swelling has subsided. If you don't have this instrument, take a reed and attach an eggshell to one end so that the patient's mouth doesn't burn.
Because the eggshell prevents the heat of the steam from burning the mouth, this is a good treatment and safe.
Chapter Thirty-Eight.
On Removing Splinters and Other Objects Lodged in the Throat.
Often a bone, fishbone, or something similar becomes lodged in the throat. You should remove what is visible after pressing down on the tongue with an instrument in sunlight.
To make what is in the throat clear, and for what is not visible to you and has hidden itself in the throat: induce vomiting in the patient before their food has been digested in their stomach. Perhaps the lodged object will come out with the vomit, or the patient may swallow a piece of lettuce or radish root.
Or he swallows a mouthful of dry bread, or takes a piece of soft sea sponge, ties it in a thread and then swallows it; when it reaches the place of the thorn, the thread is pulled quickly, repeating this several times.
Often the thorn or bone will adhere to it and come out; if it does not come out as mentioned, then use an instrument of lead in this form:
It is somewhat thicker than a plumb, and at its end there is a hook with which the patient inserts it into his throat gently while raising his head upward, and he refrains from touching his throat so as not to cause coughing, and pushes the bone or thorn with it, or the doctor inserts it with his hand.
The patient inserting it himself is better because he knows the location of the foreign object and pushes it down or pulls his hand up with the instrument as much as possible until it comes out.
Chapter Thirty-Nine.
On extracting a foreign body lodged in the throat.
If you have treated the foreign body as mentioned in the division on treatment with medication and it has not been successful, then look into the patient's throat toward the sun after pressing his tongue down with the instrument that I have described for you.
If your sight falls upon the foreign body, pull it out with a small fishing line or a delicate pair of forceps; if you are unable to do so, then take a hollow tube and insert it into the patient's throat towards the foreign body, then insert an iron rod heated in fire into the interior of the tube.
Repeat this several times, and the patient abstains from water for the whole day; then he takes a syringe full of cold water, opens his mouth into it and gargles without swallowing a drop and moves the water occasionally with his hand.
For the foreign body will fall out on its own when it feels the water; if it does not come out as we have described, then vaporize the throat with urine and with hellebore by the instrument that I have described in fumigations of the laryngeal area, repeating this several times until it falls out.
The way to use the incense is to take a quantity of it and put hot embers in it, covering the quantity with a lid that has a hole in the middle. Then the end of the instrument is placed into the hole, and the incense is poured in. The patient puts their mouth on the end of the tube and closes it so that the incense doesn't escape until they know it has reached their throat.
If the leech falls on the specified place, then it is done; otherwise, the incense is repeated several times. The patient must endure thirst and eat salt and garlic but not drink water.  The leech will certainly come out with this treatment. This is a picture of an instrument used to attract the leech from the throat once it is seen.
It resembles forceps as you see, except it has this curved part that enters the throat. Its ends resemble a bird's beak and are rough like a file so that when it seizes something, it doesn't let go.
There is another instrument with which the tongue is pressed down. It's a wide iron ring, as large as the mouth, inserted above the tongue and into the mouth, standing on its side. The mouth remains open involuntarily and the patient cannot remove it at all until it is removed.
Description of the instrument with which the mouth is opened to this very extent.
Chapter forty.
It deals with speech concerning the cutting and opening of tumors.
Tumors are of many kinds, diverse in nature, as will be described individually in this book. They differ in their cutting and opening in two ways: one is the type of tumor itself and what it contains.
The second type is according to the places in the body where they occur, because a tumor that occurs in the head is not like a tumor that occurs in the buttocks, and a tumor occurring in a fleshy place is not like a tumor occurring in a joint. Each of them has its own rule for treatment.
Some tumors should not be incised until the pus in them has matured and become complete, while others should be incised even though the pus has not fully matured, such as tumors near joints.
For if a tumor occurs near a joint and its matter lingers until it suppurates, it may corrupt the ligaments or nerves of that joint, thus becoming the cause of lameness in that limb, or the tumor may be near a principal member.
For if you delay incising it until it matures, you will thereby injure that principal member; or it may be near the seat, and you should incise it while immature, for it is necessary to incise a tumor near the seat while the pus is not fully mature so that it does not suppurate and penetrate into the inside of the seat, becoming a danger.
And it becomes to such a degree that it will not heal, and you should know the time for incising tumors that have fully matured, which is when the pain of the tumor subsides, the fever goes away, the redness diminishes, the throbbing ceases, and the head of the tumor softens, along with all other signs.
The incision should be made at the lowest point of the tumor if possible, so that the discharge flows downward more easily, or in the thinnest and most protruding part of the tumor; the incision should run lengthwise in the body if the tumors are on the hands or feet.
Avoid making incisions in the locations of muscles, tendons, nerves, arteries, and in general, in all flat places where there is no bending; as for those that bend, the incision should be made according to that location.  If a tumor is located on fleshy areas, it is best to leave it un incised.
Let it mature completely as we have said; for if you incise it before that, the pus will flow for a long time and will be foul and dirty, and perhaps its edges and cavity will harden. Some tumors may be incised transversely across the body when necessary or according to what the limb requires.
And one should use a single probe for small tumors, and a wide probe or many incisions according to the size of the tumor for large ones. Some tumors may require that the skin be scraped and cut if they have become like cloth.
And it has sometimes happened that a man died as is shown in many dens and lairs, some of which are cut with a three-cornered incision and some of which are cut out in the shape of a playing card like the Arabic ace.
Some use a round incision, some a crescent-shaped incision and so forth for these incisions. If it does not have a head like smooth, flat tumors, then only a simple probe should be used. And if the tumor is large and has collected for a long time, and you have probed it, do not hasten to draw out all the pus at that time.
But draw out some of it, then bind up the tumor until the next day and draw out some pus again. Do this repeatedly gradually until you have drawn out all of it.
Especially if the patient is weak or a pregnant woman or a young child or an old man, for the animal spirit often dissolves with the sudden exit of pus and the patient may die without you realizing it. Therefore, be extremely cautious in this matter.
After probing these tumors, you should wipe the wound and look at it. If the tumor's wound is small or the incision is a single simple one, use a bandage of linen or soft cotton. But if the tumor is large and there are many incisions from the probing
you should insert a wick into each incision so that they reach one another. If some of the skin of the tumor has been cut or scraped off, it should be packed with soft cotton or dry linen bandages and bound until the third day.
Then remove it and treat it with the appropriate ointments until it heals. If bleeding occurs while you are working, use cold water and vinegar after soaking a linen cloth in them and applying it to the bleeding spot several times.
If the bleeding continues, it is necessary to use treatment and remedies which we have described in many places in this book and in the division. If you are in winter and the place of the swelling has many nerves, then you should moisten the bandages with a hot syrup and oil.
You should place them on the area. And if you are in the summer and the places are hot, then you should soak the bandages with water and oil or syrup and oil all of that cold until as we said on the third day you should dissolve the swelling and rub it.
And you should use in its treatment what seems fitting until it is healed. This is what you need of knowledge to treat tumors in general. As for the way of detail, I have mentioned how to treat every tumor summarized and classified.
Chapter Forty-One.
On the incision for tumors that appear on the scalp.
Small tumors appear on the scalp, and they are of a kind that contains sheaths like a chicken gizzard, and their types are many: some are fatty and some contain moisture that resembles slime.
Some contain moisture that resembles broth and soup, and so on. Some are petrified and solid. All of them are not dangerous to incise and remove unless you encounter an artery while incising.
The procedure in incising it is to first probe it with the instrument that will come after this, which is called a speculum, so that you know what it contains. If what it contains is moisture, then incise it lengthwise in a simple incision like this:
Begin the incision from line ~b~ to line ~j~. Once the moisture has drained, peel off the sac that contained that moisture and cut it all out, leaving nothing at all behind. This is because it often recurs if anything is left.
Then dip cotton in the small ointment if available, otherwise in salt water, and fill the wound with it, leaving it until the next day. It will eat away what remains of the sac. Then apply cotton with Egyptian resin again a second and third time if needed.
Until you are sure that nothing of the sac remains, then treat the wound with ointments until it heals. If the tumor contains a fatty substance, incise it in this manner:
Make a firm incision and hold the edges of the wound with forceps, extracting it from all sides. Strive to remove the peritoneum that contains it. If you encounter an artery, do as I have previously described for you. Do the same for tumors if they are hardened from incision.
The treatment is the same as mentioned, and the incision on a hardened tumor is easier because it has little blood and moisture. Some of these tumors in the head of some people have no moisture at all. This was the case when I incised a tumor...
on the head of an old woman, and I found the tumor hard like a solid, rough, white stone that could not be broken even if someone threw it at something. And none of the other tumors occurring in the head were like this except for the tumors that appear on the heads of boys.
And at the base of the ears, incise them all with a simple incision and make its opening from below so that it is easy for the material to flow downward. Then treat it with the appropriate treatment.
Chapter Forty-Two.
Chapter Forty-Two: On Incisions for Warts
Every cyst has its own peritoneum, just as in boils and tumors of the head, as we have described. There are many types of cysts: some are calcified, some contain fluids, and some are malignant and do not respond to treatment.
I have not seen a cyst in good condition upon palpation, with its appearance close to the color of skin, movable in all directions, not adherent to the neck nerves, jugular vein, or artery, nor was it sunken. It should be incised with a simple cut from above to below the body in this manner, from line ~j~ to line ~b~.
It should be dissected from all sides and the edges of the skin extended with a forceps, or two forceps, or many forceps if needed, as we said for head tumors. It should be extracted little by little, resting on the neck so as not to cut any vein or nerve. The scalpel should not be overly sharp.
