So as not to increase the cutting with your hand or alarm the patient by cutting what is not needed; if you cut a vein or artery and are prevented from working, place powdered alum or some astringents in the wound to stop the bleeding.
Tighten the wound and leave it until the sharpness of the cyst subsides and the wound relaxes, taking care not to allow it to become infected. The bleeding will stop at that time, so return to your work until you have finished emptying it; then search with your index finger if there remain any other small cysts, cut them and remove them.
If there is a large vein at the base of the cyst, it should not be cut from its origin. Instead, it should be ligated with a strong double thread and left until it falls off on its own without harm. Then, pack the wound with soft cotton soaked in Egyptian ointment and treat it accordingly.
If the whole pig's foot is cut off, its lips should be brought together and sewn up immediately after you are sure that there is no remnant at all; but if you see that there is a remnant of bone from the pig's foot,
you should direct the cut to its origin and base and use the stitching as we mentioned. And if any pig's foot contains moisture, you should apply a simple poultice wherever you see it ripening and make the poultice of what is below, as we said.
Then you should use, after the poultice, a bandage with Egyptian ointment or similar to consume whatever remains of corruption, and when you see that the wound has been cleansed, treat it with ointments that promote flesh growth until it heals.
Chapter Forty-Third
On the incision of the throat for a tumor occurring within the pharynx.
The ancients mentioned this incision in the throat, and I have not seen anyone in our country perform it; this is consistent with their speech. But those with angina should avoid incising the throat as they will not benefit from it.
This is because all the vessels and lungs will be diseased, but for those with a hot tumor in the mouth, throat or tonsils, if there is no cause in the trachea, incision of the throat should be used to escape the harm that results from suffocation.
If we desire this, the throat should be incised beneath three or four small incisions in width between two circles as much as the incision is in the peritoneum and not in the cartilage; this location corresponds to the incision.
Because it is devoid of flesh and its blood vessels are far, if the practitioner is timid, he should stretch the skin of the throat with a snare and then incise the skin until reaching the trachea, avoiding blood vessels if he sees any.
Then the peritoneum is incised as we have described, and evidence of incision of the trachea is found in the phlegm that comes out of it with the rattling sound and cessation of voice, and the wound is left open for a while. If the time during which strangulation was feared has passed, the lips of the wound are stitched together with skin.
And it is drawn alone without cartilage, then medicines that promote flesh growth are used until it heals. The author of this book says: the explanation of this statement which we have mentioned is only if they see the patient has blocked their throat with one of these tumors that we mentioned.
And the patient was close to death and intended to die, so they went to cut the throat so that the patient could breathe on the wound for a while and be saved from death, but they were ordered to leave the wound open until the course of the illness is over and its form is three days or so.
At that time, they were ordered to sew up the wound and treat it until it healed. And what I myself have seen is that a servant took a knife and sent it across her throat, cutting off part of the trachea. They called me to treat her, and I found her fading like a slaughtered animal, so I exposed the wound.
I found that the blood coming from the wound was flowing slowly, so I realized that she had not cut an artery or vein and the air was escaping from the wound. So I proceeded to draw the wound together and treat it until it healed, and nothing happened to the servant except a hoarseness in her voice, no more.
And after a few days she returned to her best condition, so we say from here that cutting the throat is not dangerous.
Chapter forty-four.
On incision for the tumor that appears in the pharynx from the outside and is called elephantiasis of the pharynx.
This tumor, called elephantiasis of the pharynx, is a large tumor the color of flesh and it is common in women. It is of two types.
It is either natural or accidental. As for the natural, there is no remedy for it. As for the accidental, it is of two types: one resembling fatty tumors and the other resembling a tumor from the complication of an artery, and danger in its incision. It should not be exposed to iron at all except if it is small, and if you probe and examine it with a sound and find that it resembles a fatty tumor and is not attached to any of the veins, then incise it as you would on tumors. Remove it with its sac if it is in one, otherwise, excise all of it and then treat the site as necessary.
Chapter Forty-Five.
On Incision for Different Types of Tumors.
Tumors are of many kinds and I have mentioned all their types in the classification. We should state here the difference between a tumor and an abscess, as it is confusing. I say that an abscess has heat, fever, and intense pain until the boiling of the pus subsides. The putrefaction then continues, and at that time the fever and pain subside. A tumor is not accompanied by heat, fever or pain and it is contained within a peritoneal sac which is its special envelope. It is the color of the body and begins like a millet seed, growing to the size of a melon and sometimes larger and sometimes smaller.
It is of two types: either fatty or containing moisture. The colors of the moisture are numerous, as mentioned in the classification. If you intend to treat a tumor, first probe and examine it with an instrument called a sound, whose image will be shown in the following chapter.
Determine the size of the tumor, then withdraw the sound and look at what comes out after it. If liquid moisture of any color emerges, incise it with a simple incision as mentioned for other tumors. If no moisture emerges after the sound, know that it is fatty.
Make a straight incision upon it in this manner as I have taught you, and hang it up with hooks.  Peel off the skin from all sides carefully, and if possible preserve the sac with it so that it may come out entire along with its contents.
But if the sac be torn during the operation and you cannot extract it whole, which often happens, take it out piece by piece so that nothing of it remains; for if any part of it be left behind, whether much or little, the contents will more readily return to its former state.
If, however, you are overpowered and a small portion remains, enlarge the wound with some sharp-cutting instruments, draw together the edges of the wound, and apply over it something to allay the hot swelling. Treat it with all suitable remedies until it heals. If the contents are large, cauterize its edges and treat them in a manner that promotes union.
If a throbbing vein, or any other vein, obstructs you and blood flows freely, immediately cauterize the spot with powdered litharge and let it remain constricted for two or three days until the wound becomes gangrenous and the flow of blood subsides; then return to excising what remains of the contents.

Chapter the Forty-Seventh.
In the Treatment of Men's Breasts.
Which resembles women's breasts, sometimes the breast of some people swells during puberty until it resembles a woman's breast, remaining warm and ugly. So whoever dislikes this should make a crescent-shaped incision on the breast in this manner.
From line ~b~ to line ~c~. Then scrape away all the fat, then fill the wound with salted medicine, then bring the edges of the wound together by stitching and treat it until it heals. If the breast tends downward and relaxes due to its size as happens to women.

Chapter Forty-Nine.
On incising the tumor that appears before an artery or vein and is called aneurism.
If the artery is injured and the skin above it heals, a tumor often results. Similarly, a vein can also swell and form a tumor.
The signs that indicate whether the tumor and swelling are before an artery or a vein: if it is before the artery, it will be rectangular and deep in the body. If you press the tumor with your finger, it feels as though it has a pulse.
The tumor before the vein is round and on the surface of the body. Incision into these tumors is dangerous, especially those in the armpit, groin, neck, and many other parts of the body, and if they are large, it should be avoided to treat them with iron.
And if it is in the extremities or head, it should also be avoided. If it is an aneurysm of the artery, cut a lengthwise incision into the skin and then open the incision with forceps. Then dissect the artery and free it from adhesions.
Until it is exposed, then you insert a needle under it and pass it to the other side and tighten the artery with a double thread in two places as you were taught in the case of the two arteries that are in the fingers, then you pierce the place between the ligaments with a scalpel until all the blood in it comes out.
And the swelling dissolves, then you use a treatment that produces pus so that the ligaments fall off, and then treat it with suitable ointments until it heals.
But if the swelling is from a vein incision, you should hold as much of the swelling as possible with your hand along with the skin, then insert a needle under the place you are holding with your hand and pass it through with a double thread so that it comes out on the other side. Chapter Fifty.
On the Swelling Resulting from Nerve Constriction.

Cut the skin with a scalpel, if the tumor is small grasp it with forceps and cut it from the root. If it is large, hang it with a thread and draw it out, then remove it and bring the wound together with stitches and treat it until it heals.
Chapter Fifty-One.
On Excising Warts Appearing on the Abdomen.
Warts often appear on the abdomens and throughout the bodies of some people; they are called fungal warts because of their resemblance to fungi. They have a thin base and a thick head, and some have lips that have transformed. Some are small, while others grow very large.
I once saw a man who had two warts on his abdomen that resembled fungi; they had a thin, white base and their lips had transformed and cracked, with moisture always flowing from them. I excised them both, and one weighed about eight ounces, while the other weighed about six.
The procedure for excising them is as follows: If the affected area is moist and the warts are white, damp, and have a thin base, cut them with a wide scalpel. Keep your forceps heated in a fire, because there is often profuse bleeding when they are cut.
If bleeding becomes excessive, cauterize it immediately. If you see that the affected area is cowardly and afraid of being cut with metal, take a tight lead thread and tie the wart that has this characteristic. Leave it for two days, then tighten the lead further. Continue tightening the lead.
Continue this process until the wart detaches and falls off on its own, without any pain. If the wart has a thick base, there is some danger in excising it, especially if it is located on the abdomen. However, you may be able to cut off half or part of it.
Then cauterize it so that it does not return, then treat it until the wound is healed, and beware of exposing yourself to a cut which is bluish-black in color, insensitive, and foul-looking, for it is a cancerous tumor; I shall mention cancer later on.
Chapter Fifty-Two.
On the Treatment of a Superfluous Growth.
A superfluous growth may result from various causes, either from a rupture of the peritoneum on the abdomen whence the omentum or intestines protrude as occurs in other hernias, or from blood issuing from a vein or artery as previously mentioned, or from wind accumulating therein.
If it results from a rupture of the peritoneum and protrusion of the omentum, the tumor will be of a color similar to that of the body, soft, and painless, and its appearance will vary. If it results from protrusion of the intestines, its position will be even more different than what we have described. If you press it with your fingers, it will disappear and then return, and it may be accompanied by rumbling; it increases considerably when entering a bathhouse and during strenuous exertion. If it is due to moisture, it will be soft.
If it is not due to moisture, it will not disappear when pressed and neither increase nor decrease in size. If it is due to blood, along with these signs, the tumor will appear dark. If it is due to growing flesh, the tumor will be hard, fixed, and of a constant size.
If it is due to wind, touching it will feel soft. The procedure in this case is that you should examine it; if the superfluous growth results from arterial or venous blood, or wind, treatment should be avoided because there is danger and risk involved as I have informed you in the chapter mentioning tumors arising from arteries and veins.
But if the superfluous growth is from the intestines or omentum, you should instruct the patient to hold himself and stand upright and extended, then mark the entire growth with ink. Then order him to lie on his back before you and cut around the tumor with a broad scalpel at the place marked with ink, then extend a large ligature from the middle of the tumor upwards and tie the incision site tightly with a strong thread or silk suture.
The ligature should be tied firmly, then the middle of the drawn tumor is opened above the ligature and your second finger is inserted into it, searching for the intestine; if you find it has been taken by the ligature loosen the tie and push the intestine back inside the abdomen, but if you find a clot extend it with a ligature.
Cut the rest of it, and if a vein or artery obstructs you, tie it firmly, then return to your work. Take two needles and insert strong threads into them, passing the needles into the incision made around the tumor, crossing them; then draw up the tumor in four places on the needles.
If you wish, remove the needles and leave the area until the drawn flesh rots and falls off by itself or cut it off if it becomes soft and rotten, then treat it with appropriate ointments until it heals; but if the tumor grows from newly formed flesh.
Or from moisture, you should bore the tumor as we have said and draw out the blood or moisture that you find there, then treat it with what will heal the area.

It is made of silver, thin, smooth, and hollow like a bird's feather quill, long in the vicinity of one and a half finger lengths. It has a delicate funnel at its head, and the way to draw urine with it is to take a folded thread and tie a wick or cotton securely at its end.

This order is followed because the passage through which urine flows has curves. Then the wick thread is pulled with a slight tension, and the urine follows the wick. It is then removed, and urine flows out freely, and the catheter is reinserted.
This is repeated until the bladder is emptied and the patient feels relief.

Chapter Sixty-One.
On Removing Stones for Women.

You should open it and order her to do all that you order her to do in examining the stone, first of all, to see if the woman is a virgin, then it is necessary for you to insert your finger into her vulva and search for the stone; if you find it, press it down under your finger.
Then you order her to be incised; but if she is not a virgin and is an older woman, order the midwife to insert her finger into the vulva of the patient and search for the stone after placing her left hand on the bladder and squeezing it very well.
If she finds it, it is necessary for her to push it down from the mouth of the bladder downwards as far as she can until it reaches the base of the thigh, then incise her opposite the middle of the vulva at the base of the thigh on whichever side allows her to feel the stone in that area.
Her finger should not be removed from the stone while pressing down on it, and the incision should be small at first; then insert the probe into that small incision. If she feels the stone, enlarge the incision as much as she knows the stone will come out of it. Know that there are many kinds of stones.
Some are small, some large, some smooth, some rough, some long, some round, and some have branches. Know its varieties so that you can deduce what you want. If blood flows freely, sprinkle powdered alum on the spot and hold it for an hour until the bleeding stops, then return to your work.
Until the stone comes out, prepare with yourself the instruments mentioned for removing stones from men so that you can use them in your work. If bleeding is excessive and you know the pulse indicates it is from a severed artery, apply a ligature to the spot.
Tighten it firmly with bandages and leave it; do not disturb it again, and leave the stone in place. You may kill the patient if you remove it. Then treat the wound. When the severity of the bleeding subsides after a few days and the area becomes infected, return to your work until the stone comes out.
Chapter the Sixty-Second.
On Incision for Watery Humor.
Watery humor is a collection of moisture in the white membrane which lies beneath the skin of the testicles surrounding the testicle, and it is called the tunica vaginalis. It may also be in a special membrane prepared by nature on one side of the testicle.
Sometimes it may be mistaken for another testicle, and it is found between the skin of the testicle and the white membrane which we mentioned. This rarely happens, and this humor arises from weakness affecting the testicles, and this material builds up in them. It may also occur after a blow to the testicles.
This moisture comes in many colors: it may be yellowish, reddish, dark purple, or watery and white - the latter being most common. The signs by which it is recognized are...
Where the water collects, if it is in the white membrane we mentioned, the swelling will be somewhat elongated like an egg shape and the testicle itself will not be visible because the moisture surrounds it on all sides.
If the moisture is in a special membrane of its own, the swelling will be round on one side of the testicle.
Because of this, a person might think it is another testicle. If the moisture is between the skin of the testicle and the white membrane, it will be felt under the hand. To determine the color of the moisture, probe the swelling with the square instrument whose image we have previously shown; judge the contents by what comes out after probing.
If we turn to treatment with cautery, the patient should be bled if possible and you see that he is full-bodied; then he should lie on his back on something slightly raised and place many cloths underneath. You should sit to his left.
You order a servant to sit to your right, extending his penis to one side of the scrotum skin and towards the direction of the abdominal cavity; then you take a wide speculum and cut lengthwise through the middle of the scrotum skin, close to the perineum.
The cut should be straight and parallel to the line that divides the scrotal skin in half, until it reaches the white peritoneal sac; then you remove it carefully, avoiding cutting it, and you make your removal from the side that is attached to the testicle more than the other side, and you continue the removal as much as you can.
Then you stretch the water-filled peritoneum with a wide clamp and remove all the water; then separate the edges of the incision with specula, pulling the peritoneum upwards without touching the scrotal skin containing it; and cut the peritoneum as much as possible, either completely or in pieces.
Especially its thinner side, because if you do not remove it completely, you cannot prevent the water from returning; and if the testicle protrudes outside its skin while you are working, then once you have finished cutting the peritoneum, return it to its place and then close the edges of the scrotal skin with sutures.
Then treat it as any other surgical wound until it heals. If you find that the testicle is damaged from another disease, you should tie off the vessels in the spermatic cord to prevent bleeding; then cut the testicle from the spermatic cord and remove the testicle, treating it as we have previously described.
If the collected fluid is on both sides, know that it is hydrocele; so incise the other side as you did the first, and if it seems best to perform one operation, do so; then wipe away the blood.
Introduce into the incisions cotton wool steeped in oil or rose ointment; and place over them another layer of cotton wool steeped in syrup and oil, extending it over the testicles and abdominal cavity; then lay a folded cloth, which is called a "refyad", on top. And it is attached from above by the ligament which has six ends in this manner:
Then two cloths are taken, sewn together and stuffed with blown wool in this manner, and the ends are made from cotton or wet wool, gathered tightly into a trouser belt. One of the ends is taken to the buttocks towards the back.
The second is taken towards the groin, and the two sides on the thighs from below, and the remaining two sides on the armpits, all gathered tightly into a trouser belt. This splint can also be made by cauterization instead of cutting with an iron.
And it is to take a fine, skinny iron and cut the skin of the testicle while it is held as we described until the white peritoneum containing water is exposed. Then take another iron in this manner:
Which resembles a Greek eye, then apply it to the peritoneum while it is held until all the moisture comes out, then stretch the peritoneum with tweezers and peel off the containing layer with the iron and cut it as much as possible so that you eradicate all of it, preserving the testicle from being touched by fire.
Know that this procedure is safer and further from bloodletting and better than the procedure of cutting, for if during the cut or cauterization a hot tumor, bleeding or anything else occurs, you should treat it with whatever remedy suits it as previously mentioned.
But if the patient is cowardly and cannot endure this procedure due to its ugliness, let him use a poultice as I will describe, which, if it is correct according to what we have presented of evidence.
Since the splints are watery, the patient should sit on a high chair and then place the tumor under it with a wide spatula. Make the poultice extend along the length of the body, ensuring a wide opening so that all the water flows out. Then place cotton on the area and tie it.
Let him continue to drain the remaining water and then treat it until the wound heals. If there is difficulty in draining the water when you press it, know that it is because the white membrane obstructs the mouth of the wound and prevents the water from escaping.
Then you should insert a goose feather or the instrument with which water is extracted for lovers, the image of which has been presented, or enlarge the opening of the wound slightly.
Know that water may return and collect after six months or so. If it does collect, press it in the same manner and the patient should rest for a few days.
Chapter Sixty-Third.
On Incision of the Muscular Part and Its Treatment.
Know that incision on these muscles is one of the most dangerous things leading to death in most cases. Therefore, we advise leaving them alone and staying safe from them. I will mention the work done on them and the types of tumors.
I say that many tumors can occur in bodies, composed of the anthrax, and this is due to several causes, either from excess humor flowing into the anthrax, or from a blow. The tumor will be the color of the body and there will be no water in it.
The tumor will be hard and sometimes stony, the color of soot, without any sensation. It may also be due to the complication of arteries and their swelling, as mentioned earlier, or from the swelling of the veins. Any swelling of the arteries can be recognized by the dispersion of the tumor.
If you press it with your fingers, nothing should be done to it. As for that which is from the swelling of the veins, nothing disperses from it when pressed with the fingers. This type may be incised along with all other flesh.
This is done by incising the skin of the scrotum, then extending the testicle upwards and removing it from the white peritoneum.  You should free the spermatic cord from its vessels, tie off those vessels, and cut the spermatic cord after freeing it from all sides of the testicle. If the testicle has adhered to the newly grown flesh...
It is necessary for the egg to be removed and cut open. If the adhesion is between some of the peritoneal membranes or between vessels, it is necessary to free all of that adhesion and cut it in a circular manner.
If the growth of flesh is in the place of adhesion which is behind, it is necessary to cut all of it and remove the egg as we said. When your work is done, dress the wound abundantly with wool soaked in rose oil and wine, then treat it with all other treatments until it heals.
Chapter sixty-fourth.
On the treatment of hernias with a femoral ring.
The femoral hernia is a twisted tumor, somewhat resembling a cluster with relaxation of the ligaments and difficulty for the patient in movement, exercise and walking. In the treatment of this ailment, it is almost the same as what has been previously mentioned.
But it is necessary to mention the work done on it, as was done by those who came before. I say that the patient should sit on a high chair and then push the femoral ligaments downward, then grasp the skin of the scrotum with your fingers along with the vessels that are near the penis.
Have a servant hold it and extend it firmly, then make a wide incision with a sharp scalpel in a direction parallel to the vessels until the vessels are exposed. Then dissect from every side as I have described to you regarding the arteries in the groin, then insert a needle with a doubled thread into them.
Tie it at the first place where the hernia appeared and also tie it at the last part of it, then make a vertical incision in the middle along the length of the body and remove the foul corrupt humors that have collected there.
Then treat the wound with all the treatments of other surgeries that you want to occur so that the vessels feeding one of the ligaments can fall away. There is no harm in this. If the hernia occurs in relation to all the vessels, it is necessary to remove one of the ligaments along with its vessels so that the testicle is not deprived of nourishment. When vessels are severed they become necrotic and are no longer useful.
Chapter Sixty-Fifth.
On the Treatment of Intestinal Fistulas.
These fistulas occur from a tear that appears in the stretched peritoneum of the abdomen, toward the groins from the navel, where the intestines protrude into one of the two groin areas.
This hernia is either from a tear in the peritoneum or its extension, and these two types occur for many reasons, such as a blow, a jump, a shout, lifting something heavy, and so on. Its sign, if it is from the extension of the peritoneum, is that it occurs gradually over a long period.
It does not happen suddenly and the swelling is level towards the depth because the peritoneum presses on the intestines.  Its sign, if it is from a tear in the peritoneum, is that there is great pain from the beginning, and the swelling appears differently under the skin nearby.
This is due to the intestine protruding and its course outside the peritoneum.  Sometimes, it may protrude with fat, which is called an intestinal-fat fistula. It may also be accompanied by wind, or there may be pus running in the intestine, and it becomes blocked, leading to the patient's death.
Because it causes severe pain and rumbling, especially when pressed. The treatment of these types of conditions with iron is dangerous and should be avoided. The course of action is to instruct the patient to return the intestine into his abdomen with his hand if it attempts to return.
Then he lies on his back between your hands, and raises his legs, then you stretch the skin next to the groin upwards and cut open all of the scrotal skin lengthwise. Then insert specula into the edges of the incision as much as is needed to widen it.
The incision should be as large as the egg can pass through. Then you peel away the peritoneal layers under the scrotal skin until the white, firm peritoneum is exposed on all sides. At that point, insert your index finger into what is next to the egg.
Between the white peritoneum under the tunica albuginea and the second peritoneum, adhesion is released behind the testicle. Then, with the right hand, it is folded inwards into the scrotum skin. At the same time, the left hand stretches the white peritoneum upwards and raises the testicle with the peritoneum towards the incision.
You order the assistant to raise the testicle upwards, and you completely release the adhesion behind it. Then, with your finger, you search carefully to make sure there is no twisted bowel within the firm white peritoneum. If you find any, push it down into the abdomen below.
Then, you take a needle threaded with a thick cord twisted from ten threads and insert it at the end of the peritoneum under the scrotum skin next to the incision. Then, you cut the ends of the folded thread so that there are four threads. Then, you overlap them firmly and tie the peritoneum under the scrotum skin with a tight ligature on both sides. Then, you also wind the ends of the threads and tie them tightly so that nothing from the vessels that nourish it can reach it. This is done to prevent a hot tumor from forming, and a second ligature is made externally, away from the first one by less than two fingers. After these two ligatures, you leave a portion of the peritoneum under the scrotum skin the size of a thumb bone.
This is done to prevent a hot tumor from forming, and a second ligature is made externally, away from the first one by less than two fingers. After these two ligatures, you leave a portion of the peritoneum under the scrotum skin the size of a thumb bone. You cut off the rest entirely in a circular fashion and remove the testicle with it. Then, you make an incision below the scrotum skin that bleeds freely as described previously. Afterwards, cotton wool soaked in oil is applied to the wound, and the ligature we described is used.
The white peritoneum that we mentioned after it is cut may be cauterized carefully to prevent bleeding, as this often occurs. The ligature is left until it falls off on its own. If it delays in falling off, it can be loosened with hot water and something that makes it sweat so that it will fall off.
Then treat the wound with all surgical treatments until it heals.

Chapter Seventy-Sixth.
In the hernia that occurs in the groin.
The hernia in the groin may appear, as we said, where the place is revealed and nothing of the intestine descends into the testicles, and if it descends, it is a little and returns at all times.
But if it lasts for a long time, the rupture in the peritoneum increases so that the intestine and omentum descend into the scrotum, and this appears from the extension of the peritoneum which is in the groin as we said. This is because the peritoneum extends, then relaxes, and the place bulges and protrudes, and its treatment is by cauterization as we have previously described.
And it can be treated with iron in this manner: the patient lies on his back between your hands, then the warm hernia site is incised transversely to the width of three fingers, then the peritoneal layers under the skin are sutured.
Until the white peritoneum that follows it is exposed, then you take a ligature and place it on the site resulting from the peritoneum and press it to the depth of the abdomen, then you stitch the two resulting sites at the ends of the ligature from the peritoneum and glue them together with stitches.
Then you pull the end of the ligature and do not cut the peritoneum at all, nor touch the testicle or anything else as I have taught you in the treatment of intestinal hernias, then treat it with surgical treatments.
If the sutures break and are replaced, and the wound is sutured until it heals, the peritoneum will contract and the hernia will not increase.
And cautery is best in this place because it is closer to safety.
Chapter Sixty-Eight.
On relaxing the skin of the testicles.
Often the skin of the testicles relaxes in some people without the flesh inside relaxing, and its appearance is unsightly. Therefore, whoever wishes to treat it should put the patient on their back.
And cut away all the relaxed skin on the thick skin, then gather the lips. And if you wish to sew first, sew the excess relaxed skin with three or four stitches and secure the stitching, then cut between the stitches.
Then treat it like all other surgeries until it heals and the sutures fall out.
Chapter Ninety-Six.
On castration.
Castration is forbidden in our law.
Therefore, I should not have mentioned it in this book of mine, but I did so for two reasons: one, so that it would be within the knowledge of a physician if he were asked about it and to know how to treat someone who has undergone it; and the other, that we often need to castrate some animals for our benefit.
Such as rams, stallions, cats and similar animals. I say that castration is of two types: either by pressure or by incision and cutting. The one done by pressure works as follows: we place the animal in hot water until its testicles relax, soften and hang down.
Then press them with your hand until they dissolve and are no longer perceptible to the touch. As for castration by incision and cutting, you should hold the animal and squeeze the skin of its testicles with your left hand, then tie the cords and make one incision on each testicle.
Even if the two testicles protrude, cut them off after you have flayed them, and do not leave upon them any of the tunics except for the thin tunic which is on the vessels; this kind of castration is better than that which is done by crushing.
For crushing may perhaps leave something of the testicles, and the animal may then desire copulation; afterwards treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter Seventy.
On the Treatment of Hermaphrodites.
Hermaphroditism in men occurs in two types, one of which is that it appears below the space or in the skin of the testicles, between the testicles, in a shape like a woman's vulva, with hair, and urine may flow from that which is in the skin of the testicles.
As for women, there is one type, and it is above the vulva on the pudendum, like a small penis in men, emerging outwards; one of them, as though it were a man's penis, and both are like testicles. The treatment for all three types.
Both types in men, and the one type in women, should have that superfluous flesh cut off so that its trace is removed, then it should be treated with the treatment of all other surgical wounds until it heals. As for the second type of man from whom urine flows from that which is in the skin of the testicles, there is no action necessary nor any benefit whatsoever.
Chapter Seventy-One.
On the Excision of the Clitoris and the Flesh That Issues From the Vulvae of Women.
The clitoris may sometimes be excessive in size beyond the natural norm, so that it becomes prominent and unsightly; and it may become enlarged in some women to such an extent that it spreads like that of men and becomes capable of copulation.
Therefore, you should hold the excess clitoris with your hand or forceps and cut it off; do not be too thorough in the cutting, especially deep at the root, lest bleeding occur.
Then treat it with surgical treatment until it is healed, and as for the growing flesh, it is flesh that grows in the mouth of the womb until it fills it.
And perhaps it protrudes outward, like a tail. Therefore, some of the ancients call it the tailed disease, so it should be cut off as the foreskin is cut off, and treated until it is healed.
Chapter Seventy-Two
On the Treatment of Retroversion.
Retroversion is when a woman's vulva is not pierced or the hole is small, and it is either natural, born with it, or acquired, resulting from a previous cause, which can be due to excessive growing flesh.
Or a thin or thick peritoneum, which can be in the depth of the uterus, or on its sides, or at its top or bottom, and prevents intercourse, pregnancy, and childbirth, and may prevent menstruation. Knowledge of this is obtained by looking from the midwife.
If the obstruction is apparent and nearby, if not, examine it with her fingers or a probe. If the obstruction is from thin peritoneum and is close to the labia, then proceed to puncture it, which is done by placing on the labia something like stirrups, then place your thumbs from both hands together.
And the woman on her back with her legs spread, then stretch the labia forcefully until the thin peritoneum is punctured and the obstruction opens. Then take wool, soak it in oil, and place it on the spot. Intercourse with the woman every day so that the spot does not join again.
If the peritoneum is thick and dense, it should be cut with a wide spatula-like scalpel. If the obstruction is from growing flesh, hang it with forceps and cut it off. Have pain-relieving medications that are not caustic on hand, such as tragacanth, mastic, and frankincense mixed with egg white.
Then a wide lead pipe is used so that the wound does not heal quickly, it is held for several days and a wick of dry linen is used, then treated with all the usual treatments for healing until it is cured, and if another fleshy growth appears in the womb, it should be cut in this way.
Unless it is a cancerous tumor, the cancerous tumor that occurs in the womb is not treated with iron at all.
Chapter Seventy-Three.
On the treatment of hemorrhoids, warts, and red pimples that appear in the vulva of women.
Hemorrhoids are a swelling of the openings of the veins until a great deal of blood constantly flows from them, and if hemorrhoids develop, they become warts, and I have mentioned their types and signs in the classification, and we will mention them here.
What is treatable and what is not treatable, I say that hemorrhoids and warts if they are deep in the womb and do not appear to sensation there is no treatment with iron, and what is in the mouth of the womb that falls upon sensation is what is treated.
She should enter a cold room, then spread the warts with a scraper or a rough rag and cut them from their roots, then put one of the hemostatic agents on it without smearing.
Such as catechu, mastic, frankincense and the like, then raise her legs to the wall for an hour, then lower them into cold water, if the bleeding continues, sit her in a decoction of senna, pomegranate peel, rue and the like, then take barley flour knead it with honey and vinegar.
And apply it to her back, if the bleeding stops, otherwise, stick to hard squeezing of the breasts and nipples without restriction, then take a wick and dip it in the juice of mallows or the juice of lamb's tongue or the juice of almond branches with rue syrup and apply it to the place.
If the swelling subsides, treat the area with ointment until it heals. As for the red wen, it resembles the heads of millet, rough in appearance. You should cut off what appears from it as I have mentioned regarding warts, and treat it until it heals.
Chapter Seventy-Four.
On the Incision of an Abscess That Occurs in the Womb.
Many kinds of tumors may appear in the womb, such as cancer, a petrified tumor, ulcers, fistulas, gangrene, warts, hemorrhoids, fissures, and hot tumors. I have mentioned all these diseases, their types, and their signs in the division.
Therefore, we should mention in this article the hot tumor that appears in the womb if it is one of the tumors that gathers for a while, how to incise it with iron. You should look and see if the pain in the beginning of the tumor is sharp with throbbing, inflammation, and fever.
And if you see it red and feel pain when touched, do not rush to incise it and treat it with something that promotes ripening until these symptoms subside. Then, the woman should sit on a chair with two seats and lean against her back.
She should bring her legs together toward the lower abdomen, and her thighs should be open. Her arms should be placed under her knees and tied with a suitable bandage.
Then the midwife should sit on the right side and use the instrument that opens the mouth of the womb, a picture of which will come later. If you want to use this instrument, estimate with a rod the depth of the woman's womb so that what enters from the instrument is not more than the depth of the womb.
It will harm the patient if the instrument is longer than the depth, so pads should be placed on the lip of the cervix to prevent the whole instrument from entering the depth of the womb. The spiral that runs in the instrument should be from the upper side.
And you hold the instrument while the attendant rotates the screw until the cervix opens. If the abscess then appears and is touched by the hand and found to be soft and thin, it should be incised at its head with a broad bistoury, and if the whole period has been evacuated...
A soft wick soaked in rose oil or green oil with some astringency should then be placed in the wound, and the wick should protrude from the incision in the cervix. Clean wool soaked in water that has boiled with yeast should be applied externally over the womb and the vulva.
It is then treated after two days with an ointment suitable for this purpose until it heals.
The womb and the wound may be washed by injecting it with water and honey, then with water that has been boiled with marshmallow root or wild rhubarb, and then return to the ointment. If the abscess is hidden internally in the uterus.
The use of iron should be avoided in its treatment but treated as mentioned in this section.
Chapter Seventy-Fifth.
On Teaching Midwives How to Treat Live Fetuses When They Are Delivered in an Unnatural Manner.
The midwife should first know the signs of a normal birth. These include the woman leaning downward and desiring to breathe deeply, and feeling relief from her labor pains.
The delivery of the fetus is hastened; know that this birth is normal and occurs with the head presenting first, the placenta either accompanying it or hanging from its cord. If you see these signs, you should press on her abdomen so that the fetus is delivered quickly.
If the head descends, the placenta will follow and be thoroughly cleansed of such residue. Any birth other than this is abnormal and reprehensible because the fetus may emerge with its legs first or with its hands preceding its head and legs.
Whether one hand or one foot emerges, or his head and hand emerge together, or he comes out folded, or perhaps turns over onto his shoulders, or any other of these disgraceful forms, the midwife should be skillful and gentle with all of these things and beware of errors and missteps.
I will explain each of these forms and how to manage them so that you can recognize them and deal with them accordingly. If the fetus emerges head first, which is the natural way, and labor becomes difficult for the woman, and you see her strength weakening, seat her on a chair and order women to hold her steady.
Soak her womb with boiled fenugreek and wet ointments, then the midwife inserts a small speculum between her fingers and cuts the placenta with it or cuts it with her fingernail until the moisture inside flows out, and she presses the woman's belly until the fetus comes down.
But if it does not descend, she should inject the woman with fenugreek saliva mixed with shiraj ointment, then order her to gargle and sneeze by tickling her with a reed, while holding her mouth and nose shut; for the fetus will emerge in its hour. If the fetus's hand comes out first, it should be gently pushed back a little at a time.
If entry is not possible, place the woman on a raised platform and raise her legs above it, then shake the platform on the ground while the woman holds on so as not to fall when shaken. If the hands still do not enter and the fetus dies, cut them off and pull out the rest of the fetus.
Or tie his hands with a cloth and pull them moderately, for it will come out. Emergence of the fetus on its legs. If the fetus emerges on its legs, push them upwards then turn the fetus a little at a time until it is straightened, then take one of its legs and extend it a little at a time.
If it comes out to the thighs, order her to crawl and pull it with forceps; it will come out. If it does not come out as described, then push back the fetus a little at a time until it becomes in its natural shape, for then it will come out smoothly. But if all of this fails.
Take saliva from the hyssop and licorice, and clarified butter and dissolved mastic, and beat all of them in a mortar very well; then apply it to the woman's vulva and lower abdomen. Then seat her in lukewarm water until it reaches the hips.
But if you see that her lower part has become soft, make for her a poultice of rue and apply it; if she holds the poultice for an hour, then seat her on a chair, and pull it out while covering her mouth and nose and squeeze her lower abdomen lightly; for the fetus will come out at once. The fetus comes out upon its knees and hands.
If it comes out in this manner, help insert its hands a little at a time; then let the woman lie on her back and lower her legs downward while lying on a bed with her hands clasped together, and squeeze above her abdomen gently. Then remove what is under her legs from the pillows until her lower part is raised; if the fetus does not come out, take hold of both of her legs and move them vigorously, then squeeze above the hips gently so that the fetus ascends upward; then let the midwife insert her hand and straighten the fetus a little at a time.
Order the woman to crawl until the fetus comes out; if the fetus comes out obstructed, with one of its hands extended, help push back its hand; if she is unable to push it back, have the woman stand and make her walk; if she cannot walk, let her lie on her back.
Her bed and shake her legs vigorously; then use the pessaries that have been described made from saliva; if labor starts, seat her on a chair and then work at pushing back the hand and straightening the fetus into its natural shape; pull the woman and order her to crawl until it comes out.
The expulsion of the fetus on its head, extending its hands and facing its face towards the mother's back, should be grasped by the midwife at the fetus' hand then gently turning it little by little and straightening it. If it becomes straight, apply to the front what we described of fat and lubricants.
Then let her move a little, and if she does, seat her on a chair and order her to lean to the right side then let her slide and sneeze her with a speculum, for it will come out, the expulsion of the fetus upright on its side.
If it descends to the mouth of the uterus in this manner with the placenta, tear the placenta with your nail until it is freed from moisture. If it clings to the face and neck of the fetus, cut it from the navel lest it be strangled and die, then push the fetus back inside until it becomes as it should be.
And order the woman to slide several times, for it will come out easily. The expulsion of twins or many fetuses, know that twins often are born and sometimes three or four are born and live, but this is rare. As for five, it is something beyond nature and they do not live at all.
They are surrounded by one placenta, even if they are many, and they are separated by a partition membrane tied at the navel of each one. They may be expelled as a single fetus is expelled naturally easily, or they may be expelled with difficulty as we have described.
If some of them are difficult during childbirth and become what we have mentioned of the unnatural shapes, treat it as we described. Let the midwife be gentle and do what she does gently and with patience and try to overcome every shape with whatever trick leads to safety.
Know what is conceived in the womb of fetuses may be miscarried, one, two, three, four, five, six, seven or more than ten may be conceived. It has been confirmed to me that a woman miscarried seven and another fifteen, all of them conceived.
By the esteemed and knowing One, all of this should be in the knowledge of the midwife; for perhaps such a thing may befall her one day, so that she may meet it as is fitting.
Chapter Seventy-Sixth.
On the Extraction of a Dead Fetus.
If you have treated the dead fetus as mentioned in the division of treatment and it has not come out, and you are forced to work with your hands, then you should see if the woman is strong and healthy and does not have anything that she fears damage from. She should lie on a bed on her back.
Her head should be inclined downwards and her legs raised, and an attendant should hold her from both sides or she should be tied to the bed so that her body is not pulled when the fetus is extended.
Then the midwife should moisten the mouth of her womb with soft ointments mixed with saliva from a suckling infant, fenugreek and flaxseed. Then the midwife should anoint her hands with these ointments and salivas and gently insert them into the womb. Then she should search for a place where to implant the needles from the fetus and suitable places for that.
See, if the fetus is likely to descend headfirst, implant the needles into its eyes or temples or mouth or throat or under its chin or collarbone or in places near the ribs or under the shoulder blades.
But if its descent is feet first, then in the groin and the middle ribs; she should hold the needle in her right hand and twist it between the fingers of her left hand, insert her hand gently and implant a needle in some of those places that we have mentioned.
Until it reaches something empty, then implant another or a third needle opposite it if needed so that the pull is equal and does not lean to one side; then extend in a level manner, and the extension should not be only straight but moved to every direction.
To make it easy for him to come out as is done with a molar when it is extracted, and it should be relaxed in the meantime. If one side of it becomes retained, it should be smeared with oil by the midwife and some fingers inserted from that side to turn what is retained. And if part of the fetus comes out, the forceps should be moved to other places.
It is raised a little to do this until the whole fetus comes out, and if a hand comes out before anything else and cannot be returned due to its compression, it should be wrapped with a cloth and then pulled until it comes out entirely, then it is cut.
Either at the wrist joint or at the elbow, and this should be done with the other hand and the legs. If the fetal head is large and it undergoes pressure during delivery, or if there is accumulated water in its head, a sharp spoon-shaped instrument should be inserted between the fingers.
And it should split the head so that the water comes out, or it can be inserted with the instrument called a craniotome, the picture of which will come with the pictures of all other instruments in the chapter after this.
This is also done if the fetus has a large head naturally, its skull should be split or crushed with a craniotome as we said, then the bones are removed with forceps. If the head comes out and gets compressed at the neck, it should be split so that the moisture in the chest drains.
For then the chest will rejoin, if the chest does not rejoin and it is not possible to exit, cut as much as you can. If the lower abdomen is hot or has pus, it should be bandaged so that the moisture drains from it.
If the fetus is on its feet, pulling it is easy and straightening it to the cervix is simple. If it gets compressed at the abdomen or chest, it should be pulled with a cloth wrapped around your hand and the abdomen or chest should be split so that what is inside them flows out.
If you remove all the limbs and the head returns and is held fast, let the midwife insert her left hand; if the mouth of the womb is open, let her insert her hand into the neck of the womb and seek the head with it and draw it down to the mouth of the womb with her fingers, then introduce a pessary or two pessaries and draw it out.
If the mouth of the womb has become closed by a hot swelling, do not force it, but use applications of fatty moist substances, and apply poultices, and sit in waters which soften and moisten.
But if the fetus is on its side, and it can be straightened, use what we have mentioned for a living fetus; but if that cannot be done, cut it into pieces and remove it. Nothing of the placenta should be left inside at all. When you have finished your treatment, use what we have mentioned for treating hot swellings which occur in the wombs.
If bleeding occurs, let her bathe in astringent water and use all other remedies. I have seen a woman who had been pregnant, the fetus died inside her, then she became pregnant again.
The other fetus also died, and after a long time she developed a swelling in her abdomen which became distended until it opened and began to discharge pus. I was called to treat her, and treated her for a long time but the wound would not close, so I applied some strong drawing ointments to it.
A bone came out of the place, then after a few days another bone came out. I was astonished because there are no bones in the abdomen. I realized that they must be bones from the dead fetus. So I searched the wound and removed many bones from the skull of the fetus.
I continued to do this until I removed many bones, and the woman was in the best of health. She lived for a long time, with only a little pus discharging from the place. I have related this rare case here.
For in it is knowledge and assistance for what the artisan physician attempts by his hand in treatment.
