Chapter seventy-eight.
On the removal of the placenta.
If the placenta is retained after childbirth, you should order the patient to hold herself tightly and then sneeze it out with a speculum while grasping her mouth and nostrils. If it comes out this way, that's good; otherwise take a quantity (of something).
Make a hole in the lid and insert herbs that are open for the mouth of the womb, such as fennel, licorice, parsley, chamomile, rue, sage, and coriander; all these drugs or some of them. Soak them in water and place it over the fire. Then place a silver tube on the hole in the lid and the other end in the mouth of the womb.
Hold it until the steam reaches the bottom of the womb; then make her sneeze as we said, for the placenta will come out quickly. If it remains after this treatment and does not come out, order the midwife to dip her left hand in sheep fat or camel saliva.
Then insert it into the vagina and search for the placenta. If she finds it, grasp it and pull it out slowly until it comes out. If it is stuck deep in the uterus, insert the hand as we described until you find the placenta. Then pull it out gently, little by little, with compassion.
So that the womb does not fall during strong traction, but it should be moved gently to the sides, right and left, then the amount of traction should be increased; for it will then respond and become free from adhesion. If the cervix is closed, we have described treatment by sneezing and treatment by quantity and herbs.
If it does not come out with all that we have described, then beware of violence against her in re-traction; but it should be tied from what came out to the cervix to the woman's thigh, then inject her with the fourfold ointment, for it will soften it after a few days.
It will dissolve and come out; but if it becomes putrid, then a foul odor arises from it to the stomach and head, and that harms the patient. Therefore, appropriate incense should be used.
Therefore, some of the ancients tried incense of aloeswood and dried figs. An image of the instrument with which a woman is fumigated when there is retention of menstruation and placenta, etc.:
It is made of lead, resembling a funnel, or made of copper; the slender end is placed in the vulva and the wide end on the fire. The incense is placed on the embers, held until that incense has gone, and then replaced with other incense.
Chapter Ninety-Seventh.
In the Treatment of an Imperforate Anus.
Many boys may be born with imperforate anus, often closed by a thin peritoneum. The midwife should pierce this peritoneum with her finger; otherwise, let her incise it with a sharp bistoury, being careful not to touch the muscle.
Then she should place upon it a wick soaked in wine and oil, and treat it with ointments until it is healed. If there is fear that it will close again, insert a lead tube into the opening for many days and remove it when the child wishes to defecate.
Sometimes the blockage of the anus may also be due to a wound or a tumor, so it should be incised and then treated as we have mentioned in terms of treatment and suturing.
Chapter Eighty.
In the treatment of fistulas that occur below.
Fistulas that occur below are a complication and thickening that occurs near the anus from the outside or in the space from one of the sides, and the fistula may be one or more.
If this complication opens and moisture, watery or thin pus flows from it, and some of these fistulas may be penetrating to the urethra and into the intestines, and others may not be penetrating. The penetrating fistulas are known by what comes out of them of feces and gas when the patient defecates.
Perhaps worms may come out of it, and some fistulas may be penetrating into the bladder and into the urethra if they are in the space. Some may also be penetrating into the hip joint and into the coccyx, and what is known as a fistula penetrating into the anus is also sometimes not penetrating.
Insert your index finger into the anus and insert a thin probe into the fistula, made of copper or iron, if there is no bend in the fistula; but if there is a bend, insert a thin lead probe or a horsehair into it.
Until you feel the probe or hair with your finger, and if you do not feel it at all, and nothing of feces, gas, or worms emerges from the hole as we said, then know that it is not penetrating, so proceed to treatment.
And if the fistula is penetrating into the bladder or into the urethra, then its sign is urine coming out of it and its refusal to heal with medications. As for if it is penetrating into the hip joint or into the coccyx, then its sign is the probe reaching there.
If there is no extension to the bone and there is pain in a certain direction, and pus constantly discharges from it, and treatment is ineffective and ointments do not heal it, and all of these perforating fistulas, then there is no cure at all.
Its treatment is a futile effort for those ignorant physicians who attempt it, but the one that is not perforating and not chronic may be cured with iron as I will describe and have experienced. It is to lay the patient down on his back between your hands.
Raise his legs upward and incline his thighs toward his stomach, then insert a copper or lead probe if there is an extension in the fistula until you know where the probe ends. If the patient feels it towards the rectum, then you should insert your middle finger into the rectum.
If you feel that the probe has penetrated on its own, exposed and without feeling any fascia or flesh between your finger and it, then know for sure that it is perforating, so do not exert effort in this because there is no cure as we have said.
And they have said that it is cured in some people rarely, and of the treatments from which benefit is hoped, is to heat a thin iron according to the width of the fistula as previously mentioned and insert it while hot into the fistula until it reaches towards the rectum, then repeat this two or three times.
Until you know that all of the excess, loosened flesh which resembles a bird's quill tube has been burned, then treat it with a bandage soaked in butter until those burned tissues come out, and then treat it with prepared ointments; if it is cured.
Otherwise, it will never be cured by any other treatment. But if you insert the probe and it does not penetrate to your finger in the rectum and there is a thick barrier of flesh or fascia between it and the probe, and you see that the fistula is below the surface of the skin, then cut open the skin from the beginning of the fistula. And you pass with the probe while it is in the nostril until you reach the cut where the end of the probe is, and the probe is released and falls out; then you clean those adhered tissues that resemble a bird's feather tube, leaving nothing behind.
Then treat it with prepared ointments until it is healed. If blood overwhelms you and prevents you from working, the best thing to do and the fastest benefit is to cauterize those tissues with fire or a caustic drug, because cautery by fire accomplishes two good things: burning the excess tissues.
And cauterizing the blood and drying the moisture, then treat it with a bandage moistened in butter or sulfur powder mixed with oil until the area suppurates and those burnt tissues come out in the pus; then treat the wound at that time with prepared ointments that promote hard flesh.
These are drugs that contain astringents and drying agents; if it becomes constricted, it has healed and you need not fear its return. If the nostril has reached the depth of the cavity and is far from the surface of the body, insert your finger into the cavity and explore with it.
If you feel the probe and there is a veil of peritoneum or flesh between you and it, and it is near penetration, then there is no work to be done except on the path of hope and ambition. This means using one of three methods: cauterize it as we have said.
Or incise it until you reach its bottom so that you can insert the bandage and treat it from close range, and do not overdo the incision lest you cut the muscle surrounding the cavity, which will cause the patient to have involuntary bowel movements; then treat it as we mentioned, for perhaps it will heal as we said.
Or pierce that veil and perforate it after you have incised it near the cavity as we said, either with a probe or another sharp instrument; then clean all those adhered tissues in the incision as much as possible by careful examination.
Then, bind the whole wound with the incision and the upper opening until it is sealed, leaving the anus open inside the seat so that it is lighter for the patient, and the anus may remain in this condition.
If you insert the probe into the anus and it is in a side of the seat toward the surface of the body with the skin and the ring of the seat, then take a probe pierced at the end like a cobbler's awl in this manner:
And insert into it a twisted thread of five threads or so, then insert the probe with the thread into the anus until it reaches its bottom. If it is perforated in the edge of the seat from inside near...
Then pull the thread out of that hole by inserting your finger into the seat and pulling out the end of the thread, gathering both ends together and tightening them, leaving it for a day or two. Every time the thread cuts into the flesh, tighten it well so that the flesh between the two ends of the thread breaks off and falls, then treat the wound until it heals and is cured.
If the anus opening is not perforated, then perforate it as best you can, unless it is very deep, in which case you should not do so because of the muscle, lest you cut it. Then proceed with it as we mentioned until it heals.
The image of the spiked scalpel with which you incise the anuses has a very sharp point, and the other side is not sharp so as not to cut what is not needed, as you see:
Chapter Eighty-One.
On the treatment of hemorrhoids that bleed, their excision, and the treatment of fissures.
Hemorrhoids come in two types: either they are inside the seat, resembling red cushions or like grapes, and some of them are small and some are large, and blood constantly flows from them.
And it may be single or multiple and may be outside the seat and at its edges, but those that are outside the seat are in most cases few in moisture from which yellow water or a little blood oozes constantly, and they are of the color of the body.
And the treatment of that which is from inside the seat is to order the patient to defecate and strain until the seat comes out and the warts appear to you, then hurry up and attach them with forceps or hold them with your fingernail, and then cut them off at their bases; if the forceps do not hold in them due to their moisture and laxity.
Then grasp it with a coarse rag or a piece of toasted bread, or draw it with your fingers and then cut it, then scatter some sharp grains on it after cutting so that they act as a cautery for it, or burn them as previously mentioned in the chapter on cauterization.
Then treat it with all the treatment until it is healed, and if the seat does not come out for you, inject the patient with an injection containing a little bitterness to wash what is in the seat and make it come out quickly when the patient strains; as for the warts that are outside the seat, their treatment is easy.
It is to take it with your fingernail or attach it with forceps and cut it off, then treat it as we have mentioned until it heals, and whoever dislikes cutting with iron should use its cautery on this method: Take a twisted thread and put it into a needle.
Then draw the wart upwards and pass it through the needle at its base from the other side, wind the ends of the thread below the needle while it is obstructing and tighten the wart tightly, then tie the thread and remove the needle. Do this to all the warts and leave one without cauterizing it.
So that pus drains from it, then place a rag soaked in rose oil or quince on the seat and put it on the seat, and order the patient to rest, then leave him until they fall off; when the warts fall off, treat them with ointments and all other treatment until they are healed.
As for the treatment of fissures, they often arise from dryness and stagnation of the humor. If it persists and medicine proves ineffective, one should pare it with a scalpel or your fingernail until it becomes moist and the upper scab that prevents its union is removed.
Then treat it until it heals properly. If it does not heal, pare it again more deeply than the first time until it bleeds and sheds, then treat it; for it will be cured.
Chapter Eighty-Two.
On the Treatment of Inverted and Non-Inverted Warts, Dry Corns, and Calluses.
A wart is a round nodule the color of the flesh, resembling the head of a nail. It occurs throughout the body, particularly on the soles of the feet and toes, and causes pain when walking.
It should be incised around the wart and grasped with forceps or a snare and removed from its root, then treated. If you wish, cauterize it as previously mentioned in its chapter. As for dry corns, each one is a small, rough, adherent, round prominence above the surface of the body.
They often occur on the hands of boys. Treatment consists of stretching the corn and cutting it or scraping it with silk thread or a hair until it falls off. If you wish, cauterize it as previously mentioned with fire or caustic agents.
Inverted and non-inverted warts can also be removed with the instrument I have described in the treatment of calluses. As for calluses, they are also small, adherent, and thick prominences on the surface of the body that extend deeply inward. When exposed to cold, they produce a sensation similar to an ant bite.
They occur throughout the body, most commonly on the hands. Treatment involves taking a quill from a goose or a strong eagle feather and placing it around the callus to form a circle. Then rotate your hand with the quill until you incise the skin surrounding the callus.
And make it deep in the flesh and the ant will detach from its root, and if you wish to do that with a copper or iron tube in this manner:
The top of the tube should be tightly closed and twisted to make it easy for the fingers to hold and turn it, and if you wish, cauterize the ant as previously mentioned in the chapter on cautery.
Chapter Thirty-Eight.
On instruments used for injection in cases of sciatica, diarrhea and colic.
The syringe can be made of silver, pewter, or brass that is cast or hammered, and small or large ones can be made from this instrument depending on those who use it. Those used to treat young children will be small, and those with narrow or painful bowels will have very fine syringes. Image of a large syringe:
Its upper funnel, where the cannula is attached, is wide and has a barrier where the cannula is attached as you see, and its lower end which enters the rectum is tightly closed and very smooth, with two holes on one side of the neck and one hole on the other side.
The size of the hole should be equal to or slightly larger than the thickness of the cannula, and the cannula in which the drugs are placed is made from a bladder animal or sheepskin, shaped like a small spoon, with a diameter of one span.
It is pierced with many holes as it rotates, and the distance between each hole is the thickness of a finger, then a tight thread of double-stranded yarn or similar is inserted into these holes, and the cannula is gathered together like a spoon. If medicine is placed inside, the top of the syringe is tightened between two stones tightly, and then injected. This is an image of the cannula circle and hole:
How to fill it: Fill the bladder or the sac with warmed medicine as much as the patient can tolerate, be careful that it is not too hot or too cold, then the patient lies on their back and raises their legs up.
A piece of cloth or a towel is placed underneath, then the tip of the syringe is smeared with grease, egg white, fenugreek saliva, or something similar. It is then gently inserted into the anus. Then an assistant squeezes the syringe firmly with both hands until all its contents are emptied into the intestine.
The syringe is then removed, and the patient leans against a wall with his legs raised. If the medicine urges him to evacuate, he should hold it as long as possible. If he sleeps all night, it will be most effective in purging. If he was injected for diarrhea and wants to hasten its exit, the injection should be repeated until he is cured.
If there are hemorrhoids or painful tumors in the anus, the syringe should be very fine and smooth, like those used for injecting children. This is an image of a fine syringe with its skin drawn tight:
Chapter Eighty-Fourth.
On Surgical Treatment.
As I mentioned in the section on treating what resembles cysts, and as I will further detail here, treatment with both hands and medicine is complete. I say that surgeries differ according to the thing being operated upon and according to the location of the wound.
The things that involve surgeries are numerous, such as a stone bruise, a sword or knife cut, a spear or stick thrust, an arrow wound, and similar things.
As for surgeries according to the location on the body, there are wounds on the head or neck, chest or abdomen, liver, and similar organs.
I will describe the treatment of some surgeries to make them a measure and rule for all other surgeries, and I begin with simple head wounds, since complex ones will be mentioned at the beginning of Chapter Three of this book.
I say that when a simple head wound occurs without a bone fracture, and it is from a stone bruise or something similar, and the skin has merely been split open, and the wound is large and there is fear of a hot abscess developing, immediately apply a poultice to the area. Do not delay it, and let blood out of him to the extent of his strength, unless something prevents him from phlebotomy, especially if the blood coming out of the wound is little; and let the phlebotomy be from the opposite side of the wounded area.
Warn the patient against repletion, drinking, and thick foods; if something prevents him from phlebotomy, let him reduce his food and keep away from meats and drinks. Apply to the wound if a hot swelling occurs, cotton wool soaked in rose oil alone or with a drink that has astringent properties.
And if you are confident that there will be no hot swelling, apply to the wound if it is soft with its blood and has not been changed by the dry air which I will describe to you shortly; but if it has been changed by the air, apply some ointments to it.
Until it produces pus, then treat it until it heals; if the wound is large and is from a sword cut or similar and its lips have not come together with ligatures, bring them together by suturing as I will describe in suturing abdominal wounds.
If the skin exposes the bone and hangs, and its adherence is only slight, cut it off; then treat the wound with medicines that will produce solid flesh instead of skin; and order the patient to eat foods that have tenacity, such as heads and tails and similar things.
If putrefaction occurs in the skin and it does not adhere to the bone, cut off all the putrified part, cauterize it, then treat it; if there is an artery or vein bleeding from these wounds and the blood does not stop with medicines, search for the artery.
If you find it, do not amputate it but cut it with a scalpel and ligate it; if necessity requires and what we mentioned does not work, cauterize it until the bleeding stops. If these wounds are small and simple, their treatment is easy:  it suffices to treat them by leaving this dry air on them while they are bloody.
Before the air changes it, the recipe for stopping bleeding is to take one part frankincense, two parts mastic, and three parts slaked lime or unslaked lime; pound all together, strain it, and pack the wound with it, binding it tightly so that a good plaster adheres to it.
It will then coagulate with the blood, and you should bind it tightly from above with bandages and leave it undisturbed as long as there is no swelling; for the medicine will not separate from it until it is healed. If frankincense and mastic are not available, slaked lime alone may be used.
This same procedure can also be performed on wounds with a probe in many large operations, if it is skillfully applied and tightly bound, provided the wounds are fresh and bloody, as long as the air has not changed them; but if the air has already altered them somewhat, especially in summer.
Then there is no point in applying this styptic, as we have said that you should apply some stimulating ointments to it or apply a poultice of barley flour made with water and honey so that it will ripen the pus and then treat it with all other remedies until it is healed.
But if a fracture occurs in the wound and it is slight, draw it together with a ligature; as I have mentioned the treatment of head fractures later on, under surgery of the neck, if the wound occurs in the neck there is no difference in treatment between it and a simple head wound.
But if a nerve or artery has been severed in the neck, and it is a nerve, there is no remedy except to avoid treating it with anything that constricts severely, such as lead plaster or litharge, for they will injure the nerve and cause it to contract.
Nothing cold should be put on the wound at all, because the nerve itself is cold, and its connection to the brain, which is the noblest of organs, makes it necessary that any medicine used for treatment be soft, such as rose water washed with fresh water many times and moistened with oil or pomegranate seed ointment.
If it is also washed with fresh water and all metallic stones in this manner, and with wet ointments, but if the wound is large, use stitching or bring its lips together with bandages, then treat it until it heals.
If the wound has a cavity and an abscess has formed at its bottom, puncture it in the lowest part of it. If an artery has been severed in the wound and you see its blood flowing, amputate it, or tie it off, or cauterize it if necessary.
If the wound has severed some or all of the cartilages of the larynx, but the vocal cords are preserved, bring the lips of the skin together with sutures over the trachea; do not touch the larynx itself, but straighten it and return it to its natural shape, then tighten it firmly and leave it for several days.
Then treat it with ointments that have astringent and drying properties until it heals. If the wound has affected the bone, examine it. If there are bone fragments in it, extract all of them or as many as possible. If you cannot extract them all, leave them until the wound is granulated and healed, making removal easier.
If the wound is soft, apply the cerate that we have described. If bone becomes putrefied in the wound and turns necrotic, treat it as follows, as mentioned in its chapter, in thoracic surgery and between the shoulders.
If it is a stab wound from a spear or knife and you see a cavity, observe if air escapes from it when the patient breathes. If so, know that it is a combat wound. If there is no cavity and it is soft with blood, do not apply cerate to it at first, nor tighten it lest the blood be retained in its cavity.
For if it reaches the heart, it will kill the patient. Instead, apply a drawing ointment to it and dissolve it twice during the day. If you do not have an ointment, put an old cotton swab in the wound to absorb any moisture that comes out of it and let the patient sleep on the wound.
If pus gathers in it, and even if the wound is three days old or more, and the patient has no tremors, bad palpitations, or shortness of breath, and I see all his other conditions are good, then know that the wound is healthy and treat it with bandages and all other treatment until it heals.
If its pus cannot be seen and the discharge continues, then know that it has become suppurative and treat it from the opening, allowing the patient to drain what gathers in it. If the wound is on the chest or back, treat it as previously described with sutures.
If it is large, or on the arm if it is small, and it has affected the bone and pieces have broken off from it, then search the wound and immediately remove those pieces if they are clean; but if they are not clean, leave them until.
The wound will fester and it will be easy to remove them. As for all other wounds that occur in all other organs, their treatment is ruled as we have mentioned.
Chapter Eighty-Five.
On Abdominal Wounds and Evisceration, and Their Suturing.
The rupture that occurs in the abdomen may be large, or small, or medium-sized. The large one may protrude intestines, or several intestines, making their insertion and suturing more difficult.
Even the small rupture may be difficult in another way, requiring a more laborious return, and if the intestines are not immediately returned to their place they will swell and thicken, making insertion difficult. Therefore, medium-sized ruptures have become preferable because the return of the intestines is not as difficult as with these two types.
Know that suturing the abdomen is done in four ways: two general sutures, which are suitable for suturing the abdomen and all other wounds of the body; and two specific sutures, which apply to all wounds but are especially suited for suturing the abdomen.
And I will describe them to you one by one with explanation and clarification, saying that if the wound is small and some of the intestines have come out and it is difficult to put them back in, this is due to one of two things: either because the tear is small as we said, or because the intestine has been exposed to bloating from cold air beforehand.
If this is the case, it should be heated by soaking a sponge or a damp cloth in lukewarm water alone, or in which rue, saffron and fennel have been cooked, and wrapping the intestines with it until the bloating subsides.
A drink that contains astringents is also effective and better than water alone in dissolving bloating. If you see that the bloating has subsided, moisten the intestines with water in which marshmallow or anise have been cooked, as this will make it easier to insert them.
If it is still impossible for them to return after this treatment, enlarge the tear slightly with an instrument used for perforating nasal passages. The first treatment, if successful, is preferable to incision. However, one must resort to incision only when necessary and after all other options have failed. This is a depiction of the instrument:
One side of it is curved and limited, while the other side is unlimited. The thin end is not as sharp as a scalpel but is slightly blunt. It is an instrument resembling a small probe, as you see. If the wound widens and the intestines enter, they should be returned to their natural shape.
Its specific location, if you can achieve it, is best. If the tear is wide and located in the lower abdomen, the patient should lie on their back with their legs raised higher than their head. If it is in the upper abdomen, their head and chest should be raised higher than their lower body.
Similarly, if the tear is on one side of the abdomen, always aim to have the surgical side raised higher than the other. This should be done in major and intermediate surgeries as well.
As for minor surgeries, you place it according to what is possible for you, then you prepare a reliable assistant in front of you who holds all the rags with his hands and brings together his lips, then he reveals from it to the one who sews something after something.
This is the description of a common suture, which is to take a needle or several needles according to the width of the wound, then leave from the edge of the cloth a length equal to the thickness of the fingernail and insert one needle without threading it into the edges of the skin. With the edges of the peritoneum under the skin from inside until it passes through that side, having gathered the edges of the skin and the edges of the peritoneum, resulting in four layers, then tighten with a doubled thread around the needle several times on both sides so that the lips of the wound come together firmly.
Then leave a length equal to the thickness of the finger also and insert another needle, then interlock it with the thread as you did with the first needle, continuing to do so with as many needles as needed until the entire wound is filled. Let the needles be medium in thickness.
Because needles that are too thin will quickly cut through the flesh and those that are too thick are difficult to insert into the skin, so it should be medium in thinness and thickness. And the needle should not be inserted into the edge of the skin nearby.
Lest the flesh be cut quickly and the wound opened before it heals, and do not space the stitches too far apart lest the wound fail to heal. Then cut off the ends of the needles so as not to hurt the patient when he sleeps and make him bandages of linen cloth from each side to hold the ends of the needles.
Leave it until you know that the wound has healed, this type of suture with needles is thus suitable for minor surgeries because it may be sufficient to sew it with one or two needles or so.
As for the manner of suturing for a second intention, it is to gather by suture the four margins, namely the skin margin and the peritoneal margin at once with a needle containing a twisted thread that is moderate in fineness and thickness; then when these four margins have been passed through by the needle, return the needle from the side where you began.
Let the thread lie locked from above the wound so that the suture is according to the stitching of a purse which is tightened by drawing it, and make the distance between each stitch equal to the thickness of the little finger; this suture is known to all people.
With this suture, I operated on a man who had a wound in his belly inflicted by a knife; the rupture of the surgery was more than a span long and about two spans of the middle intestine had protruded from it; the rupture was in the middle of the abdomen.
I replaced it after his intestines had been outside the wound for twenty-four hours, and the wound healed in about fifteen days; I treated him until he was well and he lived for many years afterward, behaving normally in all respects, whereas the physicians had pronounced him incurable.
And it is marvelous that I did not treat him with ointment, because I was in a place where there were no medicines at all; I used to put clean cotton on the wound twice a day and I used to undertake to wash it with honey water until it was healed.
As for one of the special kinds of sutures, I mention it according to the statement of Galen, which is to begin the suture from the skin and insert the needle from outside to inside; when the needle has passed through the skin and into the muscle going straight along the entire length of the abdomen.
Leave the edge of the peritoneum on the side where you inserted the needle, and pass the needle through its other edge from inside to outside in the other edge of the abdominal wall; when you have passed it through, pass it again in this same edge from the abdominal wall from outside to inside.
Remove the edge of the peritoneum on this side and insert the needle in its other edge from inside to outside, then pass it through the peritoneum to the edge of the diaphragm on its side until it passes completely through.
Then begin again from the same side, drawing a line with the edge of the peritoneum on the other side and bringing the needle out through the skin near it. Then return the needle to that skin and draw a line along the edge of the peritoneum on the other side with this edge of the diaphragm.
Bring it out through the skin on its side, and do this again and again until you sew the entire incision in that manner. The description of this stitching is that it sews the stitching of furriers.
By sewing the peritoneum once from one side with an edge of skin, and leaving it once on the other side until it empties. As for the second type of specific stitching that Galen also mentioned, this is his word verbatim.
It is to sew it according to the example of how some healers sew, by bringing together each part with its counterpart, naturally joining the edge of the peritoneum to its other edge and the edge of the diaphragm to its other edge, as I will describe to you.
You should insert the needle into the edge of the diaphragm closest to you from the outside and pass it inside alone, leaving the edges of the peritoneum, then return the needle and pass it from outside to inside in both edges of the peritoneum, then return it again.
And pass it from inside to outside in the other edge of the diaphragm on the opposite side. This type of stitching is better than the common, easy stitching, which is the stitching previously mentioned. This is Galen's word verbatim. Some have said that abdominal stitching can be done with two needles. This is done by inserting one thread into them and beginning by inserting the needle from your side, passing it through to the other side, then inserting the other needle from the opposite side, passing it through to you, according to the stitching of shoes.
Know that a rupture if it is in the middle of the belly, its stitching is more difficult than all other places of the belly. As for the treatment of the wound, its ruling in its treatment is like all other wounds, so that if you find the wound fresh with its blood before the air changes it, and you return the intestines and stitch and tighten them, then apply a poultice to it made of saltpeter.
But if the wound has been changed by the air, apply to it some of the ointments that you prepare until it suppurates and the threads fall off and the peritoneum and omentum unite, then treat it like your treatment of all other wounds until it heals.
If you do not have medicines, apply clean cotton to it from the beginning of suppuration and change it twice a day as I have taught you until it heals; for you will not need any other treatment in most cases if the wounds are simple. If you fear that the wound may involve major organs in pain.
Then you should dip soft wool in lukewarm oil or rose ointment and place it around the areas between the groin and armpit; if he feels pain or foulness in his intestines, which often occurs, inject him with a warm black astringent potion.
Especially if the foulness has reached the intestines and become a penetrating wound into it, and know that what was thick from the intestines is easier to heal and what was thin from them is harder to heal.
As for the intestine known as the jejunum, it does not accept healing from any surgery that occurs in it at all, due to its abundance of large veins and thinness of texture and proximity to the nature of nerves. As for if what protruded from the wound was pus and you found it fresh, return it according to how you return the intestines.
If a time has passed and it has become green or black, it should be tightened with a thread above the place from which it has blackened so that bleeding does not occur, for there are veins and arteries in the hemorrhoid, and then what is below that ligature should be cut.
The two ends of the thread should be attached from below the operation outside of it to make it easy for you to pull and remove them when the hemorrhoid falls off and the wound suppurates, mentioning the wound that occurs in the intestine. As for if a rupture occurs in the intestine and it is small.
It may be possible to heal it in some people, for I saw a man who had been wounded in his abdomen by a spear thrust and the wound was on the right side of the stomach. I sutured the wound and it became straight, from which feces and gas came out. I treated him as though I did not expect his recovery. I continued to treat him until he was healed and the area joined together. When I saw that the area had joined together, I feared for the patient that an accident might happen inside him. Nothing bad happened to him at all, and he remained in the best of health. It is true that he eats, drinks, has intercourse, enters the bath, and performs his service, and some experienced people have mentioned that whenever a wound occurs in the intestine and it is small, it should be sutured in this manner.
This is done by taking large-headed ants, then bringing the lips of the wound together, and placing one of the ants with its mouth open on the lips of the wound. When it grips it and tightens its jaws, cut off its head; it will stick and not come undone. Then place another ant near the first one.
Continue to do this with several ants according to the size of the wound, then return it and suture the wound; those heads will remain stuck in the intestine until the intestine heals and recovers, and no harm will befall the patient at all. It is also possible that the intestine can be sutured with fine thread as well.
The one who wants to draw a thread from the animal's flesh after it has entered into the needle, should take the end of this thread from the flesh and draw it out nicely; then he should tie on its end a thin, well-twisted linen thread; then he should insert that thread into the needle. And in it is a thread of the flesh, with which the intestines are sewn together, and then it is returned to the cavity; this kind of stitching with silk and the flesh is only on the path of hope and desire.
But if the tear is large and wide, especially if it is in one of the thin intestines, there is no remedy or cure at all.
Chapter Sixty-Eighty.
On the Treatment of Catarrh and Coryza.
Know that every wound or swelling, if it becomes chronic and does not heal but forms an ulcer from which pus continuously flows without ceasing, is called in general, and whatever organ it may be in, a "naasoor," and we call it catarrh.
It is in fact a complication, an adherent, hard white mass without pain, with a cavity like the cavity of a bird's feather.
And because of this, some people called it "a feather." Sometimes it is moist and continuously discharges pus, and at times the moisture may cease. This moisture can be abundant or scant, thick or thin.
And as I previously mentioned in the division on surgery, every ulcer that does not heal and in which flesh does not grow is due to one of nine causes: one of them being either a deficiency of blood in the body or its corruption, or because there is hard flesh inside it and on its edges, preventing the growth of good flesh. Or because it is full of dirt and impurities; or because the ulcer itself is putrid and the material it discharges is of poor quality; or because the medication is not appropriate for its treatment; or because there has been a pestilence in the town; or because of something particular to the town.
Or because it is full of dirt and impurities; or because the ulcer itself is putrid and the material it discharges is of poor quality; or because the medication is not appropriate for its treatment; or because there has been a pestilence in the town; or because of something particular to the town. As was shown in the city of Saragossa, where the ripening of diseases is difficult and the healing of tumors is slow, naturally, or because it contains one or several bones; all these causes, their signs, and treatments with drugs have been explained in the division.
And I should inform you here of the treatment for fistulas and colds which are treated by handwork, know that fistulas may occur in all parts of the body, some fistulas ending in large veins or arteries or nerves.
Or to the peritoneum or to the intestine or to the bladder or to a rib or to a vertebra of the spine or to a joint of the complex joints composed in many ways such as the hand and foot, and some lead to a major organ or something like that.
So, whatever these fistulas are on this description, they are of the difficult diseases that do not accept treatment and working on them is a hardship and ignorance; If any of the fistulas have not reached the places mentioned, I hoped and desired to treat them.
And I wanted you to reach that knowledge, so take a probe of copper or iron if the fistula passes straight, search it with that. If there is a bend in the fistula, search it with a thin lead probe because lead softens its body.
It will slide upon entering and turn towards the bend, if the fistula has many openings you cannot determine them with a probe, then inject one mouth from its mouths; for the moisture you inject into it will travel towards the other mouths and flow.
Then continue the investigation in any way possible for you to know if there is a bone or nerve, or whether the fistula is deep or shallow, or whether it is one fistula with many openings; stand on all of this to the extent of your ability by skillfully inquiring and its presence with pain when you pinch your hand at that spot.
And such are the signs and causes that occur with a tumor. If you have truly grasped all of this, then proceed to treatment confidently, which is to look and see if it...
If the tumor is clearly near or in a healthy location far from a joint, nerve, artery, vein, or any of the locations I have mentioned to you, then incise the tumor as previously described and remove all purulent material, putrefied flesh, and excess flesh.
Remove any flesh, pustules, or the like that grow in its opening and treat it until it heals. But if the tumor is deep-seated and straight, then you should incise it as deeply as possible and then cleanse it of all putrefied flesh.
Then use a twisted ligature impregnated with caustic medications and insert it into the bottom of the tumor, which you can reach with an iron instrument. Repeat this process several times so that the caustic medicine consumes all remaining corruption in the bottom of the tumor.
Then dress it with ointments that promote the growth of healthy flesh until it heals. If it does not heal in this way, excise it as previously described. If the cause of the tumor is bone and you have confirmed this, then probe and incise it as I have instructed you, unless something prevents you from doing so.
If bone is exposed and there is some corruption or discoloration, scrape it away until the corruption is gone and then treat it with what promotes healing until it heals. If it does not heal and pus continues to form as before…
Know that you have not removed all of the corruption. Examine everything again and meticulously scrape and cleanse it with your utmost effort, then dress it. If it heals, well and good; otherwise, know that this corruption lies deep within. Examine everything again.
Meticulously scrape and cleanse it with your utmost effort. If the corrupted bone is small and can be extracted, then extract it with fine forceps suitable for this purpose.
If there are many bones, draw them all out and do not leave anything behind. If any ligaments stand in your way, and cutting them is not dangerous, cut those ligaments and the flesh if they are present, and free the bones.
If you cannot pull out the bones at once, apply a dressing that will cause it to putrefy and leave it for several days until the flesh and ligaments around those bones become rotten. Keep the wound from closing together along the incision you made during your work.
Rather, if you fear that, place cotton wool soaked in powdered sulfur with oil or cotton wool soaked in Egyptian ointment or one of the green ointments upon it. For if those ligaments putrefy and the bones become loose, it will be easy to pull them out and draw them forth.
But if there is one large bone, such as the shinbone or thighbone and the like, and only the surface has decayed, scrape it thoroughly until that blackness and decay are gone, then bind the wound. But if a large part has decayed and the decay has reached the marrow of the bone, then it must be removed.
Cut it all away to where the decay ends, then treat it so that it heals and closes together. And I shall tell you of a case that befell a man in his leg as an example and aid to your treatment. This man was a youth.
He was about thirty years old and suffered pain in his leg, originating from a cause within his body until the humors reached his leg, causing great swelling and inflammation, with no external cause. Time passed, and with mistakes by physicians, the swelling burst open.
A great deal of matter flowed from it, and his treatment was mishandled until his leg became ulcerated and had many openings, all discharging pus and bodily fluids. A group of physicians treated him for about two years, but none of them were skilled in surgery.
When he came to me I saw his leg and the humors flowing from those openings in a great torrent, and the man had weakened his body and his color had turned yellow. So I inserted a probe into one of those openings and it led to the bone, then I explored all the openings.
I found them leading from one to another on all sides of the leg. So I proceeded and made an incision along one of those openings until I exposed some bone, and I found it corrupted, eaten away, blackened, putrid, and perforated until the hole reached the brain, and I spread out what was revealed to me.
And I was able to remove the corrupted bone, and I believe that there was nothing else in the bone except for that corruption which I cut out and spread, and that I had eradicated it. Then I bound the wound with astringent medicines for a longer time, but it did not heal together. So I returned and exposed the bone again above the first exposure.
I found the corruption connected to the bone, so I spread out what appeared of that corruption as well, then I tried to bind it, but it did not bind or heal together. Then I exposed it again, and I continued to cut away the bone piece by piece, trying to bind it so that it would heal, but it did not heal until I had cut away about a handbreadth of bone.
And I removed it with its marrow, then I bound it with medicines and it healed quickly and completely. This repetition in my work and incision was necessary due to the weakness of the patient and his low tolerance, and out of fear for him dying because he used to experience at all times from excessive vomiting a poor fainting spell.  He recovered completely.
And flesh grew solid in place of the bone and his condition improved in his body, his strength returned, and he was able to move around without any ailment affecting him at all. If a bone has been broken in any part of the body.
It should then be spread out in this manner: take a ligature and tighten it on the end of the protruding bone, and order someone to pull it upwards. Make another ligature of thicker wool than the first one.
Then tie it to the flesh beneath the bone and extend its end so that the flesh is drawn downwards while you expose the flesh from the place you wish to spread, so that the saw does not injure the flesh. Place a plank or board securely under the bone from below.
For if you do this, the saw will not be prevented from cutting the decayed bone, and the sawing should be done slightly above the place of decay so that there is no decay in the cavity of the bone, and it does not appear externally to the senses, thus compelling you to spread it again.
But if the bone is decayed and not protruding, but its parts are connected and the decay is in the middle or part of it, expose the flesh from all sides, then place the board underneath and saw it from the first side where the decay is.
When you have finished sawing it, saw it from the other side; let the sawing be a short distance from the decay, as we said. If the decay is in a joint, cut the decayed joint itself and spread the bone where it connects on the other side.
If the decay is in the connection of two joints, there is no remedy except excision. If the decay is in a metacarpus or metatarsus, the matter is extremely difficult to treat, but you should pare away the decay as it appears to you, and clean and purify it however you can.
By any means possible, provided you do not encounter a vein or nerve; know that saws and cutting instruments for cutting these bones are many, according to the position of the bones, their setting, thickness, thinness, size, and whether they are firm or brittle. Therefore it is necessary to prepare for each type of operation a tool suited to that operation.
Know that the operations themselves may guide you to the type of instrument you need if you have long practice and knowledge of the techniques of this craft, for he who is skilled in the craft and has witnessed various types of diseases. May deduce for himself what instruments are suitable for each disease, and I have depicted for you at the end of this chapter several instruments.
You should make them examples to follow and a measure by which to measure others, God willing, An image of a saw.

Chapter Seventy-Eighth.
On the Amputation of Limbs and the Setting of Bones.
Limbs may become gangrenous either from an external or an internal cause. If you treat this corruption with medication and the treatment is ineffective, and you see that the corruption is progressing in the limb and nothing halts it.
Then you should amputate that limb where the corruption has reached, so that the patient may be saved from death or a great affliction greater than the loss of the limb. A sign that this has occurred is when the limb turns black until you think fire has burned it.
Or it may become gangrenous after turning black, so that the gangrene spreads to the limb and takes hold of the whole body; hasten to amputate it. The same applies if the cause of the corruption is from the sting of certain creatures such as the sea scorpion, snake, centipede, or similar creatures.
If the corruption or sting is at the tip of a finger, amputate the finger and do not delay until the corruption spreads to the hand. Likewise, if it has taken hold of the hand, amputate it from the base of the wrist and do not delay until the corruption spreads to the forearm.
Similarly, if it has taken hold of the arm, amputate the arm at the elbow joint itself. If the corruption spreads and you see that it is moving towards the shoulder, do not amputate the shoulder at all, for this would lead to the death of the patient. Use other methods of treatment according to your ability.
Do the same with the leg: if the corruption takes hold of a toe, amputate the toe at one of its joints; and if it has taken hold of the foot, amputate the whole foot. If it ascends to the knee, amputate the leg at the knee joint.
If the corruption has reached above the knee, there is no recourse but to leave it and resign the patient to death. The method for amputating a limb or setting a bone is to tie a ligature below the place where you intend to cut and another ligature above the place.
The attendant of the lower ligature extends it downwards, and another attendant extends the upper ligature upwards. You then expose the flesh between the ligatures with a broad elevator until all the flesh is exposed, then cut or divide it. It should be covered on all sides with pieces of linen.
So that the saw does not touch the correct spot, causing the patient additional pain and a hot swelling. If bleeding occurs during your work, staunch the area quickly or apply some styptic substances, then return to your treatment until you are finished.
Then bind the injured limb with a suitable bandage and treat it until it is healed. I will give you an example of a man who had this same condition in his leg, which I will describe to you. It happened that he had blackness in his leg accompanied by a burning sensation like fire.
This gangrene first occurred in his toe, and then spread to his entire foot. The man amputated it himself when he saw the gangrene spreading through the limb with the intense pain and burning sensation he was experiencing.
After a long time, he experienced the same gangrene in the index finger of his hand. He came to me and I applied remedies to his hand after cleansing it, but the gangrene did not subside and continued to spread through the other finger.
The gangrene spread throughout his hand, and he asked me to amputate it. I refused, hoping to suppress the gangrene and fearing for his life if I amputated his hand because the man was weak and prone to falling. When he despaired of me, he returned to his town.
I was later informed that he had amputated his entire hand and recovered. I have related this story as an aid in cases of this type of disease and as a guide to be followed.
Chapter Eighty-Eight.
In the treatment of abscesses and the method of injecting them with medicines.
If a tumor occurs in some fleshy organ and its duration is prolonged until it collects a mass, then it bursts or breaks open and all that was inside comes out, leaving the place empty as if it were a vessel.
And the skin upon it is like a rag, having become thin and not having gone so far in its corruption as to affect bone, nerve or ligament; therefore it deserves to be called an abscess and not a fistula except its duration lengthens until corruption affects something of these organs.
Then it is called a fistula or a running sore, and its treatment is to cut away all that skin as it goes around, especially if it has become thin and like a rag, and you are certain that it will not adhere to the place due to its corruption.
But if you hope that the skin will adhere and it has not reached that degree of corruption, and there is a thickness of flesh in the skin, then treat it by injection, which is to look at the abscess; if it is large and the pus that issues from it has a foul smell, inject it with Egyptian medicine.
This is vinegar and oil and honey and asafoetida in equal parts, collected in a vessel and cooked over fire until the medicine thickens and comes to the consistency of honey. Then take what you need from it and thin it with water and honey, and inject it into the abscess, constrict its opening and leave the medicine inside for two hours.
Then squeeze it out; do this for several days until the abscess is cleansed and the foul odor is gone. It may also be injected with ash water if this medicine is not available to you, which is to take the ash of vine wood or oak wood and pour water over it.
Then strain it and inject with it until you are certain that the abscess has been washed. If the patient cannot tolerate Egyptian medicine nor ash water, inject him with water and honey into which a little powdered asafoetida has been mixed.
Or you inject it with honey and drink mixed together, for honey is purifying and washing, and the drink sticks to the hidden place, especially if the drink has an excess of astringency and dryness. If you do this several times and are sure that the hidden place has gone its corruption, then inject it with something that causes flesh to grow in it.
Such as taking some of the palm ointment and dissolving it with rose oil and a constricting drink, or injecting it with some of the other medicines and ointments whose properties we have established in the article on ointments. If the mouth of the hidden place is narrow and does not fit the injector tube.
Then widen it a little with iron, or put in it a wick soaked in Egyptian ointment or a rush ointment until it widens. And if the mouth is also wide, then gather its lips by stitching and leave from it as much as the injector can enter without more.
And if its mouth, from which pus flows, is raised upward, then split it at the lowest point so that the pus flows downward, for pus, if injected into the depths of the hidden place, prevents flesh from growing in it.
If you cannot split the hidden place downwards as you want, then arrange the limb so that the pus flows easily according to what is available to you and ensure that nothing of the pus is retained at all.
As for the medicinal poultices that are placed on the hidden place from the outside and how to put them on, it is to take one of the suitable medicinal poultices and spread it on a linen cloth, let the cloth be as large as it covers the whole hidden place. Then cut with scissors opposite the mouth of the hidden place a hole slightly wider than the mouth of the hidden place, and spread some soft ointments on another cloth according to the size of the hole, and put it on top in a way that is spaced from the mouth of the hidden place so that pus can easily come out. Do not remove the large cloth except after.
For several days, and the one on the mouth of the abscess is the one that you remove at all times so that what has accumulated in it may come out, and to know whether what flows from it is much or little, ripe or unripe.
Also, examine the location of the abscess itself whether its owner feels pain in it or not, and whether there is swelling in it or not. Then if your treatment continues like this and you see that the material coming out on the mouth of the abscess is slight.
Know that the abscess may have adhered or is nearing adhesion, so then solve it on the morrow or in the third day. And when you have solved the wound, replace the cloth with another and renew the ointment. If thin pus comes out from the abscess after a long time, do not despair of its adhesion.
Its healing is near because some people are slow in the growth of flesh in their wounds and others are contrary to that. If, after many days, pus that is unripe comes out from the abscess, know that the abscess has not adhered.
And if its matter is prolonged, increase the drying medications and let them be medications that are moist in their consistency and dry in their strength, such as the palm ointment if excess orpiment has been added to it. And the most effective treatment for this is to take the palm ointment.
And solve it with rose oil and sprinkle on it a well-tempered, aged syrup then knead it with it and use it. You may do something like this if the palm ointment is not available to you; honey if you boil it until it becomes thick and use it. Or take bitter aloes, patience, and sandalwood, crush them all together and let it rest on honey while it is on the fire. Then spread it on a cloth and tighten it on the abscess, or coat it with honey that has been boiled until it thickens, sprinkle medications on it and put a cloth on it and tighten it.
Long zedoary, the root of asphodel, and caraway powder may be used singly or in combination. These are crushed, sifted, and sprinkled on honey as previously described. If the abscess persists and does not heal with this treatment, know that there is corruption within it.
If it has affected the bones or any other solid nerves or ligaments, treat it as described for nasal ulcers.
Chapter Ninety-Eight.
On the treatment of ingrown nails, diseased nails, amputation of supernumerary fingers, and splitting of fused fingers.
An ingrown nail is a large amount of flesh that grows under the toenail or fingernail, and it may grow on any of the fingers. If it is neglected and allowed to continue, it will cause swelling and a hot abscess.
Or it may become corrupted and rotten until it eats away at the base of the nail, and may corrupt it all. The corruption may even reach the bone, causing a foul smell and making the tip of the finger swollen and bluish in color. If you treat it with what we have mentioned in the division, and your treatment is unsuccessful.
You should cut off all the excess flesh growing from under the nail with a knife and then cauterize the wound afterward, for cautery is very beneficial in this case. But if the bone is sound and the nail itself is also sound.
And if the angle containing the nail has pushed the flesh inwards, causing it to be pinched and inflamed, you should place a fine wedge under the angle of the nail that is pinching the flesh and lift it up, carefully cutting away that flesh.
Then apply caustic medicines to the remaining flesh until it is all gone, and treat it with ointments until it heals. But if the corruption has affected the bone, you should cut out that bone and remove it, for it will not heal as long as there is corrupted bone present at all.
If you see suppuration and putrefaction advancing in the finger, cut it off at one of the interphalangeal joints as previously mentioned, then treat it until it heals. If the nail receives a blow or contusion and severe pain arises therein, the affected part should be lanced first.
Then split the nail with a sharp, slanting knife from above downward, being careful not to reach the flesh beneath the nail with the incision, for by doing so you will cause the patient severe pain and lead to the growth of excessive flesh in that place; then treat the area with something that relieves pain.
As for the supernumerary finger that sometimes occurs in some people's hands, it may be entirely fleshy, or it may have bones in some parts, and it may even have a nail, with its growth originating at the base of the joint of some fingers.
Or its growth may be in some interphalangeal joints, and those that grow in some interphalangeal joints do not move, while those that grow at a finger joint may move. Any part of it that is fleshy should be easily cut off; this is done by cutting it at its base with a wide knife.
As for that which grows at the base of a joint, its treatment is difficult, so abandon cutting it. As for that which grows in the finger at one of the interphalangeal joints, its flesh should first be cut circularly down to the bone, then the bone should be spread apart with one of those saws suitable for that purpose.
Then treat it until it heals. As for the adhesion that sometimes occurs between the fingers, this happens quite often and may be congenital or result from a wound or burn, etc. It should be incised.
So that the fingers return to their natural form, then place between them a wick or a thin shard soaked in rose oil so that it does not adhere quickly and separate them, or put a thin sheet of lead between them until the area heals properly.
And if some of the fingers are joined to the palm, then cut that union according to what seems suitable and corrects the shape of the limb.
Chapter Ninety.
On Cutting Arteries.
Arteries are twisted, thick veins filled with dark-colored blood that occur in most parts of the body and are most common in the legs, especially in porters, carriers, and laborers. It is first necessary to use shaking of the body from black bile many times forcefully.
Then let him be bled by cupping, but its treatment with iron is of two kinds: one is to cut and extract the black blood, and the other is to cauterize the vein and extract it all. As for cutting it, it should be done in this manner: first soak the leg in hot water.
Until the thick, turbid blood dissolves, then bind the patient's leg from above his thigh to below his knee with a turban, then cut the vein in one place or two places or three wide incisions.
Then draw out the black blood with your hand from below the leg upward and from above downward until a quantity of blood that you see as sufficient or that the patient's strength can bear comes out, then bind it up and order him to avoid foods that produce black bile, and repeat vomiting and cupping.
When the veins become full and this harms the patient, as for cauterizing it, it is done in this manner: shave the patient's leg if it has a lot of hair, then take him into a bath and soak his leg in hot water until it reddens and the vein swells or he engages in strenuous exercise.
If he does not have access to a bath until the limb is heated, then cut the skin opposite the vein in a lengthwise incision, either at its end near the knee or below it near the heel; then open the skin with forceps and strip away the vein from all sides until it appears to touch.
And when it appears, you see it reddish purple; and when the skin is removed, you also see it as if it were a tendon. Then insert a ligature beneath it until, when it rises above the skin, suspend it with a blind smooth snare, then make another incision near that one, to the extent of three fingers.
Then flay the skin from off the vein until it appears, then raise it with a ligature as you did before and suspend it with another snare as you did at first, then make another incision or many incisions if you need to do so.
Then draw it out and cut it at the end of the incision near the heel, then pull it out and draw it until it comes out of the second incision, then pull it up to the incision above it, and do this until you draw it from the third incision highest of all the incisions until it all comes out, then cut it.
But if it does not yield to drawing and pulling, insert a needle with a strong doubled thread and tie it and pull it, then insert a ligature beneath it and twist it with your hand in every direction until it comes out, and be careful that it does not break; for if it breaks, it will be very difficult for you to draw it out.
And harm will befall the patient from it; and if you draw it all out, place wool soaked in syrup and rose oil or oil upon the site of the operations and treat it until it heals. If the varicose vein is twisted and convoluted.
And was not straight as we said, it should be incised at each side of its convolutions and points of twisting, then hung by snares until you draw it all out; and be careful when you incise it that you do not cut the vein or injure it, for it will be difficult for you to draw it out if you do so.
Image of the instrument with which the varicose vein is incised:
Image of the blind cautery.
It does not have a groove like other cauteries and is not sharp at the end so as not to injure the vessel; it is thick and curved, smooth because if it were thin, it would cut the vessel with its sharpness. It should have the thickness we mentioned.
Chapter ninety-one.
On cutting a vein.
This vein is generated in the legs in hot countries like Hejaz and Arab lands, and in hot, arid and infertile lands, and it may be generated in other parts of the body besides the legs. It is generated by putrefaction that occurs under the skin.
As it happens inside living bodies, maggots, and the pulp of a fruit called gourd and maggots that are generated between the skin and flesh, the sign of the beginning of this vein is that a severe inflammation occurs in the leg and then the place puffs up, and then the vein begins to ooze out from that puffed-up spot.
As if it were the root of a plant or animal, and when its tip appears, it should be wrapped around a small piece of lead weighing from one to two dirhams, tied tightly, and left hanging from the leg.
Whenever anything comes out of it, wrap it in the lead and tie it. If it grows long, cut off some of it and wrap the rest. Do not cut it from its origin before it has all come out because if you do, it will shrink and go into the flesh, causing a boil, putrefaction in the place and a bad ulcer.
Therefore, it should be pulled and tugged little by little until it comes out entirely and nothing is left in the body. It may happen that this vein grows to a length of five or ten cubits in some people.
I have heard that it came out of a man twenty cubits long. If it breaks off while you are treating it, insert a probe into the hole and press it down along the body with a long bandage until all its contents are emptied, and try to heal the place with medicines for several days.
Then treat it with the treatment of tumors, and this vein may have many branches, especially if it appears in the joint of the leg or in the leg itself, so it will have many mouths and a branch will come out of each mouth, so treat it as we mentioned in the division and what preceded.
Chapter Ninety-Second.
Incision for worms generated under the nail, called bovine disease.
This disease is called bovine disease in some of our countries because cattle are often affected by it; it is a small worm that generates between the skin and flesh and crawls throughout the whole body, ascending and descending.
It becomes apparent to the touch as it crawls from one limb to another until it pierces wherever it has pierced the skin, creating a place where it comes out and forming it from putrefaction of some humors, as it is also subject to worms and animals, and pumpkin seeds in the abdomen.
And it is expected that its harm is that if it crawls in the body and rises to the head and reaches the eye, it may open there and come out, thus destroying the eye; this often occurs. If you want to treat it and remove it, it must be done when it is crawling and apparent to the touch.
You should tighten above and below it with a strong ligature, then cut it open and remove it. If it has sunk into the flesh and you cannot find it, apply heat to the spot with fire until it is burned. As we said, the greatest harm it can cause is to destroy the eye.
If you see it has reached the head near the eye, tighten below it on the forehead with a strong ligature, then cut it open and remove it. The patient should take medicines that loosen foul humors and abstain from foods that cause putrefaction.
Chapter Ninety-Third.
On incision for the ailment known as nafir.
This disease which is called in our country nafir is a pain which appears in some organs and then moves from one organ to another, and I have seen it as I will describe to you.
I was called to a sick woman in some outlands, and when I uncovered her arm, I saw a slight swelling in the tendon of the arm. When I stayed for an hour, I saw that swelling crawl along the vein like a worm crawling upward to her elbow as quickly as could be, like mercury.
Whenever it moved from one place to another, the pain would disappear from that location and settle in the elbow. Then I sat for an hour and it flowed throughout her whole body until it reached the other arm. Then she told me that it circled her entire body as she had seen.
I was amazed at the speed of its movement from one organ to another. I had never seen this disease with my own eyes in this way before, although I have seen groups of people who experience pain moving from one organ to another. But I had never seen it with my own eyes as I saw it in this woman.
And I concluded that it must be because the woman was from the desert, with a dry body and visible veins, that this moving wind appeared to sensation. It follows that it would not appear to this extent in people of comfort and those with moist bodies and hidden veins.
Therefore, if you wish to treat it and the sufferer feels pain and it appears to you as we have described, bind tightly above and below it quickly and make an incision over it so that this trapped wind is released and the area is emptied.
But if you do not see it with your own eyes, treat it by loosening the body and using what expels winds and disperses them like loveage, spikenard, and similar medications.
Chapter Ninety-Fourth.
On the Extraction of Arrows.
Arrows may differ according to their types and according to the positions in which they are located in the body. As for their difference according to their types, some are large and some are small; some are hollow glass and some are solid glass; some have three angles and some have four angles.
Some have tongues and some have splinters. As for those that are according to the organs in which they are located, they are of two types: either the organs are major hollow organs such as the brain, heart, liver, lungs, kidneys, intestines, bladder, and the like. So when an arrow falls into one of these organs and the signs of death which I will describe to you later appear, then you should avoid removing that arrow from it, for death will befall its owner in most cases. And when these bad signs do not appear to you and the arrow has not been concealed in the depths of the organ, then remove it and treat the wound. And among the signs of the brain, if an arrow strikes it and penetrates the bone and wounds the dura mater on the brain, is that it will experience severe headache, headache and dizziness.
And when these bad signs do not appear to you and the arrow has not been concealed in the depths of the organ, then remove it and treat the wound. And among the signs of the brain, if an arrow strikes it and penetrates the bone and wounds the dura mater on the brain, is that it will experience severe headache, headache and dizziness. And redness in the eyes and redness of the tongue, convulsions, confusion, vomiting sometimes, and blood may come out of the nostrils or ears, and speech may be interrupted and the voice lost, and a white moisture resembling porridge will come out from the wound site, and it will flow like meat water.
If these signs appear to you, refrain from treating the patient and removing the arrow if it has not already come out. As for the signs of the arrow if it hits the heart and is close to the left breast, he will feel as if it has been inserted into something solid, not something empty.
And perhaps the arrow will have a movement resembling the pulse, and black blood will flow from the wound, followed by coldness of the limbs, cold sweat, and fainting. Know that death is inevitable. And a sign of an arrow if it wounds the lung is the release of frothy blood from the wound.
And the vessels following the neck swell and the color of the patient changes and he breathes with a loud breath, and requests to inhale cold air. If the arrow has indeed struck the diaphragm in the chest, then it will be close to the small ribs and the breathing will be great with severe pain.
And he sighs and all the limbs of the shoulders move. And if the arrow has indeed struck the liver, severe pain will follow that and blood resembling the liver in redness will come out from the wound. And if the arrow has indeed struck the stomach, perhaps something undigested from food will come out from the wound.
And its matter is apparent. And if the arrow has indeed struck the abdomen and lodged there and something of excrement came out from the wound or pus or an intestine that has been pierced, then there is no hope for his treatment or removal of the arrow. If the arrow has indeed struck the bladder and urine comes out.
And something protruded from it to the outside and the pain intensified upon the patient, then know that he is lost. As for the rest of the organs such as the face, eye, throat, shoulder, upper arm, vertebrae of the back, collarbone, thigh, leg and so forth from those organs they have mostly been spared.
Provided that the arrow did not encounter a vein or nerve and the arrow was not poisoned, and I will tell you of some of what I have witnessed concerning these arrows so that you may deduce your treatment from it. It is that an arrow had struck a man in the fleshy part of his eye at the root of his nose.
So I removed it for him from the other side under the cartilage of the ear and he recovered, and no harm occurred to his eye. And I removed another arrow for a Jew that had struck him in the cartilage of his eye under the lower eyelid and the arrow had become hidden, and I only reached a small part of it that was stuck into the wood.
And it was a large arrow of the strong, composite arrows, square-shaped iron, smooth with no ears and the Jew recovered, and no ill occurrence happened to his eye. And I removed another arrow from the throat of a Christian and the arrow was Arabic.
And he who had two ears cut through between the jugular veins and it had lodged in his throat, I softened it until I extracted it, and the Christian was saved and healed, and I extracted another arrow for a man who had been struck in his belly and we estimated that he would die from it.
When thirty days or so had passed and nothing changed in his condition, I cut the arrow open and extracted it, and he was healed and nothing bad happened to him. I saw a man who had been struck by an arrow in his back and the wound had closed over it.
After seven years, the arrow came out at the base of his thigh, and I saw a woman who had been struck by an arrow in her belly and the wound closed over it. The arrow remained, and nothing changed in her condition, nor did she find any harm in any of her natural actions.
And I saw another man who had been struck by an arrow in the face, and the wound closed over it, and he did not find much pain from it. And there are many like this, and I extracted an arrow for one of the sultan's commanders that had been lodged in the middle of his nose, leaning slightly to the right side.
The entire arrow was concealed, and I was called to treat him three days after the arrow had struck, and I found the arrow wound very narrow, so I widened it with a thin probe, and did not feel any resistance, but he felt a throbbing pain under his right ear from the incision.
I thought that throbbing might be from the tip of the arrow, so I dressed the area with a bandage that had drawing power and ripeness hoping that the area would swell and a mark of the arrow would appear, so I cut him open, but nothing happened in the area to indicate that the arrow had reached that spot.
So I continued with the bandage for many days, but nothing happened, and during that time the wound closed, and the patient remained miserable from having it removed until one day he felt the arrow inside his nose and told me the news, so I put a sharp, corrosive medicine on the wound for many days.
Until it opened and I probed it, feeling the slender point of the arrow stuck in the wood. Then I widened the wound with that sharp medicine until the point of the arrow appeared clearly to our sight, and it remained with me for a period of about four months.
Then, when the wound widened and I was able to enter the forceps into it, I drew and moved it, but it did not respond to coming out. I continued to treat it gently and maneuver it with various instruments until one day I grasped it firmly with all the forceps as will be shown in the figure at the end of this chapter.
And physicians used to rule that the cartilage of his nose could not be joined, but I joined it and the wound healed, and the patient recovered completely without any pain at all, and I tell you how some arrows are removed so that this can serve as a measure and guide for what I have not mentioned.
For the parts of this craft and their details cannot be grasped by description nor encompassed by a book, but rather the skillful craftsman measures the small against the large and what is present against what is absent and infers new work and a new instrument at strange emergencies.
If I descend from this craft, I say that arrows are only removed from the organs in which they have become lodged in two ways: either by drawing them from the place where they entered, or from the opposite direction, and those that come out from where they entered.
If the arrow is prominent in a place of flesh, it should be drawn and removed. If it does not respond to coming out at the time it occurred, you should leave it for days until the flesh around it rots, making it easy to draw and remove. The same applies if it lodges in bone.
And if it does not respond to coming out, leave it for days as well and try drawing and moving it every day, for it will come out. If it still does not respond after days, you should pierce around the arrow in the same bone from all sides with a fine awl until it widens for the arrow, then draw and remove it.
If an arrow has penetrated the skull and entered one of the ventricles of the brain, and some of those symptoms which I have mentioned to you appear in the wound, then desist from drawing out the arrow and leave it until his condition is clarified after a few days.
