Then proceed to draw out and extract the arrow; but if it is deeply embedded and does not yield to traction, use trephines around the arrow as I have described to you, then treat the area until it heals. But if the arrow has become concealed in some part of the body. And is beyond feeling, then search for it with a probe; if you feel it, draw it out with some instrument suitable for the purpose; but if you are unable to do so because of the narrowness of the wound and the depth of the arrow, and there is neither bone nor nerve nor blood vessel in that place.
Then make an incision to widen the wound so that you can grasp the arrow and extract it; if it has two wings, free the flesh adhering to them on every side by any means possible. If you are unable to free the flesh at the points of the wings, then break them off and split them until you are able to do so.
And if you attempt to extract the arrow from any location, use the twist of your hand with forceps in all directions until it is freed, and exercise the utmost gentleness lest the arrow break, for this will make it difficult for you to draw it out. If you are unable to do so at that time.
Leave it for a few days until the flesh around it decays, then return to it; for it will then be easier to extract. If bleeding occurs, use the remedies we have mentioned in the chapter on that subject, and take every precaution against cutting an artery, nerve or tendon, and use ingenuity.
In any case, you can remove the arrow, and let it be gently, cautiously, and steadily as I have described to you, and you should use when drawing out the arrow that the patient should assume the same position they were in when the arrow struck them, for this is the most appropriate.
If you cannot do that, then use whatever position is possible. As for the arrow that exits from the opposite side, it will either have something protruding outward or you will find the tip of the arrow close to the skin by feeling and see it emerging.
Make an incision on it, and let the incision be as wide as the arrowheads fit, then draw it out, for it will easily come out. If it is stuck in a bone, twist your hand so that the arrow affects the bone and widens itself, then draw it out.
Otherwise, leave it for a few days and return to it until it comes out. If the arrow's shaft is still in it, push it with it; if the shaft has fallen off, and you wish to use pushing, insert a hollow instrument into it so that its cavity enters the tail of the arrow and then push it with it.
If the arrow is hollow, push it with an instrument that enters into that cavity, for the arrow will easily come out this way. If the arrow is poisoned, you should cut out all the flesh where the poison has gone, if possible, then treat it with what is suitable for that.
If the arrow is in the chest or abdomen or bladder or side, and it is close to what can be felt with a probe, and you are able to make an incision on it, then make an incision and be careful not to cut a vein or nerve, and take it out.
Then suture the wound if it needs it, then treat it until it heals. Picture of the arrowheads with which arrows are shot:
Their tips are shaped like a bird's beak, made as if they were a rasp so that when you grip an arrow or something else, it will not let go. They can be made in large, small, and medium sizes, all according to the size of the arrow, the width, and narrowness of the wound.
Image of a hollow cannon.
Image of a closed cannon.
This is closed at the end like an arrow shaft to make it easy to insert into the hollow arrow and push it.
Chapter Ninety-Fifth.
On Phlebotomy.
The veins that are customarily phlebotomized in the body are thirty veins, including sixteen veins in the head: the two temporal veins behind the ears, known as the temporals, and the two arteries in the temples that are visible, and the two veins in the eye sockets, known as the viewers.
And the vein that stands in the middle of the forehead, and the vein at the end of the nose, and the two jugular veins in the neck, and the two veins in the upper lip of the mouth, and the two veins in the lower lip, these veins are known as the jaw veins, and the two veins under the tongue.
As for the veins that are phlebotomized in the arm and hand, they are five veins: one of them is the cephalic vein, which is on the lateral side, and it is commonly called the head vein; and the basilic vein, which is the middle vein, composed of a branch of the basilica and a branch of the cephalic vein, and it is commonly called the body vein.
And the basilica, which is located on the medial side, is also called the axillary vein, and it is commonly called the abdominal vein; and the brachial artery, which is located on the elbow, and the one that adheres to it, and the one that appears above the thumb clearly, and the median cubital.
It has two branches, and there are three veins in the leg and foot: one is under the knee bend on the lateral side; the second is the saphenous vein, located at the heel on the medial side; and the femoral vein, located at the heel on the lateral side.
And in the other leg there are three veins like them, but as for the two veins behind the ears, stopping them is beneficial for chronic colds, migraine, ringworm, and foul chronic head sores, and I will describe how to stop them.
You should shave the patient's head and rub the back of their head in the location of the veins with a rough cloth, rubbing thoroughly. Then, constrict the patient's neck with a turban until the veins appear behind their ears in the lower parts of the head.  Then feel for them with your finger; where you feel the pulse beneath your finger, mark that spot with ink.
Then take a sharp scalpel, the one known as a needle-holder, and insert it under the vein in the skin until the scalpel reaches the bone. Then raise your hand with the vein and skin upwards and cut the vein and skin off with a cleanly severed cut. The length of the cut should be equal to two joined fingers.
Or thereabouts, then release the amount of blood you desire and bind them with bandages, leaving them until they heal. I have previously discussed cutting and cauterizing them in the beginning of this book. As for the two arteries in the temples, stopping them is beneficial for chronic migraine, severe headache, persistent ophthalmia, and acute profuse lacrimation directed towards the eyes.
I will describe how to stop them for you: constrict the patient's neck with a turban until the arteries clearly appear and their pulse is felt beneath your finger. Then mark them with ink, and lift the skin from above the artery upwards with your middle finger.
Insert the needle-holder scalpel from below and raise the artery upwards, cutting it off as you did with the other veins. Release the amount of blood needed, then loosen the constriction on the patient's neck and press your finger on the artery for an hour.
Then you put cotton and a bandage on it, and tighten it securely from above, and leave it until it heals. Their mention, separation, and ligature have already been mentioned in the beginning of the book. As for cupping the forehead vein, its benefit is after cupping the vertex for chronic facial conditions such as ringworm, ulcers, and erysipelas.
And as for how to cup it, as I describe to you, you strangle the patient's neck with a turban until the vein appears, then you take the instrument called the hatchet, and this is its image:.
You place the fork coming out of the head of the hatchet on the same vein and strike it from above with a comb or something similar, and leave the blood to flow in the amount you want, then loosen the patient's stranglehold and tighten him until he heals.
And it may be opened with a wide spatula, but it should not have a sharp end like other spatulas, but rather be slightly wide at the end, and you dissect it on the bend because the bone is nearby, and perhaps the spatula will break if it is thin.
As for the two veins in the temple, their benefit is for eye conditions from scabies, redness, ophthalmia, and facial diseases. As for how to cup them, it is that the patient tightens his neck with a turban, then you cup them while standing above his head, and let the cupping be on a slight bend lengthwise with a small spatula slightly wide.
For the place has no flesh, indeed if the spatula is thin at the end it may break, then you send as much blood as you need and put cotton on them and tighten them for one night, then loosen them. As for cupping the nose vein, it is beneficial for acute fever.
And for severe headaches, and for facial conditions such as the red lupus that appears on the nose, especially if it is chronic. The way to cup it is to have the patient tighten his neck, then hold his nose with your left hand and take a thin, long spatula.
You plunge it into the middle of the nose itself between the cartilage, on a straight line because the vein does not appear to the sense there; then the blood will emerge from it immediately. You should press your hand with the knife a little and draw from the blood what you need, then tie it for a night, for it will quickly become firm.
As for the veins in the testicles, their excision is beneficial for shortness of breath and the beginning of leprosy and black diseases that appear on the surface of the skin, such as black vitiligo, ringworm, and foul sores. The way to excise them is for the patient to be bound under them with a cord around his neck and for the practitioner to stand above the patient's head.
The patient sits on a chair, and then you cut the vein lengthwise, making a wide incision, then you draw from it a moderate amount of blood or according to what you see is needed. Then you do the same to the other vein, and then you untie the bandage.
You tie the veins moderately so that the patient does not choke, and leave them until the next day, for the wound will heal. As for the temporal veins, their excision is beneficial after cupping the head, as it is helpful against sores in the mouth, gum disease, foul ulcers, lip fissures, and foul sores.
Those that are in the nose and around it, and the way to excise them is for you to seat the patient in front of you and tie their neck with a turban then turn back their lips and look at the two veins, one of which you see on the right side of the lip and the other on the left, and identify them by their blackness.
And note that around them are thin, black veins, so you cut them off completely. If it is difficult for you and you do not know which ones they are, aim to cut the largest and most visible ones, and do the same with the two veins in the lower lip.
As for the veins under the tongue, it is beneficial to stop them after cupping the temples for the fistulas that occur in the throat from diseases of the mouth and jaw, and how to stop them is to have the patient sit before you facing the sun, raise their tongue, and look under the tongue on one side for a vein and on the other side for a vein, and their color tends towards black.
Cut them and preserve, do not dwell on cutting them, for beneath them are arteries; perhaps bleeding may occur from those arteries. As for the three veins that are cupped at the elbow, they are the ones commonly cupped in people, and cupping them is done in two ways.
Either by inserting a wide lancet or olive-shaped lancet into the vein, or by making an incision with a scalpel lancet, which is called a "neshl" [and this is its image], an image of the wide lancet.
It should be wide as you see so that it is suitable for opening the hollow, full, prominent, and thick veins that contain thick blood like tar, and this is an image of the olive-shaped lancet.
This lancet is narrower and has a thinner point, suitable for cupping thin veins that contain light yellowish blood, and this is an image of the neshl lancet.
This neshl is suitable for incision and comes in various widths and thicknesses according to the width and narrowness of the veins, and one can deduce this from others; it is well-known among craftsmen.
As for the basilica vein, which is one of these three veins, it is beneficial to cup it because it draws blood from the ailments that occur under the throat and neck, extending to the chest and abdomen. The phlebotomist should be cautious when cupping it and keep a watchful eye on the neck; for underneath it is an artery, and if they err and insert the lancet too deeply, they will cut that artery.
If bleeding occurs, the incision should not be made with a suturing knife but should be cut open with a lancet. If the ligature is not clearly visible, it should be avoided and another one sought or some of its parts requested.
Or you can incise the place of the brachial vein, for it is visible and cut it open with a lancet as we have said. If you wish to cut it exactly, you should feel the spot before tightening the arm so that you recognize the location of the pulse, then mark it with ink.
Then tie the arm and cut the vein, a slanting incision with a lancet as we have said, taking care that the stroke falls at a distance from the location of the artery. When you see, upon tightening the ligature, swelling in the spot that you marked with ink, then know that the swelling is the distension of the artery, so avoid it.
If you see the blood, when making the incision, pulsate like a boy's urine and it is thin and red, know that it is arterial blood. At that point, hasten to place your finger on it for a long time, then remove your finger; if the bleeding stops, which it often does, tighten the arm.
Leave it and warn the patient not to neglect it, let him keep his neck still and not move it for several days until he recovers. If the bleeding does not stop and overwhelms you and no medicine is available at that time, amputate the artery if it appears to you; its ends will contract and the bleeding will stop.
Or take a pistachio shell, split it and take one half and tie it tightly over the vein with a ligature and bandages until the next day. If the bleeding stops, well and good; otherwise, treat it as previously mentioned, using astringents to stop the bleeding. Cutting off its blood supply is not difficult in most cases.
Because the wound is small and the ligature in the arm is effective, inform him of this. As for the basilic vein, its incision is beneficial because it draws blood from the upper head and lower body since, as we have said, it is composed of a branch of the cephalic vein and a branch of the basilica vein.
And the one who is to be cauterized should be on the neck from which he is cauterized, for if there is a nerve beneath it, if the cautery needle penetrates further and reaches the nerve, numbness will occur in it, making its healing difficult, and it may not heal at all. This nerve often appears to the sense.
If it is hidden in some people and is thin and does not become apparent, you should make your incision for him a lengthwise cut with a knife and avoid the nerve as much as possible. If the vein is between two nerves, cut the vein lengthwise. As for the temporal vein, its benefit from being cauterized is that it draws blood from the head.
And it is beneficial for eye diseases, and in this vein especially, if you wish to cauterize it, it should be done with a puncture of the olive-shaped cautery or the wide, windpipe-shaped cautery because it is the safest of all veins because there is neither an artery nor a nerve beneath it.
However, when cauterizing, you must avoid the head of the muscle with the cautery and aim for the soft spot. It will not harm it if it is not hit in the first strike to repeat the cautery several times, unless it may swell in some people if it is not cauterized in the first strike.
But that swelling will not harm it at all. As for the manner of cauterization and its effects, and what should precede in its repair, first you must know that cauterization is either used for preserving health only.
Or it is used for diseases. The cauterization used for preserving health and maintaining it, and for protecting against the occurrence of diseases should be in one of the three veins at the elbow, namely the temporal, the ulnar, and the basilic, and the cauterization should be in early spring when signs of fullness appear.
And the cauterization should be on Sunday or Tuesday after three hours of daylight have passed. As for cauterization used for diseases, it does not have a limited time but can be done whenever necessary, day or night.
And at every hour and in every time, and it should not be cupped the boys until they reach fourteen years nor the elderly who have exceeded sixty years, so if someone intends to cup for any reason.
His intestines should be cleansed before cupping with a gentle enema if there is much retained filth so that it does not draw into the veins during cupping from the intestine, and bad odor harmful to the main organs, and one should not cup the fasting, intoxicated, or drunk until that passes from them.
And cupping should also be avoided after defecation, vomiting, and diarrhea and excessive intercourse, fatigue, exercise, sleeplessness, fasting, and everything that weakens a physical or mental matter; then you look at thinning the humors before that if the blood is thick.
With foods, drinks, and medications if possible; then he enters the bath unless something prevents him or practices some exercise to thin the blood and makes his cupping in the middle of the day as we said and aims to free his chest that day from all bad mental states.
Such as worries, anger, fear, and all bodily conditions such as excessive fatigue and sexual intercourse and the like; and his gathering place prepares things that people have been accustomed to using from various kinds of perfumes, incense, amusements and the like, each person according to what he can.
Then the cupper sits on a cushion that is higher than the cushion on which the person being cupped sits, then he takes out his arm and the cupper rubs it with his hand twice or three times; then he tightens the bandage with the leech holder and twists it twice or three times, let the tightness be moderate.
Because tightness, when it is not moderate, either prevents the flow of blood by excessive tightening or prevents the flow of blood by relaxation; then after tightening, the person being cupped should rub both his hands together with both palms.
Until the veins swell and become apparent to the touch, then one wipes the corrupt scalpel with a little of old oil, especially, and places the index finger of his left hand on the same vein below the place he wishes to cut slightly so that the vein does not retreat and avoid the blow. For some veins are like a chord, they retreat when cut, and some are full of air so that when the scalpel is placed upon them they sink beneath it and deceive the cutter, and he does not open the vein; and if he does open it, then it will only be a narrow opening.
Therefore it is necessary for the cutter to be sure and take his time in all these matters, then he lowers the scalpel; if the vein opens at that time, well and good; otherwise, he tries again a little below or above that place hastily, unless the place has become swollen.
But if there is swelling or the patient is frightened, leave him for a day or two and do not tighten the bandage, for it may bring on a hot boil, and he should not enter the bath; then he may repeat the phlebotomy if he wishes. If the scalpel was inserted and the opening was small and the blood flowed thin...
And if you fear that the quantity of blood you desire will not come out, reinsert the scalpel into the same hole gently, keeping it straight, and widen the opening a little; do this quickly before the place swells, for in many people the place swells when the opening is small.
If you see it has swollen, do not work on it at all; it will not help you. Put some of the sediment from oil on it, for it facilitates the flow of blood and is better in this place than oil itself or any other unguents.
Use the sediment from oil in all your phlebotomies of veins when the flow of blood is difficult; the "Faruq" remedy and the skhznaya may also do this, if one of them is placed on the place, for it will thin and dissolve the blood if it is thick.
If a large tumor occurs in the place of venesection, which often happens, especially to those who have never been bled or whose vein was opened only slightly, then quickly apply a sponge soaked in lukewarm water and salt to the tumor and bind it for an hour; it will dissolve.
This should be done after all the blood has exited from the vein itself or another vein. If some blackness or greenness remains in the place after several days, it does no harm. If you wish, apply something of patience and myrrh dissolved upon it.
Or something of the juice of fumitory and the like. Often a tumor and swelling occurs at the venesection of the basilica vein; place your hand upon it, and if it feels soft when you press it, then that swelling is benign. Be careful not to put anything of what we have mentioned on it.
For it may bleed arterially, but compress it with something constricting so that the area solidifies, and then treat it with all other remedies until it heals. It should be drawn from each person according to their strength and the predominant color of their blood.
If the blood is black, let it flow until it reddens; and if you see it thick, let it flow until it thins; and if it is sharp, let it flow until its sharpness subsides. One who is full and strong and needs to have a large amount of blood extracted at once
should have a wide venesection with a broad lancet; but the opposite is true for one who is weak. Blood should be extracted in stages, and the incision should be narrow. The best instrument to use for venesection is a sharp, well-honed, split lancet, not a puncturing one.
This type of venesection is safe from hemorrhage and severing nerves; it is better and safer than venesection by cross-section or length. One who is accustomed to fainting during venesection should be given, before the venesection, some bread soaked in pomegranate water or syrup of vinegar.
If he is melancholic and blood has been let from him three or four times, and his temperament is cold, let him take before phlebotomy bread soaked in the syrup of violets, or in the syrup of honey sweetened with roses, or in fragrant rose water.
If syncope occurs during phlebotomy and is due to the loss of much blood, he should be given broth and thin rose water to drink, and aromatic treatments with ambergris should be used; his chest should be rubbed with it. He should also use all that we have mentioned in the chapter on syncope in the section on divisions.
For him who wants to ease his arm and loosen his blood again, it is necessary for one whose phlebotomy resulted in much vomiting and who is weak, that the blood be let a little at a time, according to his strength, on consecutive days.
And for him who wants to ease his arm and loosen his blood again, and whose body is strong, let him do so after seven or nine hours from his first phlebotomy. And for him who wishes to draw the blood from his body to the opposite side to which it has inclined, he should be cupped on the second or third day.
And for him in whom there is much blood, which has become hot and inflamed, and has caused fever, all the blood should be let out at once, a large quantity should be extracted, and the incision should be widened until syncope occurs, after you have ascertained that all the conditions for phlebotomy are met. And place your hand on his pulse while the blood is flowing so that death does not occur in syncope; for this often happens if the phlebotomist is ignorant and neglectful. And it should not be done if you wish to loosen the arm and the blood again.
If the mouth of the vein has become closed and it is difficult for the blood to flow, do not press or twist hard, for that is very bad indeed. Either leave him until he can be phlebotomized again, or remove with a scalpel blade what has coagulated in the mouth of the vein.
Or apply to it something of salt that has been dissolved in water, or apply to it something of the electuary faruq or shakzanaya, and compress it gently until the blood comes out, if the vein had become swollen leave it.
And do not touch it until the swelling subsides, if necessity calls for releasing the blood again it is obligatory to do so, either by making an incision above that place or by making an incision in the other arm or in another vein.
As for bleeding the arm's tendon, it is bled instead of the radial and ulnar arteries if they are not found or are concealed because it is composed of them, and the way to bleed it is for the patient to immerse their hand in hot water until the sinew reddens and the vein appears clearly then tie a bandage above it with moderate tightness.
Then bleed the vein slightly askew, neither in width nor length, and let the opening be wide, and make your incision a little above the wrist joint, if the blood does not flow, return the hand to the bowl of hot water and let the blood flow into the water.
Until you reach your need, if it is summertime you may dispense with returning the hand to the hot water, and what makes blood flow into the hot water most in wintertime, and bleeding this vein is safer than all other veins. Because there is no artery or nerve beneath it, and as for bleeding the basilica vein from the right hand, it is beneficial for liver ailments, and the way to bleed it is to tie the wrist with a bandage or with your hand after immersing it in hot water until the vein swells.
Because there is no artery or nerve beneath it, and as for bleeding the basilica vein from the right hand, it is beneficial for liver ailments, and the way to bleed it is to tie the wrist with a bandage or with your hand after immersing it in hot water until the vein swells. And it becomes very apparent to the touch, then bleed it slightly askew, and even if you cut it completely it will not harm it, but be careful not to insert your hand deeply with the lancet because there is a finger nerve underneath, and the spot is bare of flesh, then return the hand to the hot water.
And leave it so that the blood flows through it. If you do not put it in hot water, the blood will congeal in the mouth of the vein and cease to flow. And when you have drawn out as much blood as is needed, apply ointment and salt to the vein so that it does not heal quickly.
And the same should be done with every narrow vein. As for the benefit of cutting it from the left hand, it is beneficial for splenic diseases and you do the same in cutting it as you did in the second vein. As for cutting the saphenous vein, its benefit is for diseases that are in the lower part of the body, such as uterine diseases.
And retention of menstruation, kidney diseases, chronic ulcers of the thighs and legs, and other such diseases. And the way to cut it is for him to put his foot in hot water and rub it until the veins become prominent then tie above the ankle joint with a ligature and the vein is located at the heel visible towards the big toe.
And many branches diverge from it on the face of the leg, so cut it in the widest branch or at the heel where it joins, for this is better and safer. If you cut it on the face of the leg, be careful of the nerves underneath.
Make your cut with a slant as if you were amputating it, and the lancet should be sharp. If the blood does not flow freely, put his leg in hot water and let the blood flow through it until it is emptied. If the phlebotomist misses the vein at first.
Let him make the incision a little higher, for the location is safe and there is no danger to be feared if you are careful of the nerve as we have said. The same applies to the saphenous vein on the other leg. As for the vein of Venus, its location is as we have said, at the heel on the outer side.
And the benefit of cutting it is for pain in the hip if this is caused by hot blood. To cut it, have the patient enter a bath and quickly bind his leg from the hip to just above the heel with a thin long bandage.  For it will not be visible except in this way.
If it appears, excise it in any state that keeps you. Either distort it which is better or amputate it or make an incision upon it if its place is sound and in most people it is very slight indeed. If you do not find it...
If it does not appear to the senses at all, excise some of it, which appears on the back of the foot toward the little toe and next to it, preserve the nerves and send away as much blood as you desire.
Then relieve the tension and place cotton on the excision site and bind the area, for it will quickly heal.
Chapter Ninety-Sixth.
On Cupping and How to Use It.
Cupping instruments may be of horns, wood, copper or glass. Cupping is done in two ways: one with a condition and the extraction of blood, the other without a condition.
And this cupping without a condition is done in two ways.
And the cupping of the shoulders, and the cupping of the coccyx which is on the back of the tailbone, and the cupping of the arms which are in the middle of the arms, and the cupping of the legs, and the cupping of the armpits.
Cupping draws blood from the fine, scattered veins in the flesh. For this reason, it does not diminish strength like excision does and should not be used with fire or without fire for any diseases that are characterized by fullness until the body has been emptied completely.
If cupping is required by illness or custom, we employ it at all times, at the beginning of the month, at the end, and in the middle, and at any time.
This is because some people, when they have an excess of blood that needs to be removed by cupping, experience heaviness and headache in their head. Others experience fullness and redness in their face, head, and neck. Still others experience itching on their face and sides, and darkness and swelling around their eyes. Some also experience itching where the cupping is applied.
Some laugh excessively, while others experience a taste of blood in their mouth and bleeding gums. They may spit up blood. Some sleep more, and some see blood, redness, slaughter, and wounds in their dreams, and similar things. Whenever we see something like that...
Especially if it occurs in the middle third of the month, we order cupping after two or three hours have passed. As for the benefits of point cupping, it is effective against heaviness in the head and water accumulating in the eyes.
But this should only be done after the whole body has been purged. This cupping can serve as a substitute for cephalic phlebotomy.  It is cautioned against for those with a cold brain or a cold, as it will greatly harm them.
Therefore, it should not be used by the elderly or those with cold conditions in their heads, nor by those who are addicted to it, as it can lead to forgetfulness.
Therefore, you should instruct the cupper to lower their hand with the cup slightly downwards to avoid inducing forgetfulness. As for shoulder cupping, it is a substitute for phlebotomy of the ulnar artery and the basilic vein.
Therefore, it is effective against asthma, shortness of breath, headache from the respiratory system, coughing, and fullness. Shoulder cupping should be raised slightly because if it is done lower, it can cause weakness in the heart and stomach.
As for cupping the temples, it is beneficial for headaches occurring in the head, ophthalmia, migraine, angina, and pain in the roots of the teeth, and it is a substitute for phlebotomy from the basilica vein. The barber should be ordered not to deepen his hand with the cup so as not to cut an artery and cause bleeding.
As for cupping under the chin, it is beneficial for ulcers in the mouth, corruption of the gums, and other diseases that occur in the mouth, and it may replace phlebotomy from the jugular veins in the lips. As for cupping the shoulders, it is beneficial for palpitation caused by fullness and heat.
As for cupping the abdomen, it is beneficial for what phlebotomy of the three veins, the basilic, the cephalic, and the median cubital, is beneficial for because it draws blood from all those thin veins that are in the flesh and draws those thin veins from other thicker ones so that the attraction reaches the three thick veins.
The barber should be ordered not to deepen the cup because the place is bare of flesh and beneath it are nerves and arteries. As for a single cupping applied to the coccyx, it is beneficial for hemorrhoids of the anus and ulcers below.
The barber should be ordered that the cup be large and made of copper because the place needs strong suction, and a glass cup may break and it is necessary to apply many cups. As for cupping the legs, it reduces fullness noticeably.
Because it draws blood from the entire body and is beneficial for chronic pains in the kidneys, uterus, and bladder; it brings on menstruation; it is beneficial for pimples and boils; and it replaces phlebotomy from the cephalic and basilic veins, except that it weakens the body greatly and fainting occurs in most people. Cupping the brachial veins has benefits similar to those of cupping the legs.
How to Apply Cupping: First, place the empty cupping glass and apply moderate suction. Do not leave the cup in place for too long, but rather place it quickly and remove it quickly so that the humors are drawn evenly to the area. Repeat this process until you see the area become red and open.
Redness of the blood then appears. At that point, you should make an incision and gradually resume suction. Then look at the constitution of the person; if they have soft flesh with loose pores, you should only make one incision so as not to cause ulceration.
Tell the barber to widen and slightly deepen the incision, and adjust the suction gently with a light motion. If the blood is thick, two incisions should be made; the first to open a path for the thin blood and its watery part, and the second to thoroughly extract the thick blood.
If the blood is very murky, repeat the incision a third time to achieve the goal. In general, if we want to extract a small amount of blood, one incision is sufficient. If we want to extract a large amount of blood, we make more incisions. And if we consider the blood to be thick...
the incision should be deep. The moderate limit for an incision is the depth of the skin only. What should not be used when applying cupping are ointments, water, and what the phlebotomist should avoid. As for someone who has thick, hard, dry skin with tight pores...
...the areas where the cupping glasses are to be placed should be softened with opening and dissolving ointments.  As for during the summer season, ointments such as good quality oil, violet oil, sweet almond oil, or pumpkin seed oil may be used.
As for during the winter, ointments such as narcissus oil, lily of the valley oil, chamomile oil, hyacinth oil, and similar ones may be used. If the humor is thick and cold, the ointment should be one like that of marjoram, or henbane, or bean, or wild rue, and the like.
If the person who is being cupped has wide pores, the flesh should be pinched. Fat should be avoided for these people. They should wash their cupping sites with rose water, or cold water, or fox grape water, or pumpkin water, or rue water, and similar things. As for those whose blood is very moist.
They should wash their cupping sites with vinegar or water mixed with asafetida and sumac, and similar things. As for those whose superfluities are thick, they should wash their cupping sites with old wine or marjoram water or a decoction of bugloss or chamomile, and similar things. Cupping should be avoided in the bath and after the bath.
Rather, it should be used an hour or two after leaving the bath. No one should sleep after cupping. What the person being cupped and the cupper should do before and after cupping.
First, you should look to see if the person being cupped or the cupper is bilious and their blood is mostly sharp and inflamed. They should take coolants such as pomegranate, dandelion with vinegar, lettuce, syrup of vinegar, quince, and similar things. Their food should be chicken, lamb meat in stews, and similar things.
Whoever has a cold temperament should be given honey drink, or myrrh syrup, or vinegar syrup with raisins. They should take fragrant medium wine, which is between old and new wine. They should be ordered to eat little food and their food should be chicken, doves, sparrows, and young pigeons. Drinks on the day of cupping and bloodletting should be more than food.
Sometimes, some people should be given the Farooq antidote, musk medicine, or chamomile before cupping and bloodletting, or after it to strengthen the primary organs and thin the blood. It should not be given to pregnant women.
As for the lancets that are placed without a ligature, they are those that are placed on the liver, spleen, breasts, abdomen, perineum, and the location of the kidneys and the right hip because these organs cannot tolerate a ligature, but rather the aim is to draw blood from one organ to another.
When we place the lancet on the breasts for a case of hiccups or use it to relieve the organ from cold flatulence that has become lodged in the organ, such as when we place the lancet on the abdomen and perineum, it loosens the organ and heats it up, removing pain by dissolving the flatulence.
It can also be placed on the kidneys if there is a blockage or stone, and with strong suction, it may open the blockage or remove the stone from its place. Similarly, it does this when placed on the liver and spleen in cases of flatulence that disturbs them. These lancets may also be used empty by suction alone.
It may also be used with fire, or filled with lukewarm water in cases of colic. The lancet should be filled, preferably a large one, with hot water alone, or with water in which some herbs suitable for this purpose have been cooked.
Then it is placed full on the spot, held there, and removed, and repeated as many times as needed. This is an image of the lancet used with fire:
Its mouth is the width of two fingers open as depicted, and its depth is half a palm's breadth. There is a small hole on its side, about halfway along its length, large enough for a needle to enter. It is made of Chinese copper or yellow copper with a thick rim.
It is smooth and flat, polished so that it does not hurt the organ when placed upon it. In its center there is a transverse groove made of copper or iron where the candle is placed for the fire. This lancet may be made larger or smaller than described depending on the illnesses.
And if we are going to use it, then the forceps of boys and slender men are not the same as those for adult men, and as for how this forceps is put on fire on the member, it is that you put a wick on fire from firm cotton or a small candle from pitch and place it on the middle of the rigid rod which is in the middle of the forceps.
So that the fire ascends upwards toward the bottom of the forceps so that the patient's body does not burn, then it is placed on the member and the finger is put on the hole that we mentioned until when we hold the forceps as we need, the finger is removed.
And the vapor emerges from that hole and the forceps dissolves in its place, then the wick is put back on the way it was and it is repeated if needed, as for the forceps which is used in the disease of fistula with water, there is no rigid rod nor a hole in it.
And it is only used by being filled with water and placed on the member only, and this is its image:
And the larger this forceps is, the more water it can hold, the better it is.
Chapter Seventy-Nine.
On the Cauterization of Warts.
Cauterization is used most often in members where forceps cannot be placed, either because they are small, such as the lip and gums, or because the member is bare of flesh, such as the finger and nose.
And the way to use it is to take leeches from fresh, clean water from streams, then leave them for a day and night in freshwater until they are hungry and there is nothing left in their stomachs. Then the body is first evacuated by cupping or phlebotomy, then the affected member is wiped.
Until it reddens, then a leech is placed on it and when it becomes full, it falls off. If the area can be sucked by forceps, it is more beneficial. Otherwise, wash the area with vinegar, then with plenty of water, rub and squeeze it. If bleeding continues after the leech falls off and this is a discharge, soak a linen cloth in cold water.
And you place it from above until the hemorrhage stops; if the blood is profuse, sprinkle powdered alum or litharge or something similar of astringents upon it until the bleeding stops or half a pared quince is placed upon the spot.
Leave it until the quince adheres to the spot, for the blood will then stop. If it is necessary to repeat the ligature, do not use that ligature again if possible. If the ligature refuses to adhere, smear the spot with fresh blood.
Or insert a needle into the spot until some blood comes out, then apply it; for if it feels any blood, it will adhere to the place. And if you wish it to fall off, sprinkle some patience or salt or ashes upon it, for it will then fall off.
Chapter Three.
On Setting Bones.
This chapter also deals with what is necessary in the practice of medicine, namely, setting bones and reducing dislocations that occur in them.
Know, my sons, that this chapter may be claimed by ignorant physicians and laymen, those who have never browsed among the ancients or read a single letter from their books.
Because of this, this art in our country has become nonexistent and I have never encountered anyone who was truly proficient in it. I have gained what knowledge I possess from it through my extensive reading of the works of the ancients and my persistence in understanding them until I extracted the science from them, then I adhered to experience and practice throughout my life.
I have depicted for you in this chapter everything that my knowledge encompasses concerning this subject. And after bringing it closer to you, I refined it and freed it from the branches of prolixity, summarizing it to the utmost and explaining it with ultimate clarity. I have also presented for you numerous illustrations of instruments used in this practice.
If it is for further explanation, as I have done in the preceding two chapters, and there is no power except by Allah, the Most High, the Great.
The First Chapter.
It contains fine points and summaries concerning the matter of broken bones that must be presented before we begin mentioning the broken and dislocated members one by one, it is necessary to mention in the introduction to this chapter some general statements and sections. You are first compelled to understand them and grasp their truth, both you and anyone else who is eager to learn this honorable craft.
So I say, that whenever one suffers a fracture or dislocation or sprain or fall, you should hasten first to reduce it or immobilize it, or both. Unless something prevents it, such as weakness of strength, or the one who suffered this was a child or an old man, decrepit with age, or the time was extremely hot or very cold indeed.
Then he should restrict his diet to cold legumes, poultry meat, and fresh game, and avoid drinks and thick meats, overeating, and any food that fills the veins with blood, until the swelling subsides and there is no expectation of fluid accumulating at the site.
Then, let the patient return to his initial treatment as he is accustomed, and if the broken bone becomes stuck during reduction, the patient should eat nourishing foods that are thick and rich, such as barley porridge, rice pudding, heads of livestock (sheep's or oxen's heads), and beef tripe. Eggs, fresh fish, thick drinks, and the like are also recommended. For with this treatment, the fracture will heal faster and better. Know that broken bones in strong men and old people can never completely reunite and fuse to their original state.
Because of the dryness and rigidity of their bones. What may fuse are only those bones that were extremely soft, like the bones of young boys. But nature grows upon a broken bone from all sides something resembling glue, which is thick, clings to it, and binds it so tightly that the fragments adhere to one another.
And he connects them together until they come in the utmost strength and firmness as they were at first, so that nothing hinders him from his actions.
For this reason, it is necessary that the patient's food be those foods that have tenacity, viscosity, and thickness, as we have said.  Know that fracture may differ in its types according to the difference of the members, for a fracture of the leg bone is different from a fracture of the head bone, and a fracture of the chest bone is different from a fracture of the back bone.
Likewise, all the other members are different from each other. We will come to mention each type of fracture explained in its chapter in detail separately. A type of bone fracture may also differ in itself.
Because it may be a fracture by crushing without splintering, or a fracture along the length of the bone with splinters and excrescences, both detached and attached. The fracture may occur with a wound and rupture in the skin, or the fracture may be a slight crack.
Each type has a particular method for healing, as will be mentioned in detail in its place.  What distinguishes a bone fracture is its crookedness, swelling, and tenderness, and its crepitation when you pinch it with your hand.
So when there is no apparent crookedness in the place, no crepitation, and no tenderness when you touch the bone with disturbance, and the patient does not experience much pain, then there is no fracture. It could be a sprain or a slight crack. It should not be moved by stretching and pinching at all.
Rather, apply the medications that will be mentioned later to the appropriate location, and then bind it with a gentle compression. Know that if the bone breaks and splits into two without splintering, each part is separated from its counterpart.
You should hasten to straighten and align it as soon as possible before a hot swelling occurs. If a hot swelling occurs, leave it for several days until the swelling subsides, then straighten it in any gentle and skillful way you can.
Know that setting and straightening it is easier than a bone in which fragments have occurred, then tighten on what will be mentioned later. If the bone has fragments, the fractured limb must be extended from both sides, whether it is a hand or a foot.
Either with your hands if the limb is small, or with cords, or with both cord and hand. Let the position of the limb be on a level surface in its natural shape so that when the broken bone fragments are extended, then cut off those excesses and return them to their places by all means possible with skill and gentleness.
Strive your utmost to not cause the patient pain or discomfort with your actions, and make every effort to join one bone to the other in the best position. At that time you should touch and feel them with your hand, for if you see something amiss, correct and straighten it to the best of your ability.
Beware of excessive extension and forceful manipulation as many ignoramuses do, for they often cause a hot swelling or inflammation in the limb as I have witnessed many times from their actions.
Then, after the setting, perfection and tightening, confine that limb to rest and tranquility. Warn the patient not to move it while awake or asleep, when turning and restless, when defecating, and in all his movements as far as possible, and let him seek a position for the limb where pain is avoided.
And this is so that whenever he feels pain or discomfort in the position of the limb, let him move it to a different position where he does not feel pain, while still ensuring that his new position is level and straight.
So that the limb does not become crooked when it heals. As for how to splint a broken limb, I will describe it to you. Know that broken limbs differ in size and shape; those that are small, such as the arm, finger, forearm, and so on…
The bandages should be of fine, soft linen. The thicker ones, such as those for the thigh, back and chest, should be wide and firm because the broad ligament binds a large limb and draws it together evenly from all sides without any displacement.
Then, after you have finished setting, you apply the appropriate paint. Place a soft pad over the fracture site and then begin to wrap the bandage around the fracture itself three or four times, depending on what the limb requires, tightening your hand slightly with each wrap. Then, take it up to the upper part of the fracture site.
Tighten it less than you did at the fracture site, then space the wrap a bit away from the fracture and loosen the tension slightly until you get something suitable from the correct position, then take another bandage and wrap it over the fracture site as well.
Make several more wraps, then take the wrap down to the lower part of the fracture. Do as you did with the first upper wrap in terms of tightening and loosening. Then place soft padding or cloths between the wrappings to correct the angulation of the fracture.
If there is angulation, do so; otherwise, put nothing in it. Then wrap another bandage over it and apply firm splints on top of these bandages, unless there is swelling or hot inflammation in the limb.
If there is swelling or hot inflammation, apply something to soothe it and reduce the swelling. Leave it for a few days, then tighten the splints. The splints should be made of wide, carefully carved reed halves or from ravenwood.
They can be made of pine, palm leaves, tamarisk, or similar materials that are available. Each splint should be this width and shape exactly.
Unless the splint applied to the fracture itself is thicker and slightly wider than the other splints, as for the length of the splint, it should be made according to what suits the size of the broken limb.  Image of a splint:
Then tighten the splints with another bandage according to your initial tightness, then tie firmly from above with threads as we have mentioned regarding the tension, which is that your tension should be greater at the site of the fracture. And the farther away from the fracture, the less the tension.
The threads should be medium in thickness and fineness, and they should be made of damp linen, especially since if the threads are thick as I have seen done by ignorant people who make their threads from woven linen strips, it is a great mistake.
Because the tension with them will be excessive, and fine threads are also unsuitable because you will not achieve the desired tension with them, and there should not be less than the width of a finger between each splint.
If the patient feels pain from the ends of the splints after tightening them in the correct places, put soft padding or puffed wool underneath so that it does not cause him any harm. As for if the fracture is with a wound and there is a rupture in the skin, we will discuss it separately.
Know that not every broken limb should be immobilized with splints from the first day, for if the limb is large, splints should not be applied to it until five, seven, or more days have passed, depending on your confidence in avoiding a hot swelling.
This is a description of the bandages that were used by those who came before to bind fractures, dislocations, and sprains. A general bandage, briefly described: it binds fractures and is suitable for most temperaments, especially for boys and women because it is not excessively hot or cold.
Take some dust from the millstone, which is the bran that clings to the walls of the mill during grinding, and knead it as is, without sifting it, with the white of an egg. Make the dough neither too thick nor too thin, then apply it.
A plaster for setting fractures, dislocations, and sprains: Take ten dirhams of each of the following: mastich, frankincense, acacia, rue, and magnesia; five dirhams of each of mirrh and patience; twenty dirhams of marshmallow; and twenty dirhams of Armenian or Roman clay. Pound all the ingredients together and mix with marshmallow water or egg white if the patient's constitution is hot. Apply this plaster, for it unites well and sets broken bones quickly. It is suitable for most people due to its temperate nature.
Another plaster, also beneficial for fractures and sprains: Take ten dirhams of each of magnesia, mastich, and white henna; five dirhams of each of myrrh and patience; six dirhams of acacia; and twenty dirhams of Armenian clay. Pound all ingredients finely, sift them, and knead with water or egg white. Apply the plaster.
A plaster for joints and bones that have slipped out of place, which relieves the accompanying pain, bone headache, and fractures: Take purified wool, soak it in vinegar and cooked oil, and apply it to the affected area. This plaster does not have bone-setting power.
However, it is excellent for relieving hot swelling and expelling pain, especially. Another plaster for setting broken bones: Take the leaves of wild fig and poppy, pound them all together, and apply them while damp. A shorter plaster recipe used when a bone has been set.
If you want to analyze the remaining swelling, take from the root of henna, chamomile, nard, and caraway flour a part of each; pound them all together and knead with mastic if the member is not inflamed. If it is inflamed, knead it with coriander water or plain water and apply it.
Description of another plaster stronger in resolution than the first, used when a hard tumor occurs at bone union, take from the root of marshmallow and flaxseed and fenugreek and rosemary and marjoram and violet and chamomile, one part from each.
Everything is pounded and kneaded with water of barley or with fresh water or with gum, all that according to the heat of the member and its tranquility, but Hippocrates did not mention in his book to put it on the broken member when setting it.
Except for the plaster of Quiret, made from wax and oil only, and it is described to be moderate between thickness and thinness, but Galen saw that things with hollows should be placed on the broken member when setting it, with some heat such as myrrh and patience and frankincense and what we have mentioned.
Description of a plaster beneficial for weakness and pain, take equal parts of mastic and roasted sesame seeds and human hair from a barren person or bird feathers and marshmallow and salt, pound it, sift it, knead it, and bandage with it, and as for the amount that should remain on the ligament.
Then it dissolves, so look and see if the patient does not experience pain or itching or movement of the broken bone from its place, then do not dissolve it for many days, but if there is severe itching or disturbing pain or swelling in the place.
Then hasten to dissolve it at the time and do not delay it at all, and remove the plaster from it, then take a soft cloth or a marine sponge moistened with lukewarm water and wash the place with it until the itching subsides and the pain is relieved, then leave the limb to rest for an hour.
Then apply wool softened in vinegar and oil or rose ointment to it and tie it on for a night until you are safe from the hot tumor, and relieve the swelling of the limb and its pain goes away, then return it to gentle tension and light plastering, and do not tighten it as your first tightening.
And dress it until it is healed. If you see that the swelling, redness, pain, and distension and all the symptoms have gone entirely and require a dressing and binding, prepare it as you did originally, whether or not anything of what we mentioned occurred in the limb.
Do not loosen it except after three, four, five, or seven days, or even twenty days, all of this depending on what appears to you from the condition of the limb as we said, until if breaking is necessary and the flesh is about to knit together.
Also increase the binding at that time more than your initial binding altogether and also increase the strictness of the patient's diet as previously mentioned. If you see that the place of the fracture has become dry and loose more than is necessary, know that nourishment is prevented from reaching it.
So wash it with lukewarm water each time you loosen it, and let this be every three days, and slightly reduce the binding. For by doing this, nourishment will flow to the limb and it will heal quickly. As for what ignorant binders do by breaking the limb again...
If it was not first bound as it should be and is bound crookedly, this is a mistake on their part and a great deception. If it were correct, the ancients would have mentioned it in their books and acted accordingly, and you would not have seen any trace of it from any of them. And the right thing is not to do it.
Chapter Two.
On Accidental Fracture in the Head.
There are many types of accidental fracture in the head, with various shapes and ingeniously diverse causes. Some fractures may result from a sword blow, either completely splitting the bone until it reaches the dura mater beneath the bone… ... just as a chisel splits wood.
Therefore, this type of fracture is called chiseling. Or the sword may cut a portion of the bone and only split its surface, without penetrating to the end, and this type is called a complete avulsion. The wounds from these two types of fractures can be either large or small.
And of fracture there is that which is a bruise or a fissure, and its cause is a blow with a stone or a fall upon a stone or the like. This fracture is either penetrating, having nearly reached the membrane beneath the bone, or it is in the face of the bone.
And the wound of these two fractures is also either large or small, and there are fractures that are concealed in the bone at a hair's breadth, which is a slight crack.
That is why this type of fracture is called hairy, and there are fractures that occur from a fall or a blow with a stone or the like that enter the surface of the bone inward and cause the place to become concave, as happens to copper when it is struck, a portion of it entering inward.
And this is most common in the moist head bone, such as the skulls of boys. All these types of fractures may have fragments that are loose and those that are not, and we will mention the treatment of all of them in their proper place. We recognize all these types of fractures by examining them.
And by probing them and removing the corrupt flesh from them. As for the hairy type, it is recognized by exposing the bone, touching it, and smearing it with ink; the fracture will appear black. As for the treatment of a fracture, you first look at the symptoms of the patient.
If you see in his symptoms a clear indication of fear, such as vomiting bile, stretching, loss of mind, cessation of voice, fainting, and a sharp fever, redness and twitching of the eyes, and other such symptoms, then do not approach the patient or treat him, for death is upon him with these symptoms in most cases inevitably.
And if you see symptoms that do not frighten you and hope for his safety, then take him into treatment; because if the wounded person comes to you soon after being injured, and this was during the winter days...
It is necessary to make every effort to remove the bone before the fourteenth day in any case, and if it is during the summer days, you should hasten to remove the bone before the seventh day, lest what is under the bone of the membrane be corrupted, and thus expose those symptoms that we have described.
If the fracture has reached the membrane covering the brain and there was a comminuted fracture, then you should cut away the crushed part as I will describe to you, which is to shave the head of the wounded patient.
Expose the bone in any way that is possible for you, depending on the shape of the wound and what appears to be best for the patient. If upon exposure of the bone, you encounter bleeding or a hot swelling, then treat it accordingly.
This is done by stuffing the area with cloths soaked in rose water and ointment, cleaning the wound until the swelling subsides and bleeding stops. Then proceed to grind and extract the bone, which can be done in one of two ways.
As for the first method, it is to cut the bone with a delicate, narrow blade. Here's an illustration:
Then you use another cutter after this one, slightly wider, and here's its image:
Then use another cutter, also wider than the second one. You should have several cutters of different widths and lengths, all with extremely sharp ends, made of good Indian iron or steel.
Use gentleness when striking the cutter so as not to shake the head and cause him pain. If the bone is strong and hard, you should drill around it before using the cutters with non-piercing drills, which are called that because they are not deep-piercing.
This is because they do not exceed the limit of the skull bone and go beyond it. The drill has a circular shape below its sharp head, resembling a collar or small circle, which prevents it from diving and exceeding the thickness of the bone.
You should make many of these drills, each one suitable for the thickness of that bone so that you have for each skull a pierced point, in length and shortness, according to the thickness of that skull. This is an illustration of three types. Of drills large, medium and small:
As for how to drill around a fractured bone, it is that you place the drill on the bone and turn it with your fingers until you know that the bone has been penetrated then move the drill to another location and make the distance between each hole the thickness of a knitting needle or thereabouts.
Then cut with the saw between each two holes and do this as gently as you can, as we said, until you remove the bone either with your hand or with something else from some of the tools prepared for that.
Such as forceps and fine tongs, and you should be very careful that the drill or saw touches nothing of the peritoneum, and if you crushed the bone and it is separated from the peritoneum, and you take it out, then it should be cleaned.
And smooth the roughness that remains in the bone with another instrument resembling a saw, but it should be thinner and finer than all other saws, if there are any small bones or fragments left, take them gently with the tools you have.
Then treat the wound afterwards with bandages and ointments which will be mentioned, and the other side of this work is a very easy side far from danger, its mention by Galen and his great praise for it.  This is what he said. You should first begin by exposing a part of the bone from the place where the fracture is most severe and prominent so that when you expose that part, you place the end of this lenticular saw underneath it. This is its illustration:
The lenticular part of it should be smooth so that it does not cut anything, and the sharp part of it is on its sides extending in length as you see, so that the lenticular part rests on the peritoneum and the sharp face of the saw is on the bone, then strike the saw from one side.
With a small hammer, until you cut all the bone gently as it rotates and you are safe from the membrane so that nothing frightening happens at all, even if the healer is the most ignorant and cowardly of people, yes, even if he were sleepy.
If anything of the membrane remains adhering to the bone in some places, scrape it off with the tip of the lens cutter itself and free it gently, for it will come away without harm or fear.
But if the fracture of the bone has not extended to the membrane and what has broken off from the bone has a rough surface and fine splinters, then you should remove that roughness and those splinters with fine scrapers of which several have been made in various sizes so that you can use the one most suitable and best for each place according to what your work dictates.
And with the same broken bone and its shape, you should use in your scraping first the widest of those scrapers, then use a narrower one afterward, and continue to do that in sequence until you come to using the narrowest and thinnest of them all.
As for all the small, hair-like fissures and fine fractures, treatment appropriate to each one should be used according to what leads to its healing, which is something that does not escape those who have even the slightest training in this craft and have studied what we have written and explained about major fractures.
If the membrane remains exposed after you have removed the bone, you should take a linen cloth the size of the wound, soak it in rose oil, and place it over the opening of the wound, then take another cloth folded in half or into a triangle. Soak it in syrup and rose oil, and place it on top of the first cloth, making sure to do this as lightly as possible so as not to weigh down the peritoneum, then use a wide bandage over it and only tighten it enough to hold the wound together.
Leave it with this treatment for a day or two until the hot swelling is secure, then dissolve it and afterwards use medicines which have a drying effect, such as the root of marshmallow, caraway seed powder, candy root powder and zedoary, and so on. Make a poultice from these medicines.
Spread it on the wound as is, dry. In general, use a medicine that cleanses without stinging. Strive to keep the wound clean at all times during treatment, free from oily residues, dirt, and do not allow pus to collect in it at all.
For pus, if it collects on the membrane of the brain, will corrupt and spoil it, and as a result of this the patient will suffer greatly. It may also occur in the periosteum of the head when the bone is exposed, especially if its treatment is neglected.
If you see blackness on its surface and the patient shows the symptoms we have mentioned, know that he is inevitably lost. If the blackness occurred due to a medicine applied to it, and if the strength of that medicine was such as to cause it.
You should take one part honey and three parts rose oil, beat them well together, and soak a cloth in the mixture. Then place it on the periosteum and treat it with the types of treatment that are appropriate until it is healed.
Chapter Three.
On setting a broken nose.
Know that the only part of the nose that can break is its upper segment, either both or one, for they are bone. The lower part is cartilaginous and does not break, but may suffer bruising, bending, or displacement.
If one of its segments breaks, you should insert your little finger into the nostril and straighten the fracture from within with your index finger and thumb on the outside until the nose is returned to its natural shape. Do this gently and carefully so as not to cause pain to the patient.
If the fracture is at the top of the nose and no finger has been injured, it should be straightened with the end of a probe slightly thickened. If the fracture is on both sides, do the same. He should hasten to set it on the first day after the fracture.
If possible, otherwise after the seventh or tenth day when the hot swelling subsides, then insert a wick of linen cloth into the nostril hole if the fracture is on one side, or insert two wicks if the fracture is on both sides.
Let the wick be thick enough to fill the nostril hole, and some of the early setters mentioned that the wick should be soaked in butter and changed every day. I do not see this, but it should be soaked in egg white mixed with mill dust.
Then leave the wick until the bone is set and the cartilage hardens, and goose feather tubes may be inserted into the nostril in place of the wick after they are wrapped with soft linen; this will hold the broken nose more firmly, so that the patient does not refrain from breathing.
This is not necessary if you wish to do it, and if you wish to make wicks. If swelling occurs in the nose during your work, bandage the nose with cotton or with cotton soaked in vinegar and rose oil, or something from the Diachylon plaster.
If hot swelling does not occur, it should be bandaged from the outside with fine semolina flour and finely ground benzoin mixed with egg white; then put a soft bandage on top, and do not tie the nose with anything at all. If the nasal bones have fractured into small pieces.
Or if they have shattered, it is necessary to make an incision on them and remove them with the instrument suitable for this, then stitch the incision and treat it with ointments that promote healing and closure. If a wound occurs inside the nose, it should be treated with a wick and lead tubes should be used. Until it is healed.
Chapter Four.
In the setting of a lower jaw fracture.
If the lower jaw is fractured and there is no wound, then if the fracture is only external and has not broken into two pieces and fallen inward, it will be easy to recognize.
If the fracture is on the right side, you should insert your left index finger into the patient's mouth; and if the fracture is on the left side, insert the index finger of your right hand and gently raise the bump of the fracture from the inside to the outside.
With your other hand, from outside the bone, guide it into alignment. If the jaw fracture has split into two pieces, a spreader should be used on both sides to straighten it so that you can align it. If it happened in the teeth, they will become loose.
Or they separate, so tighten any that you hope to keep with gold, silver, or silk thread; then place a plaster cast on the fractured jaw, and on top of it a folded cloth, and on the cloth a large, tight splint or a piece of shoe leather equal in length to the jaw. Then tie it from above according to what is available and suitable for fastening so that it does not loosen, and order the patient to be quiet and still; and make his food soft porridges. If you think that something has changed in the shape of his face on any side, hasten to correct it on the third day.
Then tie it from above according to what is available and suitable for fastening so that it does not loosen, and order the patient to be quiet and still; and make his food soft porridges. If you think that something has changed in the shape of his face on any side, hasten to correct it on the third day. Then fix what you bandaged with rye flour mixed with egg white or semolina after removing the plaster from it and apply a soft bandage over the dressing. As long as that bandage sticks to it and the bone condition does not change, leave it.
Do not remove it until it is healed and the fracture is strong, as this fracture often becomes strong in three weeks. If there is a hot swelling during this period, use what we have mentioned many times to relieve it until that swelling subsides.
If the fracture is with a wound, and if it has been freed from bone fragments, whether single or multiple, proceed delicately to extract those fragments using an instrument suited for their removal. If the wound opening is narrow, widen it with a scalpel as needed.
Then, if you have extracted all the fragments, suture the wound opening if it is wide; otherwise, apply one of the dressings suitable for that purpose. Stitch the wound until it heals.
Chapter Five.
On Setting a Broken Clavicle.
The clavicle most often breaks in front of the end near the shoulder, and the fracture can occur in one of three ways: either it breaks and splits into two without any fragmentation, which is the easiest to set;
or fragmentation occurs in the fracture, which is more difficult to set; or the fracture may be accompanied by a wound. If the fracture is without a wound, have two assistants hold the arm next to the broken clavicle, one supporting the arm and the other extending the neck toward the opposite side.
Then, straighten the fracture with your fingers until it assumes its proper shape and there are no bumps or irregularities. If you need to extend it further, place a ball of cloth or wool under the patient's armpit.
The ball will provide the necessary elevation and extension, allowing you to raise the clavicle and press it into proper alignment. If you are unable to draw the end of the clavicle outwards because it has sunk deep, the patient should lie on their back.
Place a medium-sized pillow under their elbow, and have the assistant press down on their elbow so that the deep clavicle bone rises upward. Then, correct and align the fracture with your fingers.
If you feel that a fragment of the clavicle has been broken and is moving, it should be incised upon and that fragment carefully extracted. If the fragment is lodged in the bone, endeavor to cut it with one of the instruments prepared for that purpose. After you place the instrument that preserves the peritoneum under the clavicle, which is an instrument of wood or iron, and this is its image:
It resembles a spoon without a handle, and its width should be according to the size of the bone, whether large or small. As for its length, it should also be according to what you are able to work with. Let it have two ends as you see, one end wider and the other narrower.
But if the incision made when extracting the bone fragment is wide and you are confident there will be no hot swelling, bring the edges of the wound together with sutures. If the incision is slight or you fear hot swelling, dress the wound with lint and bandages according to the extent of the incision.
If hot swelling appears, soak the lint in rose oil and vinegar or wine and apply it. As for setting a bone if there is no wound or incision, compress the bone with a poultice made of mill dust mixed with egg white. Place a soft bandage over it, then place a ball under his armpit if needed. Then take a turban which is very long and about a span wide. Carry a folded bandage over the bandage and poultice. Let its width be three fingers and its length the same, then insert it into a cloth, then insert that cloth along with the splint into the part of the turban which is over the fracture, then tighten the turban on the fracture as it turns. And place it on his neck and under his sound armpit and under the armpit of the injured side, and return it many times on each side, as you see that the tightening sets the fracture firmly, which is not hidden from you.
And know that the splint should never be removed from the broken bone. Therefore, you should check on the patient every day, and whenever the bandage relaxes and you see that the splint has come loose, fix it and tighten the bandage, then make the patient sleep on his back and place a small pillow under his armpit when he sleeps at night.
So that his arm is raised from his side, raising the clavicle fracture with the raising of the elbow, or tie his arm to his neck, and do not loosen the bandage unless there is an incident in the place such as scratching or swelling until twelve days have passed.
Then renew the dressing if you see a need for it. Return to tightening and leave it until the clavicle fracture heals and becomes firm, and strengthens even more in twenty-eight days. And it may be less for some people.
Chapter Six.
On setting a broken shoulder. The shoulder rarely breaks in its broad part, but rather its projections. Whenever a part of it is broken or it breaks in the middle, this is known by touch; then according to the shape of the fracture, proceed accordingly.
Set it and return it to its natural shape as much as possible by any means, then apply rye flour powder mixed with egg white and a soft poultice to the area, then place a bandage of folded cloth on top, and then put a wide splint made of wood over it.
Make it thin enough to cover the whole shoulder or a little wider, and if there is a bandage under the splint in one of the places on the shoulder, smooth that bandage with gentle pressure until the splint lies flat, then tie it firmly with a long turban.
Tie it tightly and make sure the splint does not move from its place, check the bandage every day, and whenever the bandage loosens tighten it and straighten the splint if it moves, let the patient lie on their right side, and the shoulder will heal in twenty days.
In twenty or twenty-five days, and when this period is complete, untie the bandage and you will be safe, for it is one of the bones that does not fear breaking or shattering, and if a fragment of bone protrudes and sinks under the skin, cut over it and remove it. Do what was mentioned in the fracture of the clavicle to relieve any hot swelling that may occur.
Chapter Seventh
On Setting a Broken Breastbone.
The breastbone may break in the middle, which rarely occurs, while its ends are more prone to breaking and shattering.  Among its symptoms, if it breaks in the middle, is a downward slant, severe pain, difficulty breathing, and coughing.
Sometimes there may be blood discharge, and the depression in the broken bone becomes apparent and is not hidden to the touch. To set it, let the patient lie on their back, place a cushion between their shoulders, then press down on their elbows and bring the ribs together with the hands from both sides, gently adjusting it as much as possible.
Continue this until the bone regains its proper shape, then apply a bandage and pressure dressing over it, place a splint made from a thin board of linden wood or similar lightweight material on top after wrapping it in cloth. Then refine its tying to the broken bone so that it does not loosen, and pass the ligature over the back in a circular fashion several times and tighten it firmly. Then examine the ligature at all times, and whenever it relaxes, tighten it.
And if necessity requires its undoing, at the appearance of pain or swelling in the place, hasten to undo it, remove the dressing and rectify what appeared by treating it accordingly, then return the dressing if you deem it necessary. And immobilize it with a bandage so that it heals properly.
Chapter Eight.
In setting ribs when they are fractured, know that fractures in ribs occur in the thickest places following the back and at their ends in front because these parts receive blows due to them being cartilaginous; and knowledge of this is not hidden from feeling upon examining with fingers.
Set it by straightening the fracture with your fingers in a competent manner until the shape is aligned as it should be, then dress and bind the broken bone with a splint if needed. If the rib fracture is angled inward, it will cause the patient severe pain.
And a condition like pneumothorax may occur, which happens to those with a rib fracture because the bone punctures the diaphragm and causes him shortness of breath, coughing, and spitting up blood; this is difficult to treat, and those before us have tried many tricks in it.
Some say it is necessary to make the patient’s diet consist of things that produce flatulence and gas so that the abdomen inflates and expands, pushing the fracture outward; we disapprove of this lest it encourage the occurrence of a hot abscess, and if one has occurred, it will increase and aggravate it.
Some said that a cupping glass should be placed on the spot and then sucked forcefully, which is similar to measuring it; however, they fear that the cupping glass may draw excess blood to the area due to its weakness. Others have said that the area should be covered with wool soaked in hot oil.
And the dressing should be applied between the ribs until it is full, so that the bandage becomes level when wound around the curve. Then the patient should be treated with the regimen for contusion, in terms of diet and medication, unless the patient is subjected to a severe ordeal which they cannot endure.
