The bone was depressing the diaphragm in a harmful manner, and we feared for the patient, so it is necessary to make an incision at the site and expose the broken rib; then place the instrument that preserves the peritoneum, which has been previously described, underneath; and cut the bone carefully and remove it.
Then we suture the edges of the wound if it is large and treat it with ointments until it heals. If a hot swelling appears during this time, hasten to soak dressings in rose oil and apply them to the area; and treat the patient internally as well with remedies that reduce swelling.
And he should lie on the side which makes sleeping easier until he recovers.
Chapter Nine.
On Setting Fractured Vertebrae of the Back and Neck.
As for the bones of the neck, if they are fractured, which rarely happens, and also the vertebrae of the back, if this occurs to someone and you wish to know whether it will heal or not. Observe whether his hands have become relaxed and numb, and are lifeless, and he is unable to move them, extend them, or clench them; and if you prick them or insert a needle into them, he does not feel it, nor does he experience any pain in them, then know that he will not heal in most cases and that he is doomed.
If he can move them and feels the pricking and insertion, know that the marrow of the bone is safe and that the patient will heal with treatment. If a fractured vertebra of the back occurs similarly and you wish to know whether it will also heal or not,
observe his legs; if you see that they have become relaxed and the same thing has happened to them as the hands, then if he lies on his back, wind and stool pass involuntarily; and if he lies on his stomach, urine passes involuntarily. And if he lies on his back and is unable to urinate, know that he is doomed, so do not concern yourself with treating him. If none of this occurs, the matter is less serious, and the treatment for what has happened is to aim to relieve the hot swelling.
Apply rose oil alone, or with roasted egg yolks, to the diseased area three times a day until the hot swelling subsides. Then apply one of the strengthening and absorbent bandages to the area and tighten it with a bandage. Order him to rest.
He should only sleep on the side where he does not feel pain until he is healed. If a bone fragment or something else that has healed occurred during the injury, the skin should be incised and that bone removed. Then the edges of the wound should be brought together by suturing if it is large, and then treated with healing ointments until it heals. If the last bone of the coccyx, which is the tailbone, is broken, you should insert your left thumb into the anus.
Straighten the broken bone with your other hand as much as possible, and according to what is attainable. Then apply a bandage and splint if needed, and tighten it.
If you feel a broken fragment in it, incise over it and remove it. Treat the wound with the treatment mentioned previously until it heals.
Chapter Ten.
On setting a broken hip.
It is rare for the bones of the hip to break. But when they do, the fracture usually consists of crumbling at its ends, splitting lengthwise, and tilting inward. The patient will experience pain in the area, numbness, and paralysis of the leg on the side of the fracture.
To set it, pass your hand over it until you determine the nature of the fracture. If the fracture is only at its ends, straighten it as much as possible so that it resembles its natural shape.
If the fracture is lengthwise or has inclined inwards, lay the patient on his stomach so that you may set the bone. Once it is straight, apply a poultice to it and then put a splint of wood or leather upon it and bind it tightly.
Fear not displacement of the fracture nor loosening of the splint, and equalize the hollows of the bandages by filling them so that the binding may be uniform. Order the patient to sleep on his back or on his right side; if hot swelling should occur.
Cease stretching and setting it until the hot swelling subsides, and apply what will relieve it as mentioned before; then return to setting and binding it as is proper. If there should be splinters or fragments in the bone, they should not be removed.
Let them alone and straighten from without as we have said, and leave it bound until it heals.
Chapter Eleven.
On Setting a Fracture of the Humerus.
The humerus is that which is between the elbow and the head of the shoulder. If it is fractured, set it in one of two ways: one is to take a smooth, curved stick of medium thickness, in this form:
Tie strings at its two ends and suspend it from a raised place; then let the patient sit on a chair, and cast his broken arm upon the stick so that his armpit may be attached in the middle of the curve of the stick; then hang something heavy above it.
Or have a servant extend it downwards, and then the surgeon straightens the fracture with both his hands until the fracture is returned to its proper place; the other way is for the patient to lie on his back and suspend his hand from his neck by a string.
Then order two servants to hold it: one above the fracture with his hands and the other below; let each extend to his side. If you wish the extension to be stronger, tie a bandage below the fracture and another above it, and let each of the servants extend them to their sides.
If the fracture is near the end of the shoulder blade, the middle of the ligature should be placed under the armpit and the other ends under the fracture towards the elbow. Similarly, if the fracture is near the elbow, the ligature should be placed on that spot and on the elbow itself.
Then the fracture should be set gently without violence until the fracture is aligned properly and a good union is achieved, then it should be tightened unless there is a hot swelling; if there is a hot swelling leave it untightened until the seventh day and put on it a poultice soaked in vinegar and rose oil.
Until the swelling subsides, then tighten it; the way to tighten it is to hold the dressing on the fracture and then hold a bandage of new cloth over the dressing, then gather the arm against the humerus itself and put his hand open on his shoulder blade.
Hold the cloth and bandage tight on the humerus and arm so that the arm acts as a splint, unless something prevents you from doing so and nothing has changed for you from the broken bone; if you fear that something will change for you from it, then use splints.
And it is to place on the fracture itself a splint which is wider and stronger than all the other splints, and let there be the width of a finger between each splint; and let the length of the splints be according to the fracture with an increase of three fingers from each side.
Then tighten on the splints the tightening that I mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And it is to be a tighter bandage on the place of the fracture.
And the further from the fracture, the less tight the bandage should be; if you see that the placement of the splints and the tightening as we said when setting the limb is correct, then do so.
And if you fear a hot swelling, leave the tightening and splints until the seventh day as we said, then check the ligature every three days so that no chafing or distention occurs in the place, or that food is prevented from reaching the limb due to excessive tightness, correct all of this as we mentioned.
If you are confident that none of this will happen, do not untie the ligature for many days. The patient should lie on his back with his hand on his stomach and a bolster stuffed with wool placed under his arm.
Check at all times, both night and day, lest the shape of the broken limb be disturbed or the ligature relax; correct all this with your utmost effort. Let the patient's diet be such as we have previously described, that it should be light at first until the bone begins to knit.
Then his diet should be gradually increased. For it is usual for the arm and leg to knit in forty days; then the ligature should be untied, and he should use a bath and be treated with suitable ointments. If the fracture is severe and crushed.
Do not untie the ligature for fifty days or even two months.
Chapter Twelfth.
On Setting a Broken Arm.
The arm is composed of two bones called the radius and ulna; one is smaller, which is next to the thumb, and the other is larger and situated beneath the smaller one. Sometimes the larger bone breaks alone or the smaller one alone, and sometimes they both break together.
When the smaller, upper bone breaks, setting it is easy and healing is quicker. When the lower bone breaks, the fracture is worse, healing is more difficult, and worst of all when both bones are broken together. If the bone that has broken is the smaller, upper one...
The physician, when setting it, should make the extension slight and gentle so as to straighten it. If the larger bone is broken, he should make the extension stronger. If both bones are broken, the extension should be much stronger.
The shape of the hand should be placed upon the dressing and extended on a cushion, with the thumb raised highest of all the fingers, the little finger lowest of all, and the ring finger squarely based upon itself.
The cushion should be of a height opposite him so that the patient is not put to trouble.
Then a servant extends the arm from below, either with his hand or with a bandage, and another servant extends it from above in the same way; then the surgeon straightens the bone until he returns it to the best shape possible. If there are splinters in the fracture of the bone, they are removed.
Return every splinter to its place as much as you can; if a clean splinter appears and it is undermining the skin, and there is no hope of stitching it, then cut over it and remove it in the manner we have described previously. If the fracture is with a wound, a chapter has been devoted to it.
You will find the treatment for that in the chapter on fractures with wounds. If, at first dressing, a hot swelling appears, soak a cloth in rose-ointment and white wax, and let it be of medium thickness, then bind it gently until the swelling subsides.
Then remove the rose-ointment and apply a poultice prepared from mill dust mixed with egg white; then fit on the splints, and let the splint placed directly on the fracture be a little wider and stronger. Know that the number of arm splints is six in most cases.
Whether the fracture is in one bone or in two together, then make your binding on the seat of the fracture strongest and tightest; and whenever you turn in binding upwards or downwards, make the binding a little looser, as previously mentioned at the beginning of this chapter.
Let the cloths that are wound around the fracture be soft and damp cloths, not very stiff, and let the thread with which it is bound be flax, especially of medium thickness, as we have described. Examine the limb and dressing after a few days.
If something needs to be repaired, such as a sprain in a limb, the limb should be immersed in warm water until the sprain subsides, and the limb should be left unstrained for a night so that it may rest, then straining should be resumed.
If the strain has relaxed and the bone has shifted, or something similar has occurred, do your utmost to remedy all of that. Also observe whether the nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb due to excessive straining; if so, you should loosen it slightly and leave it for several days until nourishment flows to it, then strain it again.
If none of the aforementioned ailments occur to the patient, he should not be discharged until after twenty days or so. Then his hand should be hung around his neck and let his arm be moderate; he should conserve his effort from agitated movements and make him sleep on his back.
Know that this fracture of the arm heals within thirty days or in thirty-two, and perhaps even in twenty-eight; all of this depending on the states of temperaments and strengths.
Chapter Thirteen.
On Setting a Fracture of the Palm of the Hand and Fingers.
The metacarpals of the hand and phalanges of the fingers rarely experience fractures, but they are often subject to contusions. So whenever the palm experiences a fracture or contusion...
The patient should sit cross-legged with a chair directly in front of him.
Then he should place his hand on it extended, and the bone-setter's assistant should extend the fractured limb. The physician straightens it until a good union is formed. Then a bandage and splint should be applied if no hot swelling has occurred; then a splint is placed over it according to the situation.
I have inserted it into a soft cloth. If the fracture is towards the bottom, near the palm, make a semi-sphere from cloth and instruct the patient to grasp it with his broken hand; then tighten it with a long cloth.
Let the splint be made of leather, which is soft so that the skin may conform to the bending of all the hand, and it should be tightened as needed; if the fracture is outward, then one splint should be placed above and another below on the hand so that the hand remains open and upright.
Then the tension should be carried as the hand rotates and it should be secured between the fingers with a bandage; if the fracture is to one of the metacarpals of the fingers, then if it is the thumb, let it be straightened as needed and then tightened with the hand, and if you wish, you may make a small upright splint for it to support the fracture.
It should not be moved; if the fracture is to any of the other fingers, such as the middle, index, ring, or little finger, let it be straightened and bound with the finger next to it that is sound, or bind them all together on a splint, which is best, or place upon them a small upright splint.
As we said in regard to the thumb, and inspect it during your splinting and afterward at a distance from the hot swelling, meet it as needed whenever something of that sort occurs, as has been repeatedly described.
Chapter Fourteen.
On Splinting a Femoral Fracture.
The femur often fractures and becomes apparent to the touch because it displaces forward and backward.  Splinting is done by tightening a bandage above the fracture and another below, with the patient lying face down.
Then each bandage should be extended to its side, maintaining balance; if the fracture is in the middle of the bone. But if it is near the origin of the femur, then a soft bandage, either of wool or similar material, should be tightened.
At the origin of the femur towards the pubis, so that the extension is upwards and the other bandage below the fracture; similarly if the fracture is near the knee, let the bandage be near the knee with the extension downwards. Then the physician straightens the bone with both hands until it is returned to its natural form.
When the bone has united well, it should then be bandaged and supported, unless the limb has become hot and swollen; if swelling occurs, leave it for a few days until the heat subsides, and then resume treatment.
As for supporting it, a wide, firm bandage should be wound twice or thrice over the fracture; what is left over should then be wound around the leg until the heel is at the level of the thigh bone. A long thread should then be passed between the thigh and the leg near the knee, below it, with the ends extending upwards on both sides.
The remainder of the bandage is then wound around the leg and thigh. Splints are then applied to the thigh at the site of the fracture, making a single splint on the shinbone; the space between the thigh and leg is then filled with soft rags so that the support is even.
Begin with firm bandaging in the middle, over the fracture site, making three or four turns; as you move the bandage away from the fracture site, make it looser and less tight.
Then take hold of the two ends of the thread that you passed between the thigh and leg and tie them to the splints above; then pass the ends of the thread downwards until they reach the ankle, and tighten them there as well to the splints on the other side.
So that the bandage does not slip out of place, it should be left in position as long as the limb does not experience pain, swelling, distension or anything similar. If any of these things occur, immediately undo the bandage and rectify whatever has happened, as we have described many times before.
If there are bone fragments that protrude, they should be straightened if possible; otherwise, cut through them and remove them. Treat the wound as previously described until it heals. This type of fracture can sometimes be treated by supporting the thigh alone without splinting the leg.
As we mentioned in the forearm and arm, although its setting is thus not accompanied by limping for the patient, if it is set alone without being joined to the leg, then its owner will necessarily always limp.
Know that the thigh strengthens in fifty days or a little more or less, all of that depending on the difference in temperaments and other conditions.
Chapter Fifteenth.
In Fracture of the Patella.
The patella is rarely fractured, but it is often bruised. If a fracture occurs, it is either a crack or a fragmentation into its parts, with or without a wound.
You determine all of this by feeling, and you set it by straightening the separated parts with your fingers until they come together and join as best you can, with proper alignment, gentleness, and skill. Then apply a bandage and a circular splint if needed.
Then tighten the corresponding bandage above it, and observe all the conditions we described in other fractures, such as hot swelling and the like, treating each occurrence with what is appropriate until it heals.
Chapter Sixteenth.
On Setting a Fractured Leg.
The leg has two bones, one thick and called the tibia, and the other thin and called the fibula. They are subject to the same types of fractures as the arm bones. Therefore, setting it is the same as setting the arm, and the process is one. If both bones are fractured, the leg turns in all directions. If the thinner bone is fractured, the leg turns forward. And if the thicker bone is fractured and this occurs below, then it will be obvious to you.
Use traction and alignment, and bind the splints accordingly, unless it is a severe fracture with many fragments, in which case the traction should be less and gentler, and you should strive to set it carefully.
And in the leg there is something more to do than in the arm, and that is that when you have leveled the splints and finished all your work, take two pieces of pine wood which are used to level the rooms that are placed between the cracks of the boards or they can be made of palm leaves or the like.
Choose from them one that is slightly thick and is not thin, and let its length be along the leg from the knee down, then wrap two rags lengthwise on each one and place one on the leg and the other on the other side.
Let it be from the knee to the bottom of the foot, then tie the two pieces in three places at the ends and the middle, for with this binding the leg is prevented from leaning right and left and it is well immobilized, and sometimes a gutter of wood can be used along the length of the leg.
And it is put in it to keep it from moving, and this should be done most especially in a fracture if there is a wound with it, then check the leg every day and take great care of it for swelling or bloating or anything else like that. So whenever something like that happens, treat it accordingly until it is healed, and the bone of the leg is rejoined in thirty days or so.
Chapter Seventeenth.
On fractures of the bones of the leg and fingers.
As for the heel, it is not subject to fracture at all, and as for the bones of the leg, they may be fractured, and the fingers are rarely fractured, but they are more often bruised.
If the bones of the leg are fractured and you see that those bones are overlapping, let the patient put his foot on the ground upright as when walking, then you put your foot on what has risen from those bones and step on them to set them straight.
Until it returns to its place, then carry a bandage and splints from above and place under the sole of the foot a small board with two flat heads, then tighten the foot firmly after wrapping it with rags and all that it needs.
If three or four days have passed, release the bandage; you will find the bones aligned, whether they were broken or dislocated. The procedure is as you see it. As for if some fingers are broken, splint them and set them according to what I have described for you in setting the fingers of the hand.
Then make a splint for the broken finger, along the length of the finger and slightly wider than it. Then place under the foot that board which I have described to you and tighten it firmly, if two, three or more bones are broken.
Make for each finger a splint of appropriate size inserted into a soft cloth, and tighten the board under the foot, leaning each end of its head outward from the sole of the foot for good fixation.
You should remember all that we have mentioned to you in the fractures of all the conditions we have described and treat each condition accordingly.
Chapter Eighteen.
On a fracture of the female pubis bone and the male pelvis.
When a woman's vulva is fractured, seat her upright and then incline her slightly backward while holding her from behind, and have the midwife pack her vulva with cotton until it is full and becomes like a ball in her vulva.
Then shake the woman and raise her spine slightly; that cotton will come out until it forms a ball at the opening of the vulva, and the bone fracture will be reduced. Then apply a bandage to her back. Whenever she wants to urinate, remove the cotton gently so that she can urinate.
Then replace it as you first inserted it, and she returns to her original bandage. Do this for seven days or so, because it will heal. If you wish, take a sheep's bladder, stretch a reed tube over its opening, and insert the entire bladder into her vulva.
Then blow strongly into the tube until the bladder swells inside the vulva, for the fracture will return, then stuff it with cotton and stay on what we described for several days until it heals, and as for when the pubic bone is broken in a man or woman, it uses...
And it is arranged as we described in the hip bone, and it is not hidden from you the correctness in these strange fractures that rarely occur, especially for those who have some experience and understand this book of mine, yes, because the fracture itself indicates to you in most cases.
On the right path in setting and tightening it, so understand, and as for mentioning a man if his thigh is broken like a goose's throat, then enter the penis into it, then put a bandage of cloth on it and bandage it and leave it for three days or so until it heals.
Chapter Nineteenth.
In setting fractures of bones if they are with a wound.
It is necessary for whoever has a fracture with a wound, especially if the bone is large such as the thigh bone or humerus or something similar, to hasten and cupping from his time if the conditions of cupping helped you as we presented.
If the wound bleeds blood, then it is necessary to hasten to cut it by sprinkling powdered alum on it if nothing else is available for you, then take to setting the fracture on that very day and do not delay it.
If a hot swelling does not occur, if it occurs, leave the setting to the ninth day until the hot swelling subsides and do not approach it at all on the third and fourth day, for it is subject to bad symptoms.
If the broken bone is protruding on the exposed skin, you should try to return it and straighten it with your hands gently and a little stretch. If you cannot return it and straighten it with your hands, return it with this tool which is made of iron seven or eight fingers long. And expose it according to the extent of the wound.
Therefore, the physician should take three or four of them according to the type of treatment needed for each fracture, and let them be round with a slight thickness so that they do not bend when pressed upon during work.
Let its tip be sharp and have a curve at its end, with the top being thicker and gradually thinning from the middle to the bottom. This is its form:
It is called in Greek "perim," meaning a small awl. The sharp curved end should be placed on the protruding bone and driven in once so that when the bone returns and becomes somewhat even, the perim straightens the broken ends to each other.
If the broken end is thin and the instrument does not grasp it well, then the end of that bone should be cut so that the instrument can grip it. If it is not possible to reduce the bone as described at all, then cut it according to the shapes of the sections we have mentioned.
Or spread it with one of the saws as much as you can, then remove any remaining roughness or thin scales from the bone. If you reduce the bone and the patient still experiences severe pain after reduction, know that the bone has not returned to its natural position.
If you are able to return it to its natural position, then do so for you will greatly benefit the patient. Once you have finished setting the bone, dip a cloth in a black astringent liquid, especially if it is summer. Do not place any plaster or anything oily on the wound.
So that decay and putrefaction do not occur. Then use bandages when you are finished setting the bone, and leave the wound exposed by cutting a hole in the wrappings according to the size of the wound.
Beware of tightening the wound with a fracture, for ignorant physicians often do this and cause their patients either death or suppuration or gangrene. The constriction should be loose and relaxed, unlike the constriction of other fractures.
If the wound is bad, or a large one, and you fear some of the bad symptoms we described, and he finds a troublesome pain in the place, then you should not put bandages on it. Make rolls for him from strong linen in the place of the bandages and bind them tight.
If after a day or two you see that the wound has begun to produce pus, remove from it the cloth that you placed on it with drink, then use lint and ointments which we are accustomed to treating wounds with, such as the fourfold ointment and the like, and you should loosen the ligature.
And inspect the wound every evening and morning until it heals and is cured, and you should position the limb so that pus flows easily downward. If many days pass and the wound does not close nor does the pus stop flowing from it, then know that there are small bone fragments.
Therefore, you should search the wound with a probe. If any of those fragments are cleansed, remove them and take them out. And if any of them are not cleansed and they afflict the limb and cause pain, then cut them into pieces and remove them in any way you can.
If the wound shows a cold, or an eating away, or some other kind of decay and putrefaction, then you should meet each of those symptoms with the treatment that we described in its chapter.
And what you should stand by and keep in mind is that if a large bone breaks, and it's on a limb like the femur or humerus, and the like of these large limbs, then do not expose it to pulling or extraction. For very often death results from that, but leave it until it becomes putrid. Perhaps it will fall away by itself after twenty days or thirty.
Then treat the wound if you see a place for treatment, otherwise leave it.
Chapter Twenty
On the treatment of complications that arise after some fractures.
This complication often occurs after the fracture has healed, especially those near the joints, as a result of which the shape of the limb becomes unsightly and it may prevent the limb from performing its natural function.
If the complication is soft, use astringent drugs such as myrrh, frankincense, gall, euphorbia and acacia, or some of them, kneading them with an astringent syrup, egg white, or vinegar.
Apply this to the complication with pressure, tightening it firmly and leaving it for many days without loosening. Then loosen it and apply another one until the complication disappears, God willing, or tighten a firm lead plate over it.
Lead has a property that removes all growths in the limbs. If the complication has become hardened and severe, and necessity dictates its removal, cut it open from above, remove the excess that results, or pare it with some rasps until it is gone, and treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-One.
On the treatment of a fracture if it has knitted and the limb afterward remains weak, unlike its original state.
If a bone fracture has knitted and the limb afterward remains weak and feeble, this is due to many reasons. One of them is either due to excessive loosening and retying of the bandages, or excessive tightening of the bandages, preventing nourishment from reaching the limb, or excessive manipulation, or excessive movements at the wrong time, or a lack of blood in the patient's body and its weakness.
The treatment for this is to nourish the patient and enrich his body so that blood increases in him, use the bath, and introduce joy and happiness to him, and so on. Then apply pitch to the limb so that it attracts a lot of nourishment or continue to manipulate it with lukewarm water. So that nourishment may flow through it and it may return to its natural form.
The Twenty-Second Chapter.
In the Treatment of Broken Bones When They Are Bent and Prevented from Performing Their Proper Function.
If a limb, after its healing, shows a bending or a prominence of the broken bone, or a complication which has distorted the form of the limb. Unless the limb is prevented from performing its natural function, it should not be accepted that one who claims to break the limb from its head, for many ignorant physicians and bone-setters used to do this in our country.
This act is highly reprehensible and leads to great harm, which is difficult to remedy; but if the bending and complication are mild, it should be treated with water in which softening herbs have been cooked, such as marshmallow leaves and its root, rosemary, and so forth.
And it should be bandaged with softening bandages, such as well-made diachylon, or saliva from the marshmallow root can be taken and mixed with chicken fat and sheep fat and bandaged on, or a ripe fig is pounded with pigeon dung and other remedies called "contradictory to hardening" are used.
And the complication may be dissolved by continuous gentle massage with the hands, using movements of the limb in all directions at all times; if the bending has become old and severe and has hardened, and iron is necessary for its treatment.
It should be incised above and the connection of the bone released, and any excess complication or bone cut in fine sections; gentleness should be used with great effort and care. Then the wound should be treated as previously mentioned until it heals.
The Twenty-Third Chapter.
Dislocation is the separation of a joint from its socket, hindering movement and disfiguring the limb; it causes severe pain to the sufferer. Whenever dislocation occurs, it should be promptly reduced.
Do not delay it at all, for if he delays, the place will swell and become inflamed, making it difficult to return the jaw to its place. Therefore, it should not be delayed, nor moved or stretched while it is swollen, because this often causes stiffness and painful aches in the patient.
But if this happens, he should hasten to bleed the patient and then leave him until the swelling subsides a little, then bathe the limb with hot water and ointment, then gently return it and treat each member as mentioned in its place.
The chapters on fractures have also been arranged according to the order of their occurrence in the body, from top to bottom.
Chapter Twenty-Fourth.
On the Treatment of Lower Jaw Fractures.
The jaws rarely dislocate, and their dislocation occurs in one of two ways: either they are slightly displaced from their places and relax a little, or they are completely dislocated and relax fully. Towards the chest, so that the patient's saliva flows and he is unable to hold it, and cannot clench his jaw, and his tongue stumbles in speech. As for if the dislocation is slight, it often returns on its own with the greatest ease.
But if the dislocation is complete, it should be hastened and returned quickly without delay. The assistant should hold the patient's head, and the physician should insert the thumb of one hand into the base of the jaw inside the mouth if the jaw is on one side.
Or insert both thumbs if the jaw is on both sides, and with the other fingers from outside, he straightens it and orders the patient to relax his jaw and let it go in all directions, while the physician straightens the jaw and pushes it until it returns to its place.
If it is difficult to return them, especially if both jaws are involved, use a compress with hot water and oil until they are easily returned and do not delay their return at all, as we said. Then, when they have returned and aligned and the patient's mouth has closed and they have not relaxed, then place strips of cloth on them with splints made from wax and oil and red lead.
Then tie them gently with a loose bandage, and let the patient sleep on their back with their head raised between two pillows so that they are not moved right or left and do not chew anything but make their food soft mush.
Until the pain is gone and the jaw has set, let them eat what they like, and use it gently and do not force opening their mouth when eating, drinking, or yawning until the jaw has set and healed.
If it is difficult to return the jaws if they have become dislocated at some time and do not return to their place, this often results in fevers and constant headaches and perhaps the patient's abdomen will start and perhaps they will vomit repeatedly; if you see that, know that it is corrupted.
And often those who suffer from this die within ten days.
Chapter Twenty-Five.
On returning the clavicle and end of the shoulder blade.
As for the clavicle, it does not dislocate from the inside due to its connection to the chest, but it may dislocate outward and this will become apparent through feeling; force the patient to lie on their back and extend their arms.
Then press the area with your hand firmly, and it will return; then apply a compress and bandages and tighten them. As for the end that follows the shoulder blade and connects to it, it rarely dislocates. But if it does dislocate one day, it should be returned and straightened as we have mentioned and what seems appropriate to you.
Then you put a plaster, bandages, and a tight dressing on it, and order the patient to keep still and rest until he recovers. With this very treatment you can also return a dislocated shoulder blade to its place.
Chapter twenty-six.
In returning a dislocated shoulder blade.
Know that the shoulder blade is dislocated in three ways: one is towards the armpit downwards, the second is towards the chest, and rarely it dislocates above the shoulder blade.
It is not dislocated behind the shoulder nor in front of the nerve, and it is most frequently dislocated downwards towards the armpit, especially in those with little flesh because it comes out quickly and goes in quickly for them.
As for those with much flesh, it is the opposite, meaning that it comes out with difficulty and goes in with difficulty.  It may happen to some people that they receive a blow or fall, causing the shoulder blade to swell and become hot, so they think it has been dislocated. It should be examined to confirm its condition.
Then you proceed with its treatment, and recognize the dislocation if it is downwards towards the armpit. If you compare between the dislocated shoulder blade and the healthy one, you will find an obvious difference. You will find the head of the shoulder blade dented and under the armpit, upon touching, the head of the shoulder blade feels like an egg.
The patient will not be able to raise his hand to his ear or move it in all directions. Likewise, if it dislocated towards the chest or upwards, you will find that apparent upon touching and it will not be hidden.
This dislocation can be easily returned if it is recent or the patient is a boy. To return it, have an assistant raise his hand upwards, then place the thumbs of your hands under his armpit and lift the joint forcefully upwards to its place. The assistant raises his hand and extends it upwards.
Then he puts it down, and if it returns quickly, that is sufficient. If it does not return as mentioned and the fracture has been present for many days, the patient should bathe in hot water and use a poultice that loosens and softens, such as cooking the root of marshmallow, fenugreek, and rosemary in water and applying it.
Then the patient lies on his back, and a ball of wool is placed under his armpit, which should be moderate between softness and firmness. Then the physician places the patient's heel on the ball and holds the patient's hand with his hand and extends it downward.
He pushes the ball with his foot, and it returns to its place. If you wish to return it in this way, prepare a man taller than the patient and have him stand on the side, inserting his elbow under the patient's armpit and raising his armpit upward until the patient is suspended in the air. Another attendant pulls the patient's hand down toward his belly. If the patient is light, something else should be hung on him to weigh him down, for the fracture will return to its place in an hour. It can also be returned in another way.
Another method involves fixing a long wooden post in the ground, its head being round-shaped like the pestle of a mortar, neither too thick nor too thin. A soft cloth is placed on the top of the post, and the patient stands upright along the post.
Then he extends his hand downward from the other side and also stretches his body from that side with force, so that the joint returns to its place quickly. If it is difficult to return it by all that has been mentioned, use this treatment: take a wooden plank about two arm's lengths long. Its width should be about four fingers, and its thickness should be about two fingers. It should have a rounded head to facilitate entry into the depth of the armpit in this manner:
Then a soft bandage is tied around the rounded head so as not to hurt the wooden splint. Then it is placed under the armpit and the whole hand and arm are extended onto the splint down to the fingertips, and the splint is tied onto the upper arm and forearm and end of the hand.
Then the arm is placed on a horizontal beam, and the hand is extended downwards. The rest of the body is left hanging from the other side. Since the joint enters from its own time, if it can enter in any way, then a ball of moderate size made of wool should be placed under the armpit.
Then a bandage prepared from mill dust with frankincense and egg white is applied to the entire shoulder as it rotates upwards. Then the ball is tightened firmly from under the armpit, and it is wound over the bandage from above. His hand is then hung around his neck.
He should leave his hand motionless for seven days, and the patient's diet should be kept light so that the limb will grow stronger, which is quicker for healing. Then after seven or five days it is loosened and exercised with movement. If it remains stable and does not become loose, it has healed.
If the joint dislocates repeatedly due to moisture or some other cause, it should be cauterized with three white-hot cautery irons as previously described in the chapter on cautery. If all of this is done and the bandage is loosened after seven days and the joint does not remain stable...
…the bandage and constriction were repeated several times, but it did not remain stable; it fell and relaxed and was unable to be lifted upwards. Know then that the nerve in the head of the shoulder has been severed or stretched or relaxed.  Know then that the joint will never remain stable in its place.
As for the dislocation towards the chest, breastbone and back, it is reduced by pushing and stretching with the hands until it returns. All constriction and treatment are used until it heals. If after healing stiffness occurs in the limb and movement is slow...
Let the patient use the bath many times so that the flesh softens and returns to its former nature.
Chapter the Twenty-Seventh.
In the treatment of dislocating the elbow.
The elbow joint is dislocated with difficulty and also returns with difficulty as well. It dislocates in all directions, especially forward or backward. Its dislocation is not hidden from you because it is under sight and touch in any form of dislocation.
And if you compare the dislocated elbow to the sound one, it will become clear to you by the apparent depression of the joint, and the arm cannot be bent nor touched at its elbow. You should hasten and return the dislocation from the start before it becomes subject to a hot swelling.
For if it becomes subject to a hot swelling, it will be difficult to return it and it may not heal at all, especially if the dislocation is backward, for it is the worst of all types of dislocations and the most painful, and death often comes with it.
And if it can be returned, the assistant should extend his hand with both hands and arms outstretched, and the doctor's hand above the elbow and below it, while pushing the joint with both thumbs or the base of his palm until it returns to its place.
As for if the dislocation is forward, it may be returned by bending the hand at once so that the base of its palm strikes the elbow opposite to it. If the dislocation does not return, use a very strong stretch, which is to extend the arm by two assistants.
And the patient should also be held by two assistants so that he does not move during the stretch, then the arm is rotated in all directions after a folded cloth or a wide bandage is wrapped around his hand. If the doctor handles the joint with his hands, he should rub it with oil to facilitate the sliding of the joint easily.
Then the joint is pushed strongly until it returns, and after its return, it should be held with a dressing containing astringents and drying agents mixed with egg white, tightened firmly, and the arm suspended from the neck of the patient and left for several days.
Then the bandage is removed, and if the joint remains in its place, the ligature is removed from it and it is left alone. If you see that the joint has not become firm, reapply the dressing and ligature and leave it for several more days until it firms up, then remove it. If swelling occurs after the joint has returned to its place and the bandage has been removed.
If there is difficulty with movement, use moistening in the bath and gentle massage and manipulation until it softens. Or place a thick sheepskin over the joint and tie it on for a day and night, then remove it and put him in the bath; when he sweats, rub the joint moderately.
Then repeat the sheepskin application twice more, along with bathing, until it softens. If you wish to apply damp cow dung mixed with butter and tighten it on, do so several times, for it will soften and return to its former state.
Chapter Twenty-Eight.
On the Treatment of Wrist Dislocation.
The wrist is often dislocated, and its reduction is easier than that of other joints, but it should be quickly reduced at the time it dislocates before the area swells or a hot swelling develops.
To reduce the dislocation, place the patient's wrist on a board and have an assistant extend his hand. The physician then places his hand on the prominence of the joint and pushes it until it returns. However, he should observe whether the dislocation has moved into the palm of the hand.
The patient should place the back of his hand on the board when extending and reducing. If the dislocation is prominent on the back of the hand, let him place the palm of his hand on the board so that the physician's hand falls upon the same prominence of the joint.
Leave it alone and do not manipulate it, for it cannot bear it, and you will not be able to return it after a few days have passed. However, the joint will remain on its burden and will not harm the patient except if the hand rests and is unable to grasp anything.
Then you will know that the nerve has been severed or become putrid, and there is no remedy except to pull it with a ligature; perhaps it will be beneficial and perhaps not. If the wrist is successfully returned, apply the bandage we have described and then bind it tightly, leaving it for five days.
Then it is released and the hand is exercised. If its movement is difficult and there is some stiffness in it, soften it with warm water and massage several times until it becomes supple.
Chapter Twenty-Nine.
On the Treatment of Dislocated Fingers.
Fingers may dislocate in any direction. When a finger dislocates towards the back of the hand or its palm, extend the finger and push the dislocation with your thumb until it returns. Then tie the tip of the finger and hang it towards the side to which it dislocated.
Leave it for two days, then release it and extend it until it stands straight that day. If it is at night, tie it in the same way. Do not stop releasing it during the day and exercise it with movement, tying it at night. Continue this for several days until it becomes strong.
Do the same if it dislocates towards the palm of the hand, tying it towards the same side and repeating the first procedure until it heals. Also do the same for any other direction of dislocation.
Chapter Thirty.
On the Treatment of Dislocated Vertebrae.
When a vertebra in the back or neck becomes completely dislocated, or several vertebrae become displaced from their positions, there is no remedy because death hastens towards the patient.
The sign of this is that the patient's stool comes out involuntarily and he cannot hold it back, and often some of his limbs relax, either his legs or his arms, or one of them; and if one lump moves from its place, it often disappears altogether.
Its disappearance occurs in four directions: that which disappears backward is called a hump. As for its treatment, you should look and see; if the hump occurred in childhood, there is no treatment for it, nor will there be any recovery at all.  As for that which occurred from a fall or a blow, or something similar...
The ancients mentioned it with various treatments in lengthy explanations, most of which are not very beneficial. I have summarized this, and what little is needed suffices instead of much of what they presented, having drawn closer to the meaning and explained it, and depicted the instrument differently from how they described it.
I say that the hump that occurs in front of the chest is beyond remedy, and so is that which appears on either side. However, the one that occurs on the back can be treated, especially with what I will describe, which is to lay the patient face down.
On a flat workbench near a wall, spread a damp cloth underneath so as not to hurt his chest; then place a vertical plank fixed into a hole in the ground at one end of the bench towards his head and another plank towards his feet at the other end of the bench.
Have a servant hold the plank, which should not be fixed in the hole; and have another servant hold the other one in that position. Then wrap a soft, tight bandage around the patient's chest and under his armpits. Extend one end of the bandage to the plank at his head and tie it there.
Then tighten another bandage over his thighs, above his knees and at his ankles. Gather all the bandages together and tie them to the plank at his feet; then each servant pulls his plank with the bandage and the planks do not move from their fixed positions.
But they are not reliable as we have said, and the physician places his hand on the hernia with force until it returns or he places a board on it and then leans on the board with his legs until it returns. If it does not return with this treatment, he takes a board which is about three cubits long.
And you dig in the wall which we said to be near the patient a place where the end of the board enters, then you put the middle of the board on the prominence and the physician puts his feet on the other end and pulls very hard until he presses the hernia.
And it returns to its place, and if you wish to make it with the screw that is twisted by hand, which is to insert into the ground at the head of the patient in the last part of the bed two pieces of wood whose length is a cubit, and also at his feet in the last part of the bed.
Let the distance between each piece of wood be a span, and let there be made in every piece of wood a hole through which the screw is inserted and the wood is all secured in the ground well so that it does not move at all, and you insert a round stick which is the screw on which the bandage is twisted into the holes of the two pieces of wood.
And at its end there is a hole in which a stick whose length is a span is secured, and it is twisted in the two other pieces of wood as well. Then you tighten the bandage that you tightened on the patient's chest in the screw that is at his head and the bandages that you tightened on his legs in the screw that is at his feet.
Then a servant stands at each screw and twists his hand with the wrench by which the screw is twisted, and the physician smooths the prominence as we have presented. And this is a picture of the screw, the bed, and the patient:
Then after the hernia returns and the place is leveled, it should be covered with a bandage dried with egg white then with tow, and then a splint of a board whose width is three fingers or so is placed on top of the bandage, and its length is as much as takes the place of the prominence.
And on some correct pearls, then it is tied with the appropriate ligature and the patient uses fine food until he recovers. If some swelling remains in the place at the end of recovery, it is necessary to use softening and relaxing medicines with the use of the board that we described a long time ago.
A lead plate may be used for this. Sometimes a swelling appears at the end of the back pearls and it is thought to be dislocation, but it turns out to be excess bone that has grown. Do not subject this to this treatment, for death may occur.
Chapter Thirty-One.
On the Treatment of a Dislocated Hip.
Know that the hip joint and the shoulder joint are only subject to dislocation, and not to the slight displacement and flattening that affects other joints. The hip joint dislocates in four ways.
This is because it dislocates inward, outward, forward and backward. It most often dislocates inward and rarely dislocates forward or backward. The sign of inward dislocation is that if you compare the patient's healthy leg to the injured one, the injured leg will be shorter.
The knee will protrude more than the healthy one, and the patient will not be able to bend his leg at the hip. The area below the hip will be warm and swollen, as it is clear that the head of the femur has moved there.
The signs of outward dislocation are the opposite of these signs. The sign of anterior dislocation is that he extends his leg fully, but he cannot bend it without pain. There will be pain in the knee, and if he tries to walk, he will not be able to do so forward.
He will also have difficulty urinating and his groin will be swollen. When he walks, he will land on his heels. The sign of posterior dislocation is that he cannot extend his knee or bend it before bending his hip. His leg will also be shorter than the other.
The Arabic is relaxed, and the head of the thigh is clearly at the level of the waist. As for returning the types of this fracture, it is to look and see if the fracture is old, had been with its owner and no attempt was made to return it, and it remained in its condition, there is no treatment at all.
It should not be exposed to it, and as for the fracture that is recent, and was of any type from the four aspects of the fracture, hasten to twist the joint and extend it inward and outward and move it to the right and left, perhaps.
It will return and will not need any other treatment, if it does not return like this, you should prepare a strong servant to help extend his leg from below either with his hands or with a tourniquet tied to his leg above the knee, and another servant to extend it from above.
By putting his hands under his armpits, then tightening a soft tourniquet on the thigh root, and another servant holding the end of the tourniquet, the third servant will extend it either from the front from the Arabic side to the collarbone side or from behind to the back side.
And all of them extend at the same time until the patient rises with his body from the ground and remains suspended, this type of extension is common to all four types, if the fracture returns as we said, otherwise there must be a special treatment for each type as I mentioned.
As for its special return if the fracture is inward, the patient should lie on his right side then a tourniquet should be made at the thigh root between the head of the femur and the place under the Arabic.
Then extend the bandage upward from the Arabic side to the upper body to the collarbone side, then another strong servant takes hold with his arms and embraces the thick part of the affected thigh and extends it outwards with a strong extension, for it will return to its place.
This type is easier than all other types of treatment by which this member is returned, for if it is impossible for you and this type of treatment does not suit you at all, then it should be tied with a strong soft bandage on the heels and knees of the patient.
And the distance between each one of them should be the width of four fingers, and the affected leg should be extended more than the other by two fingers, then the patient is hung from a wooden beam in the house at a distance of two arm spans from the ground.
Then you order a strong boy to embrace the head of the femur and another boy hangs onto the patient, and the other boy pushing on the embraced thigh with force, for the joint returns to its place quickly, as for returning it if the jaw is out.
Then the patient should lie on the plank as we described in the case of the hump, and tighten the bandage on his affected leg especially and on his chest, then two planks are placed one at his feet and the other at his head, then an additional plank is placed in the middle of the plank firmly fixed.
A wet rag is wrapped around it so as not to hurt the patient, so that the plank is between his thighs so that he does not slip down during extension, then each servant extends to his side and the doctor straightens the hip with his hands, if it responds by returning.
If not, put the board on him and press it as we mentioned in the hump except that the patient should lie on his right side, and if the dislocation is forward, then the patient's leg should be extended once while he is in this exact description on the plank.
The doctor places the palm of his right hand on the affected thigh, then presses it with the other hand, however, making the pressure extended downwards towards the knee, and if the dislocation is backwards, the patient should not be extended downwards.
It should be raised above the ground, but it should also be placed upon something solid, as it is necessary that his hip be extended outwards, as we have previously described regarding his position on the surgical table while he is lying face down and the bandages are tightened.
As we have previously stated, the pressure plate should also be applied to the location where the joint has dislocated. Once the hip joint has properly returned, and the signs of its return are not hidden from you, which is that you extend the patient's leg.
If you see them level, and the patient is able to grasp and extend his leg without difficulty, know that the limb has returned properly. At this point, join the thighs together and apply the bandage tightly with a turban so that the hip does not move in any direction.
The patient must remain at rest for three or four days, then the bandage and bindings should be removed. Compare his leg with the other one. If they are equal in size, know that the joint has been stabilized, so allow the patient to walk. If you see any laxity in it,
reapply the bandage and tighten it according to your initial tightness, leave it for another three days, then remove it. Have him walk slowly on it for several days until it strengthens, God willing.
Chapter Thirty-Third.
On the Treatment of Knee Dislocation.
The knee dislocates in three ways: inward, outward, and downward - meaning backward - it never dislocates forward at all. The sign of dislocation is that you instruct the patient to bring his leg up to his thigh.
If it does not stick to the thigh, know that the knee is dislocated. To set all types of dislocations, have the patient sit down with his legs extended if he is able, and have an assistant sit behind him to hold his waist and twist him slightly backward. Then, you should sit on his thighs.
You press your back against his face and place his leg between your legs, then you fix his knee with your hands and interlock them between your fingers on his knee, then you press with your hands the sides of his knee firmly, while another attendant extends his leg until the knee returns to its place.
The sign of its return is that the leg will adhere to the thigh in a non-compulsive softness, then bandage it and stick the leg to the thigh then tie them all together with a bandage for three or four days, then release them. He should not use anything but a little walking for a few days until it strengthens.
If returning it by what we have described is difficult, otherwise use strong traction with the bandages whose description I have presented in the treatment of the hip until it returns.
Chapter thirty-three.
On the treatment of a fractured heel.
The heel may have a slight displacement or it may fracture completely. The fracture can be either inward or outward, and the sign of a fracture is that you will see the heel swollen and protruding towards the side to which it has fractured.
As for the treatment of its displacement, it is easy to return it; stretch it gently with the hands and straighten it until it returns. But if it has fractured completely, the patient should sit down and a strong attendant should hold him from behind in the middle of his back.
Then, hold his foot with your right hand from the top and with your left hand from the bottom of his foot at the ankle, then pull the foot towards you with your right hand and then twice with your left, then stretch it with your left and push the front of the foot with your right toward the leg without force.
Do this twice as we have described, then push the front of the foot towards the leg in the third attempt while pulling at the ankle. If it returns in one or two attempts in this manner and you see the foot straight, otherwise repeat the process because it will return.
If it is impossible to return her as we have described, then lay the patient on his back on the ground and fix a wedge firmly in the ground so that it lies between his thighs with a cloth wrapped around it so as not to hurt the patient; then have an attendant hold his thigh.
Then another attendant should extend the man's leg, either with his hands or with a bandage tied around the man's neck. Then let each attendant extend in the opposite direction of the other and keep the wedge between the patient's thighs to prevent his body from being pulled down during extension; then the surgeon straightens the jaw with his hands.
Another attendant should hold the sound leg down; then the jaw will return quickly. When the jaw has returned and you see that it is set correctly, apply a bandage and compress it tightly with straps. Bind the foot to the strap below. You must be careful of the nerve above the heel from behind.
Do not bind it too tightly so as not to hurt it, then leave it for two or three days. If the bandage loosens, tighten it, and then release it on the third or fourth day. The patient should refrain from walking for forty days.
If he wants to walk before this period, you cannot be sure that the jaw will not dislocate again and become worse, and it will then no longer be amenable to treatment. If a hot swelling appears, use the remedies we have previously described for other ailments and continue until it subsides.
Chapter Thirty-Four.
On Treating a Dislocated Finger Joint.
You should straighten what is dislocated with a slight extension, without force; this is not difficult but rather easy. If the dislocation is in some of the phalanges of the foot, the patient should place his foot on a flat surface.
Or on a plank while standing like one is walking, then you should place your foot on what is dislocated from those joints and then press it down with force until it returns and you see that it is aligned, and no bump appears in the location.
Then place a board under the sole of his foot, so that it takes the whole foot and has two heads; then bind it tightly for three days; then unbind it and keep him from walking for many days until it becomes strong and you are safe from its return if God wills.
Chapter Thirty-Five.
On the types of dislocation which occur with a wound or with a fracture or with both.
Whenever any of these things happen, and you treat it by setting and binding, it often results in death.
Therefore, it should not be attempted to treat such a thing except by one who is skillful in the craft, long practiced, gentle, compassionate, and deliberate, not hasty nor bold, and he should use, at first, only medicines that soothe hot inflammations, and surrender the patient.
To God's decree, except for what you hope for safety from harm with the ease of the disease, and it appears to you that there is some hope in it, then return it at once, before the hot inflammation occurs.
If the limb returns as you wished, use the treatment that soothes hot inflammations and treat the wound with drying ointments suitable for it; if the dislocation is with a fracture and there are loose fragments in the bone, then remove them. Follow what we mentioned about simple diseases, which has been previously stated in its place.
Strive with all your might and keep yourself from entering the path of risk, as I have previously advised you; for that is more lasting for your reputation and safer for your appearance, if God wills.
